The Red Page
The Book of Proverbs
In the immortal words of D. H. Stouter,
this Page “counted* one thousand one hun-
dred and ten, and when he woke up he’d to
count them again.” The general impression
is that the Australian does not run to pro-
verbs. Many of the contributors and also
this Page were doubtful about the existence
of any authentically Anzalian proverbs.
“Volumnia” puts the general impression
thus: —
Our picturesque expressions have not had the time
to mature into proverbs. Proverbs are the hoardings
of a past colloquial literature, the treasured and oft-
repeated epigrams of a people no longer able to
produce smart phrases for commonplace expressions.
A producing people mouths no memories. I say
“people” advisedly, for the proverb is the-' peasant’s
epigram. There are no ' authors of proverbs. No
matter where the phrase originates it only becomes
canonised a proverb in the mouths of the people.
If a famous author or best-selling novelist were to
produce an acceptable phrase it would be swamped
in the mass of magazine matter contemporary with
it. The written stuff drifts through the maundering
heads of the, library public producing a slow general
change with the times. It is the spoken word tossed
from mouth to mouth of a. single-minded people that
becomes moulded into the form that later is worth
using as a proverb.
The Australian people is single-minded and apt
with phrases. “From magpie to mopoke” might
have become a proverbial phrase for a working day
but for the Arbitration Court and the I.W.W. destroy-
ing its meaning. How apt we are at phrase-making
our troops abroad are teaching those they come into
contact with.... Put off your competition a century.
The time is not ripe.
And W.S.T. aptly remarks: —
When you come to think of it, Australians do not
run strongly to proverbs—they are too prolific of
speech, and have not time to sit back and dream out
concentrated philosophy. The Jobs of the back
country, boils and all (Barcoo rot) devote their
mental equipment more in the direction of commi-
nation services, and would rather invent new oaths
than proverbs.
But our Jobs do more than curse.
A proverb, as will be seen in the list of
those in general circulation, must be short
and terse. This cuts out probably a third
of the contributions. It must be capable of
universal application : it must embody in a
homely or picturesque phrase a sentiment
common to mankind. “Proverbs are the
economies of conversation” (W.T.C.). Many
competitors made the mistake of submitting
epigrams or aphorisms. Thus “Australia’s
birthright depends on her birth-rate” (Ed-
ward G. Parker), or “A nation can arrive
at greatness only on the wheels of its peram-
bulators” (J.D.C.), or “Love is as spon-
taneous as the chorus of the kookaburra—
and as whimsical” (Ruby C. Pavier) are
clever and epigrammatic, but they are not
proverbs. Parodies of overseas proverbs are
ruled out. “Too many crooks spoil the
course,” or “Where there’s a swill there's a
sway,” though apparently both are somewhat
general, cannot be classed as indigenous.
Many competitors failed to put their excel-
lent ideas pithily ; e.g., “A knowledge of the
track and the distance to the next water is
a poor substitute for a full waterbag” (“Bill
Badd”). An excellent sentiment like “Only
two of us live to the square mile. Bog in!”
(Simon Hickey) is not a proverb: “Bog
in!” may be. “If you leave your cheque in
the . bush pub to be cashed in the morning,
go and earn another for that Sydney trip”
(Steve Hart) is wisdom in too large a
mouthful.
It is impossible to declare that any pro-
verb is original. All proverbs were invented,
originally, by that modest but prolific bloke
“Anon.” No doubt the original of many of
these made-in-Australia proverbs will some
day be found stamped on a brick in the rums
of Babylon, or quoted in the folk-lore of
Thibet. But for the purposes of this com-
petition the entries have been tentatively
divided into five classes: (1) Aboriginal pro-
verbs, Australian and Maori; (21 that
large class of sayings that sum up the ex-
perience of the bloke or the kid Outback.
These while stating a simple fact can have,
and usually have, a wider application, but
seldom a universal application. These say-
ings are classified as Bush Lore; (o) Quo-
tations from the works of Australian poets.
(4) Proverbs already in general Australian
use. Some of these have been submitted by
more than one competitor; (5) Proverbs
manufactured by individuals at this 1 age s
lequest. ABORIGINAL provf.BßS.
The fish-hawk catches the fish, but the crow eats it.
When the ants move their eggs to high ground,
prepare for floods. .
White man fool. He make it big fire: no get near
him. Blackfellow make it, little fire, get up close —
plenty warm. ~ , ... • .
(These three by Y.)
The potato is cooked. (Te Whiti’s variant of
Caesar’s “Jaeta est alea.”) , . . ,
(Maratim Tapa.)
A parson-bird in the bush village, a red-crested
parrot abroad. (A sober-going, no-account fellow at
home, a devil of a highflier when he’s off the chain.)
A little spring of water satisfies the biggest
warrior’s thirst. (Inside meaning: a small-sized tart
can twist the heftiest 6ft. 6in. husband aroitnd her
little finger.) .
In war-time the cry is all for Paeko; when the
feast is laid Paeko’s name is not called. (Which
is a Maori equivalent of the old lines: —
When danger threatens and the foe is nigh,
“God and the soldier” is the nation’s cry ;
When war is, o’er and all our wrongs are righted,
God is forgotten and the soldier slighted.)
(These three by “Try Fluke.”)
BUSH LORE.
Only a fool camps in a watercourse, even if the
weather is good. (“Bill Badd.”)
Foresight conquers droughts. (E.W.M.)
When the Soldier-bird calls his loud alarm,
hook out for the hawks about the farm.
(William Borthistle.)
In time of drought no fire .is friendly. (“Arno.”)
When the ’possum sleeps soundest he’s widest
awake. (“Arno.”)
You never miss the feed till the drought’s right
here. (Alfred Seater.)
Have your smoke on the top of the hill.
(Steve Hart.)
pound-note cashed is a pound spent.
(Steve Hart.)
Hard to “break in” means hard to “knock up.”
(Kirkland Robertson.)
You can’t make a bull-pup do a setter’s work.
(Kirkland Robertson.)
Stick to the metalled rpads; by-tracks are often
boggy. i(“Six-'a-nd-Eight.”)
If you 'camp near water you must put up with
mosquitoes. (“Six-and-Kight.”)
Good sliprails are better than a broken gate.
A man is known by the horse he rides.
The drought breaks when all the stock has died.
(These three by S. Ellison.)
Jam is not the only thing that draws a mob of
flies. (V.L.S.)

Before grass-seeds appear.

Quotations from Our Poets.
The most widely quoted poet is C. J.
Dennis, five competitors forwarding 11 quo-
tations ; though one contributor submits 26
proverbs from the works of Wm. Biock-
sidge. In Dennis’s lot these were twice
backed: —•
’Atiny never paid no dividends.
The common end of most of us is — Tart.
Life’s wot yell make it.
Lawson and Paterson score two quota-
tions each :—•
No matter what a mate may do, a mate can do no
wrong. (Lawson.)
Names have little to do out-back. (Lawson.)
Remember, it’s seldom the pigeon can pick out the
eye of the crow. (Paterson.)
The very best tip of the lot is to watch the com-
mission go on. (Paterson.)
Ogilvie and Gilbert Murray have one quo-
tation apiece, Ogilvie’s being a misquotation
from his “Mulga Maxims,” the point being,
“Never take any man’s dust.” •
Wm. Bloeksidge’s list, submitted by “Me
Judice” contains much thought hut few
proverbs:—
Whatever we take from the skies we find again in
the heart of man.
Look upward, but not off the earth.
Man is possessed by his possessions.
Six foot of earth holds nothing.
Proverbs in General Australian Use.
Many of those submitted are obviously
suspect. “A shut mouth catches no flies”
(F. Saunders and “Mark Lane”), though
especially applicable to Australia, has a
long overseas lineage. “You are barking up
the wrong tree” (G. R. Humble) is probably
of U.S. origin. “It’s a long lane that has no
pub” (E. Cantlon) is an obvious parody.
But the following seem autochthonous: —
Ever'v man’s dog is the best—until judged.
(W. T. Clemans.)
You never know till the numbers go up.
(Maratini Tapa and F. Christie.)
What won’t fatten will fill up. (Used by the
hungry way-backer when the meat develops live
stock or food is otherwise spoiled. G. R. Humble.)
Never go to a goat’s house for wool. (Evidently
an adaptation of “silk-purse—sow’s ear.” “Im-
primatur.”)
Blast it! (“Eve.”)
Take a pull. (When exaggeration is about.)
(0. A. Partlin.)
Your cork’s bobbin’. (When something in con-
versation is to be avoided. C. A. Partlin.)
What’s the use of talking when you’re stony-broke
—and walking? (M. Roche.)
An ounce of kid-stakes is worth a ton of bounce.
(G.W.)
If your neighbor quotes Scripture, brand your
calves early. (X.Y.)
Never throw away dirty water until you have
clean. (Margaret Birmingham.)
These are Outback similes, not proverbs:
As miserable as a bandicoot. (A.G.)
As lousey as a bandicoot.
As cunning, as a dingo.
As noisy as a mob of cockatoos.
As active as a nest of ants.
As slow as a worm.
As fast as a flying doe [kangaroo].
As weird [or melancholy] as a curlew.
As startling as the scream of the giant owl.
As lazy as a cut dog.
(All these by H. W. Ford.)

classification is rough, since many of the
competitors neglected to make it clear
whether their entries were meant to be origi-
nal or not.

The prize is awarded to
“If your neighbor quotes Scripture, brand
your calves early.”
The first competitor to forward this was
“X.Y.,” whose name is A. McDonell, 143
Phillip-street, Sydney.
Arthur Wright, our native Nat Gould,
with a circulation of Australian sporting
stories alleged to be 100,000 copies, is also
alleged to be the best-seller of the N.S.W.
Bookstall Co. He has had ten Australian
novels published, and produces one a year.
These are week-end books, since he has to
earn his living (with the Fort Jackson
Ferry Co.), and support a large family dur-
ing the other days of the week. Frankly he
admits that he turns out the sort of book
that the market demands ; the thing he does
not admit is that he-turns them out. excel-
lently well.' The rewards of sporting litera-
ture in Australia do not, in his case, suffice
to enable him to give up his job and settle
down to literature. This, our best racing
writer, does not go to a racecourse more
than once in 12 months; and the only Nat
Gould's he has read were two novels that he
came across in his boyhood. His latest
novel, The Breed Hold's Good, into which
Wright has put some excellent work, is sell-
ing well. But while his market demands
the sport of l, Arthur
Wright is agreeable to go on supplying it
with racehorses and jockeys. It will be
interesting to note whether his audience
wants anything better —and The Breed
Holds Good is something better.

about Uncle Billjim. I wonder if it could be made
into a book with the rest of the Bully Australian
Nursery Rimes and sold for the little war orphans.
May Gibbs could draw the Gumnuts (I do love her)
and Lionel Lindsay Billy Bluegum. But I don’t know
who will do the Bunyip. But I think David Low
could, because h 6 used to make such funny things
about poor Mr. Hughes. D. 11. Souter could do
Uncle Billjim well. Normap Lindsay could do the
Kookaburra, but I like May Gibbs’s Jaeky in the Gum-
nut Babies better than Norman Lindsay’s in the
Australian Nursery Rimes. But I don’t like leaving
him out, beeausH he did such good cartoons for Con-
scription in The Bulletin. If you do make a book
don’t leave “Ten Little Rabbits” out.

War
After John Masefield's masterly history of
Gallipoli, his new book. The Old Front Line
(Heineinann, per Angus and Robertson,
Sydney), is a modified disappointment. It
is not Masefield’s fault: his colorful and
vibrant style dignifies this book as it did
that other ; but his subject is tamer. Mase-
field has set himself merely to describe, accu-
rately and painstakingly, the old British
front line as it was when the Battle of the
Somme commenced. Useful, as he claims;
but for such a subject a lesser pen would
have sufficed. ‘-All wars end ; even this war
will some day end. and the ruins will be
rebuilt and the field full of death will grow
food, and all this frontier of trouble will be
forgotten. When the trenches are filled in
and the plough has gone over them the
ground will not long keep the look of war.
One summer with its flowers will cover most
of the niin that man can make, and then
these places, from which the driving back of
the enemy began, will be hard indeed to
trace, even with maps....ln a few years’
time, when this war is a romance in memory,
the soldier looking for his battlefield will
find his marks gone.”
Masefield admits that it is difficult to-
describe this line without monotony: he is
tire one man for the task. And “if the
description of this old line be dull to read,
it should be remembered that it was dull to
hold.” But nothing that he writes could be
dull. And throughout it must be remem-
bered that all the advantages of position and
observation were in the enemy's hands, not
in ours. ‘‘They took up their lines when
they were strong and we were weak,” and
“almost in' every part of this old Front our
men had to go up hill to attack.” It is a
desolate picture —this abandoned old posi-
tion ; it is pitted with villages that must
have looked as villages did during the Black
Death. “The village is dead; the grass is
growing in the street: the bells are silent;
the beasts are gone from the byre and the
ghysts from the church. Stealing about
among the ruins and the gardens are the
cats of the village, who have eaten too much
man to fear him, but are now too wild to
come to him.”
The soldiers who held this old front line
saw nothing but grass and wire for many
months. “It was the limit of their world,
the horizon of their landscape, the boun-
dary.” Then came the advance into that
unknown country. “For a moment they saw
the parapet with the wire in front of it, and
began, as they ran, to pick out in their minds
a path through that wire. Then, too often,
to many of them, the grass that they were
crossing flew up in shards and sods and
gleams of fire from the enemy shells, and
those runners never reached the wire, but
saw, perhaps, a flash, and the earth rushing
nearer, and grasses against the sky, and
then saw nothing more at all, for ever and
for ever and for ever.” How bravely and
how gravely beautifully Masefield’s prose
rhythm runs!
It is to help those whose men were killed
in this battle to form an image of that long
graveyard that Masefield writes. It is a
narrative of desolation, “a mass of mud
bedevilled and bewildered. . . .strung and
filthied over with broken bodies and ruined
gear.” And when the shell craters, that have
become pools of filthy water overflow, “a
noise of water rises in that solitude which
'is like the mud and water of the beginning
of the world before any green thing
appeared.”
At some places the parapet of the front
line has been built up by thousands of sand-
bags ; and though Nature has begun to
change those heaps, “they will be there for
ever as a mark of our race. Such monu-
ments must be as lasting as Stonehenge.”
And our improvised line, seldom more than
a couple of sandbags deep, still bears the
impression of having been hurriedly con-
structed by amateurs with few tools. “Like
the old, hurriedly built Long Walls at
Athens, it sufficed, and like the old camps
of Caesar it served, till our men could take
the much finer lines of the enemy.”
This noteworthy book closes with a won-
derful description of the preliminary bom-
bardment before the battle. Readers will
look eagerly for the “account of our people’s
share in the great battle,” with which, Mase-
field hints, this book will be followed.

“Arthur R.”: Australian Nursery Rimes show a
distinct ■ improvement on the imported stuff; and
already “Tinkle, Tinkle, Telephone,” and “Three-
ha’pence for Tuppence” have, to a large extent, re-
placed “The Oat and the Fiddle” with young Aus-
tralia. A significant fact -is that since Aus-
tralian Nursery Rimes has been published a larce
number of old rime books have made their reappear-
ance in the bookshops. Perhaps some of the old
school are iealous of the new arrivals, and are doing
their best for a revival of the ancient stuff.

M these days, when everyone is feeling the pinch of
war-time conditions, and extreme scarcity of many
lines which in the gone by could be purchased
abundance, our immense resources and the big stocks
at the New Palace Emporium go a long way to lessen
the burden of war-time costs.
in
When we quote prices we mean business, but in this
the fourth year of a World War prices cannot stand for
any length of time. After present stocks are exhausted
and new supplies come to hand —bought in many cases
at an increase on to-day’s retail prices in Sydney there
must be still higffer prices. And while these increases
are likely to be considerable, it is probable that innumer-
able lines cannot be duplicated.
The wisdom of buving now will be apparent to all
purchasing future requirements to-dav from stocks that do
not carry the full burden ot war-time costs is an investment
that will prove a profitable one. Our reputation for fail
trading stands behind this statement,
Customers ordering by post are treated in the same
way as those buying at the counter.
The same prices are charged, selec-
tions are made from the same stocks,
and everything sent to the country is
carefully packed by experienced men.

RHEUMATISM
Its Cause—and Cure.
Rheumatism is caused by _ a curious,
poisonous acid in the blood. This acid, just
like tiny pieces of broken glass with rough
edges, settles in the joints and muscles and
causes tlmt awful agonising pain of back-
ache, rheumatism and gout, gravel, and the
burning sensation of inflammation of the
bladder, etc.
De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder Pills
strengthen the kidneys and drive the
poisonous pain-causing acid right out of the
system.
To cure rheumatism or any other form of
kidney trouble you must positively get fid
of the cause —the poisonous uric acid.
Remember to do this a real remedy must
pass through the kidneys and the bladder
and not through the bowels, as most kidney
pills do. When you se.e the urine change to
a muddy bluish color—a feature which dis-
tinguishes De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder
Pills from all others—you know beyond all
doubt that they have put their healing
touch on the right spot—the kidneys and
the bladder. A wonderful pill—straight to
the kidneys—that is why De Witt’s pills
give such amazingly quick relief —every
time. A permanent cure follows in almost
every case. Look for that blue-colored urine.
Don’t waste a single hour. Go to your
chemist at once and ask him for a 2s. 9d.
box of De Witt’s Kidney and Bladder Pills,
which are made expressly for kidney and
bladder troubles.
They will cure every one of the above
symptoms, no matter how long or how badly
you have suffered.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Redland Bay.
Roads of flaring Indian-red lead to Redland
Bay;
Green of bush flanks all the tracks and
scarps down to the sea;
Stradbroke Island purple in the blue and
golden day,
And again comes love aland to storm and
glamor me.
Red roads out from Cleveland on the south
and sea-edged lines,
And Redland’s red road ending in the cool
gloom of the pines.
Green of pine and papaya and banana groves; .
Green of custard apple, and the green of
bunya pine;
Red roads, like to dusted blood, whereon the
swift car moves,
Transilient as the meeting of the blood of me
and thine ;
The island’s jewels in the bay and jewels are
thine eyes,
That change to dewy stars when night and
her thick stars arise.
Red roads coiled in the plantations, through
their green and fecund grace,
As passion coils and ribbons the cold pru-
dence of a man ;
And the red road and the green bush bring
an absent piquant face,
The misty eyes of love, in trouble since the
world began;
And the red road and green forest, these shall
always find a way
To face and form whenever I shall think of
Redland Bay.
The flowing of our river, and the eastern
hills beyond,
The siekled silver reaches curving through
the close-cropped lawn,
Are a memory dear and tender, close and
intimate and fond,
As if we saw the river gilded still by fifes
of dawn;
But the mental eye can visualise, as if ’twere
yesterday, :
Love in the green raths, by the blood-red
roads of Redland Bay.
QUEENSLAND.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Nostalgia.
I want the sun-drenched»sea,
The curve of tropic beach,
The palm-fronds flung against the sky,
The lilt of island speech.
The long white coral roads,
The rain-trees overhead,
The clink of anklets in the dusk,
The myriad barefoot tread ;
The sudden tropic rain,
The evis’ breath at night,
The gleaming swords of sugar-cane,
The sense of space and light.
The trams go clanging by,
The motors hoot and screech; '
I want the tropic sky,
The curving island beach.
N.S.W.
KEI VITI.
Youth.
Youth is too elate to walk,
Speeding out to clasp
Joy that hangs on slender stalk
Ripening for its grasp ;
Foot and horse are ail too slow,
Youth is tempest kinned ;
Like the vapors it must go,
Riding on the wind.
Wind of love or wind of hope,
Wind of poesy
Bridled with a spider’s rope,
Spurning land and sea ;
Age may foot it with a frown
Trudging travel-thinned,
Youth laughs by like thistledown
Riding on the wind.
VICTORIA.
SYD C.
The Pear-Tree.
Out in the meadow
A little faery maid
Just beyond my window
Dances unafraid.
Crown of snowy blossom,
Petticoat of green,
Isn’t that a ball-dress
Fit for a queen?
Now with a twinkle,
And now with a twirl,
Dances in the sunshine
r Jfhe faery girl,
This way and that way,
A gav rigadoon.
Every leaf a laughter,
Every bud a tune.
Oh. the merry maiden!
That way and this,
Now she drops a curtsey,
Now she blows a kiss—
Blows a little kiss to me,
With a laughing word !
Nobody saw but I,
Nobody heard.
That must be a secret,
Just for us two—■
Tim kiss and the message
She gave me for you !
N.S'.W.
F. S. BURNELL.

THE SCALP BONUS.
Recruiting-officers have scornfully refused to take the proffered bonus of 10s. a head for recruits.
SERGEANT BILL ANZAC: “Take it away! What sort of a soul have you got to think that we want a tip for helping our mates!”

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The
Bulletin sent regularly to you every week for a
year, including Special Christmas Number, to any
address in Austiialasia, post paid.
Post free to any address aiiuoad, £1 10s. per
annum.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to the
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby expressly
reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column all
contributions submitted, whether in the form of Politi-
cal, Social or other Articles, Verse > Short Tales or
Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects, and
not exceeding two columns in length, or, say, 3000
words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters
or Newspaper Clippings. All communications will be
regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn—will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MARCH 14, 1918.
HOW ABOUT DELIVERING THE
GOODS?
With solemn contempt for humor, Prime
Minister Hughes persists in talking about
his scheme for the “organisation of industry”
on the basis of red tape and Government
billets. The reason for this gaseous per-
formance is, of course, clear. So long as he
blows off hot air about nebulosities he can
let the practical work incidental to his job
slide. The trick of starting a violent argu-
ment about something else instead of sawing
up the wood dates from the birth of the first
loquacious shirker born into a weary world.
If it were possible to take Hughes and his
jaw 7 seriously, there would be justification
for pointing out to him several circumstances
which make all this talk about “organised
industry” sound like a bad joke. It might,
for instance, be mentioned to him that,
though he has had practically a free hand
and an open purse to organise shipbuilding,
the only visible results are slabs of printed
language. It might be shown to him in
simple terms that, after vociferously assert-
ing his intention to organise a scheme of
repatriation, the first elements of the thing
are in a state of chaos so hopeless as to be
a jest from Flanders to Canberra. And he
might be told that the organisation accom-
plished in the Defence Department under his
auspices raised a suspicion in the public
mind that as an organiser he was an ad-
vanced Bolshevik. But to make remarks of
this or any other character in the vicinity of
Hughes is about as useful as remonstrating
with Pearce for being a messer. Both have
the delusion that they can undertake any-
thing and finish it off with triumphant
success are so puffed up with vanity that
the plainest evidence of failure and inepti-
tude only spurs them on to achieve greater
distinction as muddlers and distribute fur-
ther calamity. Having botched the ship-
building business so long that it is just a
question if it now matters 2d. whether any-
thing is done or not, made a ghastly mess
at the Defence office, organised a war policy
into shreds and patches, made some atro-
cious arrangements in relation to metals and
wheat, and set up a hideous record of what
can be accomplished in the way of w 7 aste and
evasion, it is only natural that Hughes
should yearn to try his bungling hand on
fresh material rather than explain why lie
has failed with the old.
The Bulletin is not concerned about
arguing with this little word spinner; but
it suspects that the time is nearly ripe for
the people who are concerned
about the future of Australian industry and
the financial stability of their country, as
distinct from stalling and manoeuvring for
votes, to realise that between the produc-
tion of words and the production of wealth
there is a vast difference; further, that un-
less the issues piling up on the national
doorstep are approached with softie thing
more effective than a mouthful of the dic-
tionary our experiences are going to be very
distressing. The future of the Common-
wealth, so far as the determination of it
rests with the inhabitants, turns upon the
readiness and ability of Australia to produce
things required by the people in this and
other countries. It is no use jabbering
about “our great natural resources” and “the
home market” and all the rest of it if the
capacity to utilise those resources and satisfy
that market is missing. Nor is it much good
expecting that politicians who make hash of
public undertakings and waste the public
credit can be relied upon to do anything but
the same thing over again when they widen
their field of activities. If Australian in-
dustry in the past had been run on the lines
pursued by Hughes in building ships and
Pearce in running an army, the country
would, long ago have been bankrupt and the
people have become paupers. The clear busi-
ness now of the politician is to learn how to
discharge the job he is paid for with reason-
able efficiency and give the people oppor-
tunity to make good on the actual work of
wealth-production. And the duty of the
people is to impress upon the politician that
unless he does this he will be fired out. The
spectacle of Parliaments notoriously incom-
petent to discharge their legitimate func-
tions, and of Ministries convicted over and
over again of the grossest ineptitude, maun-
dering about organising industry and simul-
taneously refusing to give the people the
chance to do some organising for themselves,
is enough to cause wonderment to a gohanna.
* * * *
If the Federal Ministry, instead of giving
sanction to the eternal talk of the Hughes
rdgime, would explain why it does not
organise a working policy for tropical Aus-
tralia, and why it omits to mend the leaks
in the cashbox, and why it cannot discover
what is meant by repatriation or shipbuilding
and numerous other things, Australia would
have something to be thankful for. And if,
after having made these explanations, the
Ministry proceeded to take off its' coat and
tidy up, and while the job was proceeding
announced that it had no time to waste talk-
ing about other things, there would be some.-
thing to say for it. But a:- Government
which does none of these things—which allows
the whole national outfit to : drift off down
stream, hustles the tariff away into the bush,
and through the mouth of a windy time-
server burbles a mass of verbal camouflage to
cover up its administrative and political
nakedness —this is no ordinary sort of
menace. The tragedy of the thing is that
an at least considerable section of the people
know this to be so, and yet are content to
see nearly everything that counts in the
national life either side-tracked or made the
sport of an agile tongue.
AT PISTOL RANGE.
The American troops are armed with automatic
pistols, which they have been using with deadly
effect in recent raids.
Four years ago it would have' seemed almost
impossible to any person who had been
watching the mighty war preparations of
the nations that troops should ever again
come to such close quarters as to make the
use of a pistol possible. The tremendous
range of the guns, the increasing perfection
of the rifle, the development of the far-seeing
aeroplane, and a hundred other thihgsj''4l^ :
seemed to point to the fact that Efiropeanj|
battles would be long-distance affairs, with,
death leaping out of a cloudless sky and
men facing mysterious powers that were
unseen and almost unheard. The first war-
wind on the Western Front blew the whole
notion away. The great guns roared muzzle
to muzzle, the trenches were dug within
speaking distance, and the rifle became often
of less account than a well-aimed' jam-tin
full of explosives. Knee to knee and hand
to hand the millions of the nations have
wrestled amid an uproar such as.the world
never before heard. The idea that troops
could not approach guns or come on in the
open against rifle-fire was a mere' dream of
the parade-ground, where there is more
stupidity than almost anywhere else in the
world. The new weapons were as deadly
as any had supposed, but the / chances of
attackers getting through even the most con-
centrated fire had been under-estimated. The
decision of the field has rested with the
actual fighters to a far greater extent than
could have been believed; and now 7 it is
quite possible that before the year has run
its course the automatic pistol may prove to*-
be the most valuable weapon in the whole
armory of death. For that automatic
pistol" is really a miniature machine-gun
for use at closest range; and once it has
been proved that the enemy may really come
through the fire to a point where the thing
will hit him, it looks a lot more valuable
than the whirling jam-tin or the anarchistic
bombs w 7 hich had hitherto held pride of place
in trench raids. “As men shorten their
weapons they increase their dominions”
passed as an axiom in days, when war was
a glorified football match; and it may be
that a pistol can blast the w 7 ay to peace
more surely than a fifteen-inch howitzer.
* * * *.
But the closeness of the modern war-
i range has an application of a sterner sort
than any mere consideration of the inches
of the weapons could suggest. In the old
days kings and Prime Ministers fought long-
drawn-out campaigns at a range which meant
absolute personal safety for the real war-
makers. The armies fought, the nations
looked on and hooted or applauded; but
nobody ever seriously 7 thought that a war
could get up against a throne or thrust
explosives into a Cabinet Council. In very
old days, of course, kings and their friends
did get right into the thick of the turmoil,
partly for sheer love of the excitement and
partly from the antiquated notion that it
was really a king’s job to be in the most
dangerous spot in defence of the rights or
ambitions of the mob over which he ruled.
But such ideas had long gone by 7 the board.
The person who threw down the glove in
the wars of the last century or two took very
little risk. He might be about when a battle
was being fought; but he was always on
the other side of the hill. The same thing
applied in a general way to the ruling upper
class. A few of the younger members of the
top families got into the mess because it was
a fine adventure and a great game; but
nobody forced the war upon them and they
could take the glory and blood or leave it
just as they pleased. Now all is changed.
The conflict is at pistol range ; not only has
The' war~iTorfor “swept right through the
homes and hearts of London, Paris and
Berlin, but it lias struck and struck hard at
places as distant from the scenes of action
as Vancouver and Balmain. Nations are
really fighting whisker to whisker as they
never fought at any previous time. A Ger-
man submarine torpedoes violently the
breakfast-table of a retired linen-draper at
Hampstead, and on the rebound hits a picnic
party in a glen near Katoomba. The rifle-
fire that crackles along the hill-slopes beyond
Ypres makes holes in hearts as widely sun-
dered as Cornwall and Collingwood. Modern
war, as the huge game is being played to-
day, has wiped out distance and brought us
all into absolute touch with the ruin. But
in a still more deadly sense the war gets
right up against those who make and wage
it from what was once a skin-safe distance.
The splendid vision of the Kaiser dangling
from a rope urges on the rank and file of the
Allied peoples to fresh sacrifices ; no doubt
the people who hunger in Berlin have as
cheerful a fancy about the ultimate finish of
the British Prime Minister or President
Wilson. The great capitals are actually
at grips, and the men who hold the battle-
pens are pushed, willingly or unwillingly,
right into the firing-line.
All this is at once strangely disquieting
and strangely suggestive. A German raider
was near Sydney the other day with an
areoplane which might easily enough have
been employed to drop bombs on the missing
mailbags at the G.P.O. A German trader
is at this very moment preparing silent
bombs with a view to a grand raid on the
wealth of Australia. We are in the world
firing-line as really as if explosives were
tearing up-the pavements of Collins-street;
and- the automatic pistol is never many feet
awhy from our easily-moving jaws. The
only pleasant suggestion of the close and
tangled fight is that once the fighting is over-
men will never allow their real enemies to
get to close quarters again ; the disquieting
side of the whole thing lies in the fact that
the fight is so near and so bitter that an
alleged peace will not end it. but that the
close-range rage of strife .will go on after
the doves have roosted. The enemy knows
us all as he never knew us before, and may
be expected to take the fullest advantage of
that knowledge. The straight and direct
remedy would seem to be the automatic
pistol. That weapon is a distinctly citizen
affair. It is made not only for close fighting
but for personal fighting. The voting power
of an elector of the Commonwealth is, in
point of fact, his automatic pistol—the thing
that stands for his own personal defence and
for the best fight he can put up when the
enemy comes right at him. The big guns of
the seven Parliaments of Australia may
roar, the machine-guns of office may splut-
ter, there may be a more or less effective
political rifle-fire; but when it comes to the
last and the trenches are really endangered,
the automatic pistol which operates through
the ballot-box is the most important weapon.
Australia is in very great danger. Our big
guns are almost silenced, the machine-gun
fire is badly directed and the rifles are
mostly aimed at points that matter nothing.
114 is the moment when, if the country is. to
-be saved, the citizen pistols in a million
hands must begin to speak. We have trusted
the larger machinery and the more power-
ful-looking weapons, and as a result the foe
is sw r arming all over No Man’s Land. Democ-
racy as a fighting weapon was subverted
when the war flames, started, and a sort of
despotism has been established for the
alleged purpose of winning the war. It is
not winning the war —not winning any of
the wars, military, economic or moral —and
despotism having failed, it is necessary for
sheer national existence to give freedom and
democracy a show again. We are at pistol-
range from verv fearful things, and we must
go under unless eacli individual citizen stays
the enemy rush by his own efforts. The
other fellow, in whom we trusted, has ceased
thinking and fighting for us; we must think
and fight hard for ourselves.
FINLAND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The little Finnish boat, nearly a week out
of Hull, slugged along at five-knot speed
through a channel marked by stakes, some-
times edging so close to the spruce-clad
islands that the tall trees seemed to tower
over its decks. Even with the sun shining
brightly the air had a tang of cold in it,
as though it had blown off fields of ice.
Blue water r- sparkled, and among the
sombre green of tile firs and spruces showed
the red-roofed chalets of the rich people from
Helsingfors who make this archipelago
their summer playground.
We were bound for Abo. on the west coast
of Finland, and had to thread through the
80 miles of stakes that make the passage
like a narrow canal. The crowded decks of
the little tub were piled with the belongings
of returning emigrants, mostly young men
whose felt hats, wide trousers and knobby-
toed shoes spoke of the Chicago department-
store. They were over-well provided
with mouth-organs and concertinas, and the
air vibrated with discordant noises and
sounds of jubilation in a strange, harsh
tongue, oddly flavored with Yankee slang.
They were going home to Finland, but to
one traveller, at least, that did not seem
anything to make a song about.
That was before a world-war had
taught geography to suburban grocers.
Finland had been associated in my
own mind with snow and reindeers,
and it was surprising when the little
tub edged into the wharf of this unknown
port to see an electric-car waiting, and all
the other appurtenances of civilised life,
such as advertisements of liver-pills and
soap. Indeed, the only primitive things in
evidence were the heavy, wooden houses and
the manners of the Russian officials, who
ransacked our belongings for firearms and
seditious literature and bullied us about our
passports. But even this seemed more novel
then than it would to-day. The powers of
government broaden slowly down from pre-
cedent to precedent.
Finland, instead of being an isolated
region of eternal snow, is a very up-to-date
country indeed. The towns have that aspect
of bright modernity that seems to go with
electric-light. All over the southern part
of the country there are endless chains of
lakes and cataracts, and the quiet of the
spruce-clad hills is broken by .the whirring
of countless dynamos. Cheap electric-power
has scattered industry all over the country-
side and brought the cost of production
down to a minimum. There is probably no
country in Europe where the ordinary com-
forts of modern life are so widely distri-
buted and so cheap. The equivalent of a
couple of shillings will procure you a
spacious room in one of the big hotels in
Helsingfors, with hot baths handy, electric
light and a telephone at your bedside. True
cheapness is not the only good, and it may
be merely relative, after all: but there is
no denying that cheap power is a good basis
for industry.
Whatever the figures may say, Finland
gives the stranger an impression of unosten-
tatious comfort and prosperity. Travelling
about in trains one is conscious of a certain
mental alertness on the part of the people,«
especially those of Swedish blood. The book-
stalls flare with cheap translations of the
literature of all countries, modern England
being chiefly represented by Conan Doyle,
Jerome and W. W. Jacobs. Bright, aggres-
sive women in felt hats, eyeglasses and
tailored clothes pursue you with questions
about your own remote country, correcting
your facts when you romance and keeping
you firmly tethered to solid realities. They
were the only Europeans who five years ago
betrayed more than a fugitive interest in this
end of the earth, perhaps because they also
are conscious of being new. For they are new
in all essentials, at any rate, these smart,
efficient women who fill the banks, the post
offices and the civil service, and venture
gaily into the muddy waters of politics.
Sitting on the busy boulevard of Helsing-
fors, watching the stream of afternoon traffic
pass by, you forget about the pictures of
reindeers in the schoolbooks. There cer-
tainly is a winter here, but it is hardly as
severe as that of Canada, and is alleviated
generally by a hard, bright sun. There was
no reason to doubt the Finnish journalist
who shared my cabin on the boat and told
me he was going home because he could not
stand the London climate. On the boule-
vard here in summer there is as brave a
show as anywhere in the world. People
promenade to see and be seen: there are
comfortable seats under the trees, and talk
and music floats over from the cafes on the
sidewalks. Yet underneath there is always
the assurance that the crowd is not merely
a leisured and fashionable one —that it knows
about work and business as well as the art
of playing peacock in the sun.
It is the tragedy of Finland that it has
hitherto been split into three parts. It
takes three languages, Finnish, Swedish and
Russian, to keep people from spitting on the
pavements or leaning against carriage-doors.
The old Finnish stock is of Mongolian
origin and still carries traces of the brown
buttonhole eye and the clear, oily skin. From
this race come the sailors, who are alleged
on every sea to have the power of casting
spells over heavy-fisted bo’suns or bringing
up storms at the wrong moment. What the
superstition derives from it would be hard
to say. Perhaps from the fact that Finns
are not a gregarious folk, but are distri-
buted singly, like black cats, over the ships
of the world. On land, at any rate, they
are a likeable people, hospitable and kindly.
The bourgeoisie are mainly Swedish-
speaking, and under the old regime the
official language was Russian. Now
Swedish and Finnish languages have to fight
for mastery. Thus there is more- than an
element of racial war in the battle between
Red Guards and White. The White Guards,
whose defeat in arms is always sympatheti-
cally described in the cables as a “mas-
sacre,” consist mainly of Swedish-speaking
students and young men of the upper classes
who have had training in the German army-
It is absurd to suppose that they have any
friendship for us. What unites them
is their autocratic and military con-
tempt for the working-class Finns. But the
difference in language does not altogether
correspond to the difference in blood.
Swedish is the language a Finn dons when
some aptitude for business or increase m
wealth carries him into middle-class snon-
bery. , *
For some time in Helsingfors 1 stajea
with a little saddler from Marquette,
U.S.A., who was a member of the older
race. We had been thrown together W
accident for a few days and he had msis e
on taking me home with him.. Though
and his wife had lived for six years in
America, and the three children were bo
there, he - alone could speak English,
short, heavily-built man, with brown ey s
and a quiet manner, he talked at gre
length in the evenings about his mtner
and his father’s father, pursuing an honest
family of woodcutters nearly back to
days of Gustavus Vasa. But when n.


talked of the future there was always the
same queer note of irony : .
•• \h yes, the Swedish bourgeois will help
us get’ rid of the Russians. But who then
will protect our rights against the Swedish
bourgeois?” . , , _ ,
No one, of course —unless it be the Red
Guards. The Finnish Republic is born into
a world that is not so liberal and humane
as it used to believe in the old, sentimental
days. The struggle for self-preservation has
left the air harsh and raw, and there is no
sympathy for the fledgling until it can show
the color of its feathers. The new republics
will have to come into being fully equipped
for the fight or perish in the nest.
VANCE PALMER.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
John Redmond.
Gone to Heaven, John Edward Redmond,
leader of the Irish Parliamentary party, and
-sane, forceful voice of Ireland for nearly a
generation. The fierce agitation during the
leadership of Parnell forced upon Glad-
stone and his English Liberals the neces-
sity for dealing with the Home Rule ques-
tion ; but it was the long constitutional
struggle, maintained with quiet tenacity by
Redmond, that caused the measure to be at
last placed on the Statute Book. Red-
mond’s fine contempt for the noisy vapor-
ings of that cheap person, Edward Carson,
and his reliance upon the British sense of
fair play, made him the ideal leader for a
cause that was no longer a forlorn hope.
Then the war came: all things were thrown
into the melting-pot again; and Carson, by
much noise, exalted himself in the eyes of
the Nortiicliffe press and secured, under
cover of the German guns, what he had pre-
viously yelled his intent of getting by
treason. Redmond agreed to the postpone-
ment of Home Rule until after the war. His
loyalty to the cause of freedom led him into
what turned out to be a fatal mistake; for
behind him rose the Sinn Fein movement —
a reckless outburst on the part of a nation
which was convinced that. it had once more
been tricked, and that England never could
be trusted to play the game. Some of Ire-
land’s contempt for the bargain-breakers over
the water made Redmond its target; and,
dying, he told Father Bernard Vaughan
that his heart was broken. It might well have
been. In all the long succession of Irish
leaders this man had most nearly achieved
success. His life-work resembles that of
Henry Grattan, to whom in his personal
loyalty to constitutional methods he was
also spiritually akin. Both men saw Irish
freedom an apparently accomplished fact;
and both saw the prize snatched away by
forces over which they had no control. What
Redmond’s place may be in history it is
hard to even guess; but if the love and
esteem of his countrymen and the hearty
respect of his bitterest foes is any guide, he
will • be remembered everlastingly as a great
and good man.

Fat Animals and War Bonds.
One of the arguments used against the pro-
posal to prevent local prices for meat con-
tinuing above export prices is that, if the
grower cannot get more for a jumbuck sold
for consumption in Australia than for a
similar animal destined for mastication in
London, he will “find it more profitable to
keep his stock for the wool.” In some cases
where the breeder is under-stocked this may
be so. But so far as the argument applies
to the majority of properties from which
sheep are turned off for the knife, it is un-
true. The difference between the price
for stores and fats, calculated on the
price realised for export, is much too sub-
stantial to induce the proprietor to retain the
meat on the off-chance of losing it while
waiting for a growth of wool. If the argu-
ment had any serious force, how comes it
that, in spite of the allegedly ruinous prices
given for export, sheep have been pushed
forward for the freezer in. increasing num-
bers? The indisputable fact in relation to
the sale of animals in the bulk is that the
market is stripped by the export buyers at
a relatively low figure, or forced up by bids
fiom export interests whenever the local
seller of flesh comes in. The business has
been brought to a pretty fine point, and,
though there are various terms by which it
may be described, “fair competition” is not
one of them. That it “pays” many people
to see mutton sold at famine rates cannot be
doubted. But it will certainly not pay a
Government to let the general population be
thus fleeced. The state of mind in which some
of the defenders of a system which makes
the export of meat a means by which the
local inhabitant can be squeezed was quaintly
illustrated the other day when an infuriated
dealer proclaimed that “interference” would
mean disaster to the War Loan and calamity
to the finances of Australia. How it helps
national finance or the War Loan for a
citizen to sell a carcase of meat cheaper to
someone who will send it out of the country
than to an individual prepared to consume
it on the spot is a problem that could only
be answered in Heaven. As a general thing
this paper views the official price-fixer and
all his works with sour mistrust; but the
absence of this individual from the neighbor-
hood of the leg of mutton is no reason why
Private Enterprise should be allowed to block
the small-saver from buying a War Bond.
This consideration may possibly serve to
keep Hughes's feet warm, though he seemed
to be looking round for a funk hole the other
day.
Reprisals.
Before the war has run its course civilisa-
tion will be painfully interested in the philo-
sophy, morality and practical value of what
are called Reprisals. The word means many
things. From a medical point of view the
next-morning headache is Nature’s mildest
reprisal. A parson suggests that pangs of
conscience, are reprisals on the evildoer, and
that Hell is the last reprisal of all. Nemesis
is the representative reprisal-monger, as a
reasoning world understands the word. But
in Germany, which isn’t reasonable, the
word was impudently Teutonised at the
very outset of the war. When the crews
of only a few British merchantmen had been
murdered in cold blood by Hun cruisers, the
British Admiralty announced that any cap-
tured pirate crews would not be treated with
the consideration shown to ordinary naval
prisoners who had not been concerned in
acts contrary to accepted rules of warfare.
Then was the moral flabbiness of the
Asquitii Government revealed. Germany
threatened “reprisals” on certain aristo-
cratic English prisoners who had been cap-
tured at the Battle of Mons: if a distinction
was to be made in British treatment of
naval prisoners, said the Chancellor of the
Kaiser, special “punishment” would be in-
flicted on a selected dozen lords held captive
in Germany. Whereupon aristocratic and
pro-German influences paralysed the will of
the British Government and over-ruled the
nation’s sense of fair play. A proper and
crushing reply to the German bluff would
have been a threat to do unto German non-
combatants in England what the Huns were
doing to British non-combatants on the seas;
and the word “reprisals” would, .have been
dropped by the Kaiser like hot coal. At
that time, and for months afterwards, the
balance of power as regards prisoners was
in favor of the Huns, hut with a long war
ahead of them they would hardly have dared
to raise a cry of “No quarter.” Now that
the balance has turned decisively against
them they talk no more of reprisals on our
men in prison camps. They reserve the word
for the intimidation of other combatants in
a weaker position. As for instance: —
The Paris newspaper llomme Libre states that 1000
hostages were selected from prominent French people,
including refined girls, taken from their homes and
sent to Holzminden camp as a reprisal for air raids on
German towns. They were exposed to scandalous
treatment. When 30 of the girls refused to make
sacks for the enemy they were locked up without
food and light for over a month, and the mattresses
and bedclothes, and even their woollen underclothing,
were taken away from them. They were only
released when a Spanish inspector arrived on a visit
to the camp. The Germans attempted to hide the
outrage, but the inspector learnt the facts.
Here is an exasperating illustration of the
German pretensions to enjoy a monopoly
of aerial offensives. With childlike faith in
the efficacy of his Zeppelins the Kaiser
started the war on the assumption that if he
was not promptly victorious in the field he
could rain down sufficient destruction on
enemy towns, far behind the lines, to set the
populace whining for peace on German
terms. Hun airmen were dropping bombs
on open towns almost as soon as the first
design on Paris had failed. The German intro-
duced Frightfulness from the air as a new
and unimpeachable system of warfare. But
with the advent of the airmen who could
drop bombs on German towns, as the Zep-
pelins had dropped them on enemy, towns,
came the Ilun-hog grunt of “Reprisals!”
Having possession of nearly a third of
France the Huns can put their bully threats
into action, and will continue to do so (call-
ing their victims “hostages”) until them-
selves are bombed into submission. English-
speaking countries continue to acquiesce,
practically, in this Hun system of putting
the screw on their civilian captives, though
it might be promptly checked by a counter-
move. Germany openly professes to regard
this as a' war of nations in which the
Hymn of Hate is its war song. But we —-
well, Hamlet might have said of us: —
We, dull and muddy mettled people, peak,
Like John-a-dreams, unpregnant of our cause,
And can say nothing.
We are waiting, it seems, for the Americans
to come with a fleet of aeroplanes and a
backing of hard commonsense to teach the
Huns what “reprisals” really mean. The
logical answer to Zeppelins is to be uttered
by the one combatant country that never
came within Count Zeppelin’s dream of
Frightfulness in the days, not so long ago,
when the Kaiser publicly extolled the blood-
thirsty bladder-maker as the greatest Ger-
man of the Age.

Brown Brother and the Annexation
Mania.
Possibly on account of reluctance to throw
clods at the doctrine of self-determination,
questions of a practical nature regarding the
German colonies are for the present being
carefully shooed off the stage of public dis-
cussion. The explanation is, of course, that
these questions raise others that are infern-
ally difficult. But in Maoriland the local
statesman is not going to stand any non-
sense from the German or anyone else, and
he has about as much time for self-determi-
nation as for cheap butter. For his part,
Massey strongly contends that Maoriland
is entitled to “have” Samoa because “our
boys took possession of it” ; while Joseph
Ward has his Imperial eye on Fiji, and
declares that he will fight to the very
utmost “the proposal to hand Fiji over to
Australia, because the natural country for
those islands to be attached to is this.” So
far as Australia is concerned, the all-con-
quering Maorilander can have Fiji. Aus-
tralia lias quite a big enough job on hand
without being cumbered with a group of
islands 1200 miles away. Meantime, how-
ever. it appears to this paper that the black
man’s cry of “Fiji for the Fijians,” if inter-
preted in terms of the self-determination
war-aim, is entitled to considerably more
respect than the yearning of Ward and
Massey to do a bit of diplomatic filibuster-
ing. And “Samoa for the Samoans” has at
least as respectable a sound as “Belgium for
the Belgians.” To look upon the decorative
citizen of Hapaii and Levuka as the German
looks upon the neighboring Slav implies sen-
timents that are specifically disowned in
every rational statement of Allied war
aims, and gives a lead in the direction of
plunder in the Pacific which the most vital
interests of Australia demand should have
no countenance. If there is any justification
at all for the British occupation of Fiji it is
that this occupancy is merely a preliminary
to self-government by the local people. To
say they are unfit for the task is rubbish —
and hypocritical rubbish, which is worse.
The fact is that Fiji has been “held” for
the exploitation of the Fijians’ land and
India’s labor —the latter so gross an out-
rage upon the peoples of India that it is
now stopped. Unless Maorilanders have a
hankering to perpetuate this double exploita-
tion in some form or other and pine for
getting tangled up in more war, what do
they want to “have” Fiji and Samoa for,
anyhow ?
Ghosts and “Other Causes.”
The 'Senate committee appointed in Mel-
bourne to consider the alcohol traffic in its
relation to the army accidentally roused up
a ghost which wasn’t on the bill of fare.
The Drink Curse is one of the recognised
assets of parsondom, and a certain pleasant
atmosphere of wowserism breathed round the
proceedings until a military witness drifted
away to the subject of syphilis and venereal
diseases in general. This is a matter on
which wowserism preserves considerable
silence, save in vague allusions. The
young are not informed about it because it
would be rude to do so, nor warned how to
avoid it lest the bloom should thereby be
rubbed off their ignorance—otherwise
described as innocence. Therefore it didn’t
seem strictly in accordance with the intent
of the bill of lading when a testifier stated
that, so far as could be estimated from the
examination of recruits, one-fifth of the male
population of Melbourne suffered from vene-
real complaints. Now it wasn’t alleged that
Melbourne is worse than other cities, and
seeing that the worst cases of disease weren’t
likely to volunteer and thus come under the
notice of the military doctors, it is probable
that the one-fifth was an under-estimate. It
was added that syphilis was vastly more
important than beer, and that so far as the
Victorian camp figures went the great
majority of the diseased (60 or TO per cent.)
were teetotallers or very moderate drinkers.
When a comparison is made between dipso-
mania and syphilis the latter wins the race
easily. No teetotaller need fall down in
the street with a bad hiccup because his fore-
bears w r ere consumers of wet goods, but the
most moral teetotaller may be a monstrosity
—deaf or dumb or blind, more or less im-
becile, stunted and otherwise reoulsive —
because of the razzles of the man before him
in company with Raiiabs who died ere he
was born. And even if his ancestors had a
clean bill of healtn and he himself has
abjured Rahab or managed to avoid the
consequences which her name suggests,
venerea] disease can be acquired at second
hand even by children, in infected baths or
lavatories, by the use of infected towels, and
in a multitude of other ways, and the conse-
quences may last in greater or less degree
for life. The man who occasionally wears
whisky-leaves in his hair may be quite wel-
come in society; but nobody welcomes the
syphilitic subject save through ignorance.
Any private hosnital will take in the hard
drinker if he can pay his way and has no
worse cnarge against him; and the most
meddlesome wowser will take him by the
hand and call him his poor fallen brother.
The sufferer from synhilis has to herd with
his kind in a place of his own lest he should
contaminate the drunk; and the average
wowser, if he has a concern for his health,
regards him from a safe distance. Further-
more, the drinker is a diminishing evil,
while the victim of contagious disease is
suspected of being, until recently, on the
increase. Perhaps he is still on the increase.
Taking all points into account there was
justification for raising at the Senate’s drink
inquiry the srhost which wasn’t exactly in
the catalogue. Tnere are two spectres that
especially haunt the white races, and the
fact that it is generally considered imnelite
to even allude to the worse of the pair has
caused a world of misery. Even some Gov-
ernment Statisticians describe it among the
deaths as “other causes.” These men will
probably put the present war among “other
causes.”
The Government Appoints Another
Commission.
It almost looks as if the procrastinating
creature at the top of Federal affairs has
got into such a state of mind that he
couldn’t chase a strange dog out of his
garden without first appointing a royal com-
mission to inquire as to
(1) Whose dog it is.
(2) What breed it is.
(3) How it got into the garden.
(4) How long it intended to remain there.
(5) Its object in coming.
(6) What plans, if any, it had made for providing
a means of retirement.
(7) Its reason for being a dog.
(8) Any other matters relating or pertaining to
the dog that may arise.
In pursuit of his usual habit, Hughes has
appointed Chief Justice Griffith, a busy
man with his own work to attend to, as a
Commissioner to “examine the records of
the Defence Department”— Pearce’s De-
partment, which has already been examined
and denounced in three reports, two of
which, at time of writing, remain unpub-
lished. Also —and this is where the supreme
jest comes in— -Griffith is to state “the
number of reinforcements that should be
embarked monthly.” Of course, if Griffith
is to decide this point according to the in-
formation derived from an overhaul of the
tangled Defence records, it will be some time
before lie can say to a split hair how many
men should be sent monthly, even if he sus-
pends his proper work entirely. It is as if
he was sent in great haste to Adelaide via
Papua. By the time he reports the great
battle in Flanders may be over and done
with. Further, the report, whether it comes
to-morrow or next year will never be worth
much, for no one can tell how many gaps
there will be to fill after next battle or any
other battle. Also the Lord only knows how
many gaps there are in the Defence Depart-
ment’s information. But meanwhile the Pro-
crastinator can postpone the work of finding
recruits on the plea that he is awaiting the
results of the Commissioner. In his utter
lack of the organising faculty the Stipendiary
Delayer finds it as hard to get a recruit as
to get a ship. In the part of the Man Who
Doesn’t Know How he is without a rival.
If, however, Hughes must set another man
(whose job it isn’t) to find out what he
(whose job it is) ought to know, then The
Bulletin suggests this obvious proceeding:
('ll Appoint Griffith to ascertain how many
recruits are wanted.
(2) Meanwhile make a rough estimate of the
number required.
(3) Send that number.
(4) If Griffith decides that more should have
been 'sent, then make up the deficiency.
(5) If Griffith says that fewer would have done,
remark that it is a darned good thing we left a
margin. , . ,
(0) Shake hands with Griffith and wind up the
Commission, charging the cost of it to loans.
When the Gorillas Came to Paradise.
The Iluns have done a wholesale bombard-
ment of Venice from the air, and presum-
ably they will come again and again, unless
the Allies dispose of their air fleet, till the
queen city of the Adriatic is a pile, ot ruins.
Venice appealed to the Ilun instinct. It
was unfortified and ungarrisoned, because
it lias been understood for many years that
an undefended city was exempt from bom-
bardment and fire and sack and other forms
of military destruction. The Italians had
a passionate desire to save their
paradise from the horrors of war. To tear
down its ancient glories in order to make
barricades was sacrilege, and to undermine
12 centuries of marvellous history in order
to dig trenches seemed an act worthy or
perdition. So Venice took its chance and
relied on the usages of civilised nations, and
the Hun came to it like any other orang-
outang coming to any other masterpiece ot
art, and put his great coarse paw through
it. He gained nothing except the pleasure
of breakage and the satisfaction of being a
Hun, as he always has been. Venice knew
him of old. It was Hun territory for some-
thing like 60 years, and no place has more
cause to remember the blessedness of
“kultur.” In Venice within the time of
people still living, ladies were arrested and
imprisoned for systematically staying away
from the theatre. It was well known that
they stayed away because Austrian officers
swarmed at the theatre, and the dames of
Venice objected to seeing them, objected to
surrendering their seats to them on demand,
and objected to rising as a mark of respect
when ' some great Austrian potentate
entered. Men were similarly treated; also
they were flogged or shot for not saluting
private Hun soldiers in the street, and if
a Venetian gentleman and his daughter met
an Austrian officer, and the Hun had a
fancy to kiss the daughter, the father
objected at. his peril. In those days the
Austrian prisons were full of Italian
“political offenders,” and though the sur-
vivors wbre supposed to be released when
the Hun was chased out of Venice in
1866-67 it is suspected that numbers
remained unaccounted for. All of which
was strictly in order —Hun order. The
same sort of thing, including the flogging
of the girls and women of Sydney and Mel-
bourne and Australia in general, will hap-
pen here if the war goes utterly wrong from
any cause —perhaps through famine among
the Allies, largely contributed to by a Gov-
ernment Which Won’t Build Ships to carry
our six or seven or eight million tons of
surplus food to the place where it is so
badly wanted. It is a peculiarity of the
Hun that wherever he has conquered another
race he has been loathed at the beginning
and loathed to the end. He has made no
friend in the wide world. As late as the
middle of last century one of the petty half-
Austrian princes of northern Italy, finding
it advisable to flee from his loving subjects,
had all his political prisoners, who in-
cluded the best of his people, first flogged
and then shot, after which he bolted with a
carpet-bag and a light heart. There was
only one public Hun whom Italy ever
regretted— Maximilian, brother of the late
emperor Francis Joseph, known as the
“Son of the Gallows.” Maximilian, as
viceroy of Austrian Italy, showed himself
an insufficient flogger and a very poor hang-
man. He was recalled by the infuriated
Son of the Gallows, and lost all his employ-
ments. Then, by devious devices, this weak
vain man with much pomp and whiskers
was induced to be a candidate for the billet
of Emperor of Mexico. He was a puppet
set up by bis brother and Napoleon 111.
and supported by foreign troops. He could
neither rule well nor fight well. When the
Republicans of Mexico captured him after
a bitter war they tried him on a charge
of “pretending to be an emperor,” and not
being Huns they only shot him politely
against a wall. And the verdict of Venice
was that he was a good sort of man—for a
Hun —and a very bad poet.
A Department of Commerce.
Tiiep,e were voluble persons and institutions
before W. M. Hughes, which is a blessing,
for it saves the Commonwealth the disgrace
of being the inventor of unlimited conversa-
tion. Boanerges (in the Scripture)
might be quoted; also Chadband and the
Shepherd, and the wild waves which dis-
coursed to Little Dombey (Dickens) ;
Talkative (Bunyan), Bartoline Saddle-
tree (Scott), the Cockatoo (Aboriginalities
column), the brook (Tennyson), Jawbone
Neild (N.S.W. Hansard), and Skakke the
Long Winded (Norse legend). Still,
Hughes takes a high place. For instance,
this is part of a recent column of verbiage
about the new department he proposes to
create: —
Its function is not to produce ( anything except
expenses and reports), but to consider (Diogenes in
his tub was a great considerer) and advise ( advice is
cheap and generally bad ) upon all the circumstances
of the industry (whether it be belltoppers or cocoanuts
or boots or agricultural implements), including pro-
duction, distribution, sale and finance ( which pre-
sumably means a loan and a deficit). The Associa-
tion will ascertain exactly the position of the in-
dustry in relation to home and foreign markets (it
must hurry, for the position isn’t nailed down and
is always changing), promote its welfare, encourage
its development (a blessed word like Mesopotamia),
assist the individual to produce at the lowest mini-
mum of cost ( not the highest minimum , please
observe), and dispose of his product ( such as wheat
and rabbits) so as to give the best return. The (1)
Association will act (with the same velocity as
Hughes's shipbuilding job) through (2) its council,
which will be directly in touch with (3) the General
Council of Science and Industry, composed of repre-
sentatives from all industries, and (4) the Depart-
ment of Commerce and Industry under the presidency
of (5) the Minister for Commerce and Industry (it
suggests five deadbeats sitting close together for
warmth).

How Yankee Labor Sees It.
From the address of Frank J. Hayes,
President of the United Mine Workers of
the U.S.A.. at the biennial conference at
Indianapolis:—
Shall we be found wanting in this present emer-
gency? There can be but one answer by free men, and
let us in this convention firmly resolve that every atom
of energy we can contribute to the winning of the
war will be given gladly, freely, and in the same
loyal spirit as those who go out to give their lives,
if need be, for the peace, freedom and security of
the civilised world. Let there be no mistake about
this war and its relation to the great humanitarian
movement in which we are engaged. It must be
evident to every close observer that the triumph of
the Central Powers means the menace of militarism
throughout the ages—means a world in turmoil and
agony for centuries to come. What chance would
labor have against the edicts and pronunciamentos
of military autocracy? A philosophy based upon the
right of a few to rule and conquer can find no place
in the minds of liberty-loving men. It is a matter of
particular pride that approximately 20,000 members
of our organisation have enlisted in the military
forces of our nation. Our hearts go with them across
the sea, and we know that these heroes of ours,
who have faced the perils of the mines year in and year
out, will not be found wanting when they face the
foreign foe. While we are earnestly desirous of
peace, wo recognise that it is unattainable as long
as the German emperor and his cohorts seek to
dominate and force upon the world the creed that
might makes right.
And let there be no doubt about the charac-
ter of this Miners’ Federation. Of these men
the radical Public of N’York —a paper so
revolutionary that it usually talks of “the
conservative Bulletin” —lately said :
These are the men who bought rifles in the open
market, issued a call to arms, and then forced
ItocKEKELiiKR’s gunmen to sue for truce in the days
following Ludlow. They are the men who have
increased coal production by 50,000,000 tons, and
who would have produced the other 50,000,000 tons
of excess required this year if cars had been forth-
coming. They are men who can fight industrial
tyranny in America and Prussian militarism in the
world at the same time, men who can and will
nullify a medieval Supreme Court decision and whip
tftill other industrial autocrats here at home while
lending valiant aid in the struggle against a Euro-
pean manifestation of the same dark forces. They
are the men who compose our greatest labor organ-
isation, our one great union organised on industrial
lines.
Dark Shadows.
Far more important than the news received
lately about the resumption of fighting on
the Western Front was the report of a
speech made in the House of Commons at
the end of last week by Bonar Law. It
seems to this paper that he was preparing
the public to hear about the evacua-
tion of Salonica, and probably also some-
thing different about the campaign over
beyond Jerusalem and Bagdad. Whether
the Allies can continue to feed the army at
and around Salonica, and whether they
could reinforce it to carry on against an
enemy offensive of any magnitude, have for
a long time been problems to which the
answers of officialdom have not been very
cheerful. What appears to be reasonably cer-
tain now is (1) that the collapse of Russia
and Roumania in the North have made the
Salonica enterprise futile; (2) that if the
army at Salonica is withdrawn the position
in Palestine and Mesopotamia will have to
be reconsidered. Bonar Law appears
anxious to have this noticed, and, though
he tries to dilute his language by mention-
ing that it had never been hoped to make
the Mesopotamian campaign “decisive”
unless transport was uninterrupted, the bed-
rock of the thing is that transport is inter-
rupted. Though we are no doubt entitled
to feel comfortable about the great German
attack alleged to be pending on the barbed
wire in France and Flanders, it would be
foolish to overlook the patent fact that else-
where Germany has every reason to be satis-
fied. And this, too, it would be foolish to
overlook: from the first Berlin has turned
its eyes towards the Black Sea, Asia Minor
and the Indian Ocean. The Western Front
is much, but it is not all. Tremendous in-
terests elsewhere are at stake. Among other
things, Australia is at stake. Germany’s
face is turned this way. If the Allies are
defeated on the Western Front anything
may happen anywhere else.
- *
From the outset the Defence Department
has made poor use of the professional men
of Australia, medical men perhaps excluded.
There are in this country some of the best
engineers and chemists in the world; but
though they have been in most cases anxious
to put their highly-trained services at the
disposal of the nation, they have had the
greatest difficulty in getting any recognition
of their special qualifications. Recently an
Order was issued that no more army commis-
sions would be granted in Australia. Gener-
ally speaking, the decision is a good one ; it was
little less than a scandal that men with no
special qualifications, but with social influ-
ence, should he permitted to hang about for
a year or so preparing themselves for a job
which could be much more safely entrusted
to men who had climbed up from the ranks
in active service. But it is both absurd and
wasteful that this Order should apply t»
men who have taken their professional know-
ledge and experience as engineers into the
Engineers’ Training School. To pack them
off as N.C.O.’s or sappers is no more ridi-
culous than it would be to first invite surgeon
Alexander MacCormick into the Army
Medical Corps and then send him away as a
stretcher-bearer.
♦
Wonderful how deeply the Freetrader has
to think before lending a hand to Australian
manufactures. Minister Millen is going to
inquire into the woollen industry—appar-
ently after 23 years of political life it is
still a closed book to him —as an occupation
for returned soldiers. But he holds that it
would be “criminal to train returned
soldiers to an industry unless there was a
reasonable assurance that there would be a
steady demand in the established industry
for their labor.” Well, the raw material is
here. No country is better supplied. , The
demand is here, for Australians wear clothes.
If Millen can’t see how to bring the
demand and the supply together when they
are living next door to each other and pass
each other in the street every day, his salary
is wasted. Certainly there is the loony War
Profits Act, which levies a special war tax
of 75 per cent, of his profits on any man
who starts a new woollen factory ito use
Australian material and employ Australian
labor, and this, with other taxes, about
absorbs all the earnings, perhaps part of the
capital as well. Then the duty on imported
woollens doesn’t nearly counterbalance the
higher wages and shorter hours in Australia
as compared with some of its competitors.
Apart from these things, what is there for
Millen to inquire into? Why shouldn’t he
give his cubic yard of brain a rest?
From the Gallery :
Hughes asks for nothing better than Irvine’s con-
tinued advocacy of Conscription. Whilst the Iceberg
pursues the topic he is hardening the feeling for
Hughes among the Nationalist waverers who, in any
other circumstances, would be looking kindly in his
direction. Irvine’s attitude is achieving no benefit
for the community and is arousing the sort of irrita-
tion which finds relief in personal recrimination
and suspicions of honorable motives. You will now
get insinuations about Irvine’s designs on the Prime
Minister’s salary. You will also hear a vast revival
of the scoff that a man possessing a safe seat, who
has contributed comparatively nothing in blood and
treasure to the Great Cause he advocates, and still
further enjoys life without any visible shrinking of
personal pleasures, is not fitted to lecture the com-
munity on patriotic conduct. The gibe is no less
stinging because it is equally applicable to other
members of Iceberg’s party. Pearce, Groom, Web-
ster, Watt and Glynn—to mention five of the
Cabinet alone—resemble Irvine in having no son at
the Front, and, also like him, are not suffering
the shortage of fox-age and income that afflicts many
thousands of worthier Australians. . Contrast the
theatrical discoui-ses of those whole-hog Conscription-
ists with the quit* conviction of conscientious but
mistaken voluntarists like Tudor, Chai'ltou, Peter
Bowling and other Laborites who have sent all
their eligibles to the conflict, and you get all the
disparity between Bathos and Pathos, totem-worship
and the Christian religion. Yet Iceberg and those
others still consider themselves qualified to rule the
country that has ignominiously rejected the principle
they stand for. Indeed, far from resigning to
show an honest man’s indignation with a hopeless
position, Irvine has become associated with a rebel-
lious and mentally negligible Nationalist corner that
can only shift Hughes with the help of Tudor’s squad.
If Irvine has any sense of humor, he surely cannot
help smiling. The situation is soaked in irony.
“F.K.E.” : There is a rather bad historical
blunder in the finding of the Vic. Leg. Coun-
cil in the Sachse case. The members state
that the city of Halle, as far as they could
find out, belonged to the kingdom, of Saxony
before 1815. It never had any connection
whatever with that Saxony. From 965 to
1648 Halle was under the powerful arch-
bishops of Magdeburg, who held vast terri-
tories as secular lords. In 1648, at the
Peace of Westphalia, the archbishopric (and
with it Halle) was merged with Prussia
(then termed Brandenburg). With the ex-
ception of the period 1807-14, when Halle
was included in the short-lived kingdom of
Westphalia, the city has been under Hohen-
zollern control since Luther’s time, as even
five of the last six archbishops were princes
of that family, and they resided at Halle,
which has always been an ultra-Prussian
place. The confusion re Saxony is due to
the fact that Halle is situated in the Prus-
sian Province of Saxony.
