“V.” tries his hand at prophecy:—
A few more days and the numbers will go up for
the Bananaland elections. The Labor party will be
returned, probably with an even more unwieldy
majority than at present. Of the seats at present
held by Labor, Townsville, Charters Towers, Windsor,
Enoggera, Bulimba, Ipswich, Bremer and Merthyr
are likely to prove troublesome. As against those
possible losses is placed the winning look of Logan,
Carnarvon, Burke, Mirani, Nundah, Rosewood, Cun-
ningham and Lockyer. Flinders will, of course, go
to Labor, ex-Senator John Mullan being the holder
of the endorsement for the pub-strewn electorate. At
the jump off there were going to be about 20 three-
cornered contests, but the Nationalists cracked the
whip and the hounds came to heel. On nomination-
day there were nine electorates with more than a
party pair, but final acceptances saw the number
down to five, and before the start it’s likely that two
more will have bowed to the Party joss. It is an
uninteresting campaign. Labor is trading on its
record, which is positively brilliant alongside the
inaction of Hughes in the Federal arena. Liberal-
ism, or “Nationalism” as it has camouflaged itself, is
casting suspicions on Ryan and Fihelly, and in an
ashamed manner is making sporadic attempts to blow
the wind of life into the skeleton of Win-the-War.
But Liberalism or Nationalism is so hopelessly in-
capable and tremendously unintelligent, if the elec-
tors can judge by the candidates, that Labor will win.
It may even be a debacle.
The Commission appointed to inquire into
the business side of the Defence Department
last week had a taste of what the press of Aus-
tralia has had to put up with for three years
and more. The members having submitted their
report, the document was passed on by the
Minister to certain officers, presumably those
who were responsible for whatever further
muddles have been disclosed; and those
officers having rfeplied, telling the Commis-
sion that it dididt know its job, the report
was returned to the Commission. Chairman
Mcßeath’s answer came promptly: after pro-
testing against the grossly improper course
adopted by the Government, he said that the
Commission declined to do any sub-editing.
In the case of a newspaper, of course, though
it may be as absolutely sure of its facts as
this Commission is, it is simply told : “This
article is untrue and its publication is pro-
hibited.” There are times when the Depart-
ment is foolish enough to add some observa-
tions of its own; and if the observations
made to the Commission are as unconvincing
as those usually made to the press, it isn’t
surprising that the Commission merely
snorted. Even that consolation isn’t allowed
the newspapers, the Department’s reply
being always “confidential and not for pub-
lication.”
“Bows” adds his protest:—
Apparently in reply to a request by the Merchant
Service Guild of Australia that Australian masters
and officers be employed in Commonwealth-owned
vessels, Sydney D.T. states that Prime Minister
Hughes says “it has not yet been determined whether
or not the vessels now building in America shall be
manned by Australian seamen.” As the present
Commonwealth-owned fleet of 15 vessels can boast of
only one Australian master and five officers, out of
a total of 50 or thereabouts, the chances of Aus-
tralians finding berths in the fleet now under con-
struction seem remote. On every side we hear of
the shortage of certificated men to man purely
British vessels; yet we have a Government here
drawing from the undermanned British vessels and
fleet to the detriment of good Australians, many of
whom, having wives, families and Australian homes,
cannot go abroad to look for employment.
When the numbers went up in connection
with a by-election at Wellington (M.L.) the
other day Premier Massey ascended to a
high place with a view to making a few
well-chosen remarks about the joy of the
Government at getting its man in on a
minority vote. But the assembled prole-
tariat had no time for Bill, and raised loud
yells of wrath and counted him out and
otherwise misconducted itself. Whereupon
Massey did what Prime Ministers usually
restrain themselves from doing. Putting his
thumb to his nose he publicly waggled his
fingers at the multitude and gracefully took
himself off. The interpretation put upon
this weird performance is that William in-
tended the masses to understand that he was
determined to carry on the war to the bitter
end and would make vulgar gestures in
Berlin at the finish.

‘‘Mrs. Dot,” Somerset Maugham’s genial
comedy presented at Sydney Criterion last
week, may be described as First Aid to
Widows. When a charming relict wants a
particular man, she has usually learnt enough
of the gentle art to use the right bait —and
plenty of it. In Mrs. Dot’s case the odds
are all in her favor: the victim hasn’t a
chance. She is fabulously rich, being a
beer-widow, young, vivacious, pretty. Also
her victim is in love with her, though, unfor-
tunately, engaged to another girl. Add a
nice man who falls in love with the
girl; and a managing mother’s pertinacity,
even when aided by the hero’s absurd sense
of honor, is doomed to defeat. Believing that
the young man, poor but of course allied to
the aristocracy, is in love with her, Mrs. Dot
Worthley proposes to him. Then comes the
disclosure that he has become engaged to
marry a flapper. He is on the point of escap-
ing this bond, owing to his poverty, when
he suddenly inherits a title and vast estates.
Naturally the girl’s mother won’t let him go.
The smart comedy’s climax is given away in
the first act: everybody knows that when
Mrs. Dot wants a husband she is going to
get him—and there will be no doubt about it
if she has the help of brilliant Marie
Tempest. The only fresh interest in the
slight comedy lies in the methods by which
the widow marks down her prey. Every
widow in Sydney should take a First Aid
course at the Criterion.
* * * *
The comedy is packed with wit, though
humor is lacking; and one character, desig-
nated as a cynic, delivers himself of much
cheap snarling. But in the third act a
perfectly ludicrous, almost farcical, scene is
inserted, in which the old cynic, ordered by
Mrs. Dot to make love to her, for her own
fell purposes, and implored to put more ardor
into his make-believe, convinces her that he
really is in love with her. That, and the
succeeding scene, where Mrs. Dot, having
“MRS. DOT,” AT SYDNEY CRITERION
Blenkinsop (Graham Browne) declares his
bogus passion to Mrs. Dot (Marie Tempest).
discovered that he is pulling her leg, pulls
his in turn, are screamingly funny burlesques
of stage love-making. (Stage love-making is
funny enough, anyhow.)
* * * *
Marie Tempest brings to the characterisa-
tion of Mrs. Dot her wealth of finished
comedy. She repeats herself sometimes,
especially in her gestures and vocal
inflexions; but what actress, appearing in
such a range of quite similar characters,
wouldn’t? Graham Browne nobly sacrifices
his personality by making a quiet cameo of
the elderly cynic. The inestimable conse-
quence is that the burlesque love-making in
the third act is presented by two great artists
in pure comedy. lan Maclaren, as the
worried hero, does fairly well as that unsat-
isfactory individual. He has little to do
but to scowl and clench his hands, and he
scowls and clenches nobly. As an ingenuous
youth Roger Barry is to be congratulated

“Aladdin” must have had to rub the Lamp
hard and pay the Slave of the Ring over-
time to crowd the big Tait-Bailey show into
Sydney Palace, which wasn’t designed tor
such proceedings. But spite of being somewhat
cramped, the panto, keeps bright and busy in
a battery of costumes through 19 scenes, and
will crowd the little theatre for weeks.
There is enough of the simple old nursery
yarn left to swear by, and when money is
dashed about by Aladdin it is minted com
by the bagful. This will show the bright
modern kid that it isn’t Yank crookadrama
and belongs to the golden age before the
war. Barry Lupino, droll and acrobatic,
cleverly plays Abanazar, a brisk, bald pill-
fakir who sells medicine by the (bass) drum
and patters into some censored Oriental place
invented by the Clints, to sing and dance
.with the distinctively European Widow
Twankey, who is “Dad” Bailey, strangely
subdued and fashionably dressed. The virile
personality of the Australian actor cannot
be obscured altogether by his various dis-
guises and feminine tricks ; but lie does his
absurd job well. When he settles himself
m a loose wrapper to do some shewing, how
motherly he looks! A parody of “Going
Home” is artistically sung, and advertises
the fact that Dad knows how to use his
deep voice.
* * * *
Singing isn’t the strong point of either
Lalla de Baere (Prince Pekoe) or Winifred
i.a France (Aladdin). They get through
with good looks, costumes and the dashing
air that goes with tights. Olive Godwin
(Princess Badroulbadourl is a gracious
spectacle and a clear voice singing in the
painted wilderness. Higginson makes a
good-looking but husky Emperor, and, later
on, an ideal young Admiral. Fayette Perry
(Nieee) is a delightful big-little girl in the
well-contrived toyshop scene, and Leslie
Donaghev is joyouslv inane in a grinning
part. Zola Terrell, the temperamental
young person who can make her white arms
wriggle like powdered snakes, does two in-
teresting dances and makes a classic final
appearance at bouquet time in a sort of
draped Greek robe. Bonnie Quinlan hits the
joyous note in her solo dance, anu the sup-
porting ballet girls do their duty gracefully.
Florence and Alice Wilson and Lucie Oluff
are acrobatic in their revels. The comedy
scene, where the Widow Twankey’s kitchen
is wrecked by the renovators and the smallest
plumber is forced to sit on the burst water-
pipe, goes with a rush and a yell, and makes
all the grown-up kids regret that the harle-
quinade can’t be done at night.

When “Within the Law” was first pro-
duced at Melbourne Royal there was more
powder and shot in Muriel Starr’s voice
than is there nowadays. Outside of her voice
Miss Starr has no dramatic force to speak
of. She can’t strike a lone attitude on the
stage and thrill the house with a couple of
glances. She doesn’t cut a strikingly pic-
turesque figure in action or repose. Her
husky voice has ever been her all-important
asset, and it is most valuable in rapid repeti-
tion. Muriel can say “I do, I do, I do” or
“I don’t, I don’t, I don’t” as effectively as any
actress. Also she can catch her breath
between utterances, and speak with a sob in
her throat as convincingly as any. But her
tone has weakened, and some of the previous
outburst of indignation in her appeal to
Gilder, the Emporium boss, is wanting in
the revived effort. In both the climax to the
first act, where she threatens vengeance on
the bloated business man, and the climax to
the second act, where she proclaims her pos-
session of his offspring and his name, Miss
Starr was less strong than of yore. In minor-
details of naturalism she was a little more
adept last Saturday night. Two of the other
characters- —Aggie and Lawyer Demarest—
were more fully played by Miss Norma
Mitchell and Frank Harvey. Kimball, too,
made an admirably fresh and ingenuous
young Gilder, and was weak only in the
absurd burglarv scene, where he has to sit
on a couch and talk love to his soiled dove
whilst four crooks are standing silent in the
background waiting for the intruder’s pre-
sence to be explained. Styan is a larger and
more majestic Dad Gilder than his prede-
cessor, and George Bryant is _an entirely
new edition of Burke, the police-inspector.
When Piumer took the part the Inspector
was like a huge, arrogant Yankee drummer,
seeking whom he might ruin in the lag
trade. His tricks for entrapping suspects
appeared contemptible because he never
struck a note of human sympathy himself.
He put the police in a worse light than the
crooks. Bryant reverses the position. His
Inspector Burke has notions of benevolence
as far as a policeman can entertain them,
and although he knocks a crook down in his
office he doesn't play the brute in his manner
of bluffing a confession of murder out of Joe
Garson. Stage-manager Morrison has put
three more coats of melodramatic color on
his picture of hysterical Joe without making
it look less artificial. Harold Moran, as the
“stool pigeon,” utterly fails to account for
the value that Burke attached to his services,
and James Hughes presents Sergeant
“YOU’RE IN LOVE,” HER MAJESTY’S,
MELBOURNE.
Percy Pinklove the boatswain (Field
Fisher) tells Aloysius Wix (Alfred
Frith) the sad story of his life.
Cassidy as a.would-be funny man, rather
than a cold, morose cop. The whole effect
of “Within the Law” is considerably altered
by these changes of cast. Perhaps a com-
plete reproduction of the old show would
have been less interesting Ho the multitude
that remembers it.
* * * *
The revival of “You’re in Love” at Mel-
bourne Majesty’s reminds this writer of
“Lady Frederick,” the charming comedy
that Ethel Irving introduced to Australia.
When Paradine Fouldes had come a long
journey to visit his sister he consumed a lot
of dinner and promptly forgot it. His valet
assured him that he had chosen much food
from the menu. Paradine merely remarked:
“Strange; it has left *0 impression.”
“You’re in Love” is like Uncle Paradine’s
dinner. It leaves no impression. One swal-
lows the performance and forgets what it
was all about. The present revival reminds
one that a ridiculous marriage contract was
the only pretence of a plot, and that Maud
Fane’s slippers were, in a way of speaking,
transferred to the entertainment’s feet of
clay. Chorus and ballet (especially Madge
Elliott as solo dancer) serve to distract
public attention from the hollowness of
Connie Ediss’s claim to lay down inhuman
rules for Maud Fane’s hbneymoon. For the
rest there are the principals who either sing
or talk. Some of them even talk their songs
for the most part, and Fred Maguire would
be less often out of tune if he talked the
whole of his one ballad and half of a duet. The
least brilliant conversationalist in the party
is Field Fisher, whose character of an
ancient mariner must have been dragged in
to till up time. As an unnecessary interrup-
tion to the bustle and incoherency of a well-
dressed musical comedy poor Field Fisher
ought to be dragged out again. But “You’re
in Love” fills a want. Something of the
sort was required to supply the craving that
an annual pantomime creates, and the depar-
ture of the panto, was this show’s oppor-
tunity. The charms and vivacity of its
numerous Australian girl are not entirely
wasted on it. They might have been em-
ployed in trying to make the thing go at a
less propitious moment.
* * * *
Bill Anderson brought his panto, of “Jack
and the Beanstalk” to Melbourne Tivoli on
Saturday. It is a rather tepid diversion,
with few inspired moments for hero Jack,
and the Giant is mostly out of work. Also
the Demon is on the- half-pay list, while the
Fairy is an intermittent damsel who has
a minute or two of muffled speech and then
retires gracefully into the gloom. The only
real appeal to juvenile fancy is provided by
a ponderous effigy of the heavy villain who
drags his unlovely hoofs about and roars in-
fanticide through a huge property head. That
bit of misdirected energy lasts five minutes
or so; then Jack prods the monster in a
vital spot and the vast corpse is removed by
the miniature Rego on his shoulder. The
largest share of the entertainment falls to
Mabelle Morgan (Jack), Dame Farrow and
the female chorus. The girls wave their
limbs with the unanimity and aplomb that
come from long practice, and they also have
the merit of making one forget some of the
songs they support. Mabelle Morgan, as
Jack, bulges from the hero’s waistbelt with
prima-donna effects, and her deep-seated
soprano gives every ear a full helping of
song. Dame Farrow needs increased lung
power to blow the fog from his utterance,
and is further disqualified hy a humor that
has become wizened with long usage. Ethel
Bennetto’s Princess is a bit bashful in the
upper register, and Vera Benson’s Alice has
nothing better to offer than a pleasant
Semitic profile and colorless amiability.
Rego and his partner, Welch, are desultory
shrieks of mirth in some business that has
become rather monotonous with Welch’s
eternal beer. Daisy Sylvester is a sprightly
Columbine with a nimble Harlequin (Yorke
Gray), and Ricardo and Smith are effective
partners in Jessie the Cow. None of the
others merit dishonorable mention ; but the
scenic artists, Ricketts and Robins, demand
a word of mild censure for a series of freez-
ing, unimaginative backgrounds that tend to
lower the pulse of the whole performance.

Norma Talmadge plies “The Devil’s
Needle” at Sydney Crystal Palace, and
wrecks so many lives that the insurance
cos. begin to despair. Then she repents,
and you see Norma Talmadge unpick-
ing the stitches. In “Red, White and
Blue Blood,” at the Globe, Francis X.
Bushman mixes it with bandits, vil-
lainous counts, drownings and other excite-
ments, and emerges victorious with all the
good things enumerated in the last chapters
of Shannon Fife’s novel. At Hoyt’s and the
Haymarket, Olga Petrova goes into Exile
with a beast of a husband, while an obliging
young man waits in the offing as a sort of
salvage party. When the fun begins Olga
parades her emotions in the usual gorgeous
gowns.

Mary Turner (Muriel Starr) on her way to

A commendable Australian photo-play
was tried before an invited audience at
Sydney Royal last week. “The Enemy
Within,” written by Roland Stavely, features
“Snowy” Baker in a plot dealing with
I.W.W. machinations in Australia. The open-
ing scenes are much too slow; but when
“Snowy” Baker' begins his Douglas Fair-
banks stunts the tempo quickens. The
climax comes in a high dive by the hero
from a cliff to rescue the heroine, who (with
the usual foolishness of the villain) is lashed
to a rock whicfy the rising tide will sub-
merge. Why villains don’t kill the lady
straight off and save further worrj r is
known only to the writers >of photo-plays.
“The Enemy Within” is admirably photo-
graphed ; and with lavish cuts should take
its place on the Australian bill.

“The Six Maniacs of the Wire” are on
the job. They are not related in any way to
Australia’s censorship, but belong to the
other circus—Wirths’, opening at the Hippo-
drome (Sydney) on Saturday.
To-morrow will see the last oil the Royal
Strollers at Sydney Playhouse. On Saturday
evening The English Pierrots will carry on
the tradition of smart entertainment.
At Melbourne King’s the conversion of
Muggs and Gilly continues to invite the
patronage of the Salv’army. They “go to
meeting” and join in the chorus of “Gather
at the River,” after which they sin no more.
The S.A. can ask for no truer moral drama
than “Turn to the Right.” It is a story that
the late General Booth might have loved if he
had lived to see it, and the Taps’ advt.
seems to be missing a point in not calling
attention to the fact.
“Empty Pockets,” adapted from a story
by Rupert Hughes, is the latest speechless
drama at Melbourne Majestic. It features a
murdered millionaire with a penchant for
red-haired females, and asks five of the
favored type to account for his death. It is
a great mystery for amateurs in detective fic-
tion, and has enough excitement to pull the
eye out of focus. “Because of a Woman,,
with Belle Bennett as star, is the r l riangle s
current contribution to the thrills of the pro-
gramme. It measures seven reels, and every
reel is a knock-down in the unexpected.
“Exile,” another of Olga Petrova’s love-
affairs, is in action at Hoyt’s Melbourne shop
this week. Olga is the depressed missus ot
a Portuguese schemer, and encounters a
Yankee financier who has charms and cash
enough to smash the marriage-tie. Even
Olga’s loveliness, however, is ineffectual
against his armor-plated propriety, and he
won’t yield to her beguilement until he can
marry the lady. But the virtuous denoue-
ment arrives so late in the film that the spec-
tator has plenty of time to expect something
else.
Bert La Blanc’s Travesty Stars are still
thronging the second half of the programme
at Melbourne Bijou, While Thurber and
Thurber, the Alerts, Carlton Max, Billy and
Doris, and Jack Martin help themselves to
the applause in the first hour of the evening.
Of the bunch the Alerts are the most arrest-
ing with their skilful balancing of swords and
other factious ironmongery. Next Saturday
the foregoing leave the premises and the
“Robinson Crusoe” panto, will take the stage.
Adelaide Majestic has a nearly-new bill,
with the mysterious Phrozo at the head of it.
Rose and Dell, delayed by illness, have
arrived at last, and made themselves popu-
lar at once; also Reo and Helmar do some
fine hand-balancing. At the Tivoli the old
“Never Too Late to Mend” has gone as well
as ever, giving Lesley Adrienne a good chance
as the persecuted boy. The March Show
was responsible for a good Adelaide Orches-
tra concert (Winsloe Hall conductor), and
on the other side of North Terrace the Eng-
lish Pierrots” have had a warm farewell after
a long open-air season. Tim Royal, is having
a good time. Advanced booking for the Dick
Whittington” panto, is heavy; while, as tor
Evelyn Thaw, in the “Redemption picture,
manager Myers advertises with pride that
the crowd pretty well wrecks the vestibule
and the ticket-box in its eagerness to get in.
The disturbance has now been moved to the
Town Hall.

From an Adelaide critic:
The collection of Old Masters now hung in the
Adelaide Art Gallery has been lent by the Public
Trustee, administering the estate of P. J. Cels, critic
and dealer, who died here lately from the effects of
gassing received in the defence of his country. They
are all of the Dutch or Flemish school, and at the
opening it was suggested that S. Aus. ought not
to let them get away again. The Adelaide Gallery’s
present fine collection is entirely modern, and to
secure a few of these would make it more attractive.
But to buy them in the mass would be a waste of
money, even supposing S. Aus. could afford half the
£70,000 or so they are supposed to be worth. Quite
a lot of the 31 pictures are of the type that may be
seen in any dealer’s shop in any European capital,
“attributed to” Raphael or someone else. Assuming
the genuineness of this lot, the most effective is a big
“Peasants going to Market,”, by Rubens. They are
heavy Flemish types, the woman especially, and an
attractive group, especially a very human small boy,
and a swan who is exchanging a few brisk remarks
with a dog. A regular gallery picture ; value, de-
pendent on war-time and the local circumstances.
The other most attractive pictures are portraits, of
the Burgomaster type, three by De Keyser, and one
possibly by Van Dyck, though this is arguable;
another is the striking portrait of a youth, artist
unknown. Cuyp has a valuable stable-interior, and
Brouwer a realistic tavern-brawl which at once
recalls Meissonier. Others are boors carousing, clever
portrait-studies of types, and some rough stuff which
is out of place. At the opening, W. J. Sowden,
president of the Gallery, made a strong appeal for
Government help in buying, pointing out that of the
£75,000 or so in the present collection, private
generosity has found all but £12,000. Jackson,
replying for the Ministry, promised a favorable ear
to “any reasonable financial proposal,” recognising
that the State’s picture-gallery is an “essentially
democratic” thing.

“Gron” tells the story of a consignment
of Anzacs:—
Birdie was a bright A’ictorian lad of the original
Division who had the bad luck to lose an eye early
in the game. He was sent to England to recuperate,
and in due course was put in more or less good
order and given charge of 100 other patehed-up
Anzacs to deliver in good order at Alexandria.
He took delivery of his party in Plymouth on the
night prior to sailing and addressed them in these
fatherly terms: “Now, you dinkums, we’re pushing
off at nine ack emma to-morrow. Never mind
where we’re going; but it’ll be a of a long
time before you’ll have another chance to get blithered,
so make the most of it to-night. Keep together in
parties, so that the red caps won’t be able to pinch
you. And, my God, don’t let one of you be late
in the morning! Now Pip O.Q. [Push off quickly]”.
THE HOLIDAY.
"Here for your health! Gracious —I thought
you were healthy enough!”
“So I am, but I knocked myself out making
enough money to get down here!”
“Hoo-blankv-rav!” yelled the blokes as they pipped
off Next morning at 8.30 Birdie lined ’em lip and
discovered that his party had grown to 120. The
surplus had heard about the bonza “loot” who told
his Billjims to get shiekered while they had a
chance. On arrival at Alexandria, Birdie again
delivered his pious injunction before the night’s
revel. They were to embark for Anzac on the
morrow.
Next day to the tick of time there were 150 gal-
lant Anzacs on parade. Birdie shipped ’em all with
a grin. It was only on the beach at the Cove that
he commenced to wonder how he’d account for the
additional 50. He told me about it afterwards.
“I was beginning to get the wind up in a most
colossal fashion when damned old Beachy Bill
dropped about three hundredweight of H.E. in the
middle of us and stonkered 50 of the poor blighters,
so I was home and dry.”
A pukka Tommy officah who was absorbing some
hops at the next table surveyed Birdie with amaze-
ment. Birdie hopped up and remarked cheerily,
“You don’t believe that, do you, you sceptical old
Scorpion ?” Whipping out his glass eye he dropped
it with case, precision and delicacy in the amazed
Majah’s malt and hops. “Ever try an optical cock-
tail?” They got very drunk together that night.
“Whist”: A nmn never hears the shell
that gets him on its full flight. Sound
travels at 40ft. per second; the muzzle
velocity of an h.e. shell is double that. You
hear it passing over your head, and at the
same time you see it bursting a hundred
yards beyond. This gives no chance of
ducking or dodging. Still, by ducking into
a shell-hole when one hears the sound one
may escape the hail of splinters, etc., sent
out by the explosion.
Surely “Somebody” (B. 7/3/T8)
is exaggerating. I was studying female con-
tours for two or three years and I never
heard unkind or personal remarks addressed
to the various duchesses who consented to
sit for us. For some months I was doing
the “nood” at the Yic. Artists’ galleries, and
I declare that the proceedings were as placid
and uneventful as gentlemanly artists and a
generally respectable model could make them.
Methodists will doubtless consider “respect-
able” ridiculous, but I affirm that the almost
invariable respectability of the models was
responsible for the solemn decorum of the
sittings. Occasionally a girl of another sort
might consider her position justified free
and easy behavior, but if she became demoral-
ising in her gaiety she was promptly fired.
Similarly if students were lacking in ordi-
nary courtesies to the Venus on the throne
they were just as promptly suppressed. It
is obvious that no life-class could endure if
the art director tolerated rudeness on either
side. Posing for artists, which “Somebody”
prefers, -is, of course, a different matter. In
that case the behavior of the model usually
depends on the behavior of the artist.
* * * *
“The Pariah”: “Kari’s” appeal (B.
21/2/T8) for some means to stop the adop-
tion of little white Australians by colored
people has my backing. Some years ago I
was a teacher at one of the biggest schools
in a British port in Asia, and here are three
instances within my own knowledge of white
boys whom 1 have every reason
to believe were Australians
that had been “adopted” by
Chinese. A, aged about 12,
lived in a sampan in Macao.
He couldn’t speak English,
and didn’t know how he came
into the Chinese family. Ah
Sam Pan protested that he had
bought the boy in Sydney. B,
aged about 14, I found work-
ing in a padi field a few miles
above Canton. He could speak
a very little English. He
remembered seeing his mother
receive money from a Chinese,
and after being given some
food went to sleep. He woke
up on a boat. He said he
came from “Chinnee.” He
had been badly treated, and
will carry for life the scars of
beatings he received. C, a
wizened specimen who might
have been 14 or 40, spoke
lurid broken English. He said
he was sold by his father for
opium. The web of his fingers
had been burned away—-he
said through repeated burn-
ings with lighted sticks as
punishment. He had also suf-
fered a horribly clumsy
attempt at castration. This
poor wretch was addicted to
opium, and once got to my
beer and whisky, stealing so
much that it all but killed
him. From what I could
make out he came from Broome. 1
traced also a 15-year-old girl, who had had
her left breast removed. She even recog-
nised a picture of Sydney. Poor kid —I
wonder what’s happened to her in the last
five years!
IN CASE OF ACCIDENT.
ST. PETER: “ Hey, mate, what have you there under your arm?”
MIKE: “Share, ’tis nothin’ at tal, at tal —only a bit of a fire-extinguisher in case you
draft me into the wrong mob.”
There are other ways of getting a' title
than by promising a Prime Minister not to
be a nark. Lately a London music-hall man-
ager named Tozer was knighted ; and while
the public was still wondering why, Oswald
Stoll, the Australian, best-known and cer-

in England, supplied a possible answer:—
I wish to place on record that since the year 1908

which were to eventuate in a knighthood. In vari-

But the business ought to be put on a

£lO,OOO, C.0.D., while another asks £15.000,
indicates great lack of organisation. They
really do need Hughes in England, after all.
Cook’s River-road, a notable institution,
has enjoyed a mention by N. S. Wales’ Upper
House. The road is very long, very narrow,
very crooked, as brown as a drought, as
dusty ,as a whirlwind, and so far as con-
cerns the height of its buildings, painfully
stumpy. There is nothing really wrong
about it except the almost complete lack of
redeeming features. It resembles nothing
to be found in the Louvre or the Vatican,
and the Roman Coliseum doesn’t resemble
it either. Some people say that even pigs
shouldn’t live in it; others that it should be
full of pigs. Most of the buildings ought
to be burned down. There are many muni-
cipalities along the road and heaps of
mayors and aldermen, but they can do
nothing of any value if they wanted to,
which they don’t, for it would be too much
trouble and perspiration. Even if one
council made its bit of the road a leafy
avenue 140 ft. wide, with palaces on each
side, the bulk of the grievance would remain.
And Cook’s River-road can’t be avoided like
some eyesores. It is the way out of the
city for the traveller bound for the beauti-
ful South Coast, so the traveller holds his
nose and takes his life in his hands as he
skirmishes by the double tramline, and goes.
The road isn’t a very rich proposition from
a landlord’s point of view, despite the
traffic, for whatever the rent of a building
may be thereabouts it ought to be less.
Rank and fashion don’t go shopping in this
great artery of Australia’s greatest city;
the peerage avoids it, and the royal family
has never been seen there. The remark
made in the Legislative Council upon the
subject was that, as the many municipali-
ties can’t possibly agree to do anything, and
there is no Greater Sydney Council with
power to do things—that G. S. Council, like
the Harbor bridge, has got no further after
many years’ discourse —dhe patient Govern-
ment should take the work of renovation in
hand. But somebody said that the time
wasn’t ripe, or words to that effect.

Thanks for the homhsick spasms divine
We feel when you kiddies drop us a line.
Stars above in a jewelled dish ;
Mud an’ slush to our kneetops, too;
Shells a-bang and bullets a-swish —
Through it all, kids, we think of you.
Dear little girls, our eyes do shine
Whenever you deign to drop us a line.
Bill went West in the last big drive
(Your letters were read, though you wrote to
him■!) ;
Fred went down when he tried to dive
Into a shell-hole when dusk was dim.
But your bundles of sweet stuff jus’ sounded fine
When he woke an’ we told him you’d dropped a line.

“Peter”: I take up “Myola”(B. 21/2/'IS)
on the subject of comely models. It is not
valuable to an art-student’s education to fill
his eye with the agreeably monotonous con-
tours of chorus girls. All life is good train-
ing for eye and hand, and it is of no conse-
quence whether the model has the figure of
an ironing-board or is the latest descendant
of Venus di Medici so long as the artist is
considering her as an artistic problem. In
my own fairly extensive experience I can
only recall two models who approached the
pictorial effervescence of Yank magazine
illustrators, and I deny that I would have
received any greater artistic enlightenment
by looking at a whole gallery of Vera
Pearces. Beauty in bulk is terribly weari-
some. If some of our fresh girl-poets and
other adorers of the sex could only take a
course in a life school they would have some
rapid and necessary exercises in disillusion-
ment, and learn to put Lovely Woman in her
proper place in imaginative creation. One
of the merits of the late Rodin was that he
never looked at Beauty from the conven-
tional point of view. Among his great
masterpieces is a bronze of a nude grandma
with Old Age ravaging her pitiful form; yet
that wonderful study is of more value to the
artist than a chorusful of the piffling pul-
chritude that drives “Myola” to such heights
of rapture.

“7043”: The tin-lid is worth having. Dur-
ing the October stunt near Polygon Wood I
was stunned by a splinter from a shell which
burst 20 yards from me. On gaining cover
later I found that the helmet had been struck
and bent into an arc fully an inch in depth.
The splinter had then glanced upward,
grazed the crown, and gone on. On the other
hand, I once saw a soldier suddenly halt and
fall in a heap like a pithed bullock. He had
been killed by a small piece of shell which
had penetrated the brain through the crown
of the helmet. The hole made in the metal
would not admit a thin lead-pencil. That,
of course, was a direct hit; but, given any-
thing less a right-angle for approach,
the lid will do its work.
“J.D.8.”: “Pot Jostler’s” kick (B.
14/2/T8) against the unfairness of classing
Billjim as immoral reminds me that com-
mentators on the Boxer trouble
gave the Australians a very
high character. The same wasn’t
passed to all the other troops
who withstood the famous siege
of the legations. On the side
defended by the Russians was
the compound for the native
women refugees. The Russians
hatched a- plot. One night they
began to retire on the com-
pound, firing as they went.
Their delight in the pantomime
got the better of their discre-
tion and they fired so heavily
that a British squad was sent
out hurriedly to support them.
The bluejackets arrived just as the bars of
the compound were being withdrawn, and of
course the bottom fell out of the plot. One
of the senior Russian officers suicided to
escape the ridicule.

“Ripeka” : In the multitude of war deco-
rations there isn’t one that approaches in
rarity Maoriland’s own
medal. Only 23 of these
Crosses have been awarded,
all for conspicuously gallant
services in the Maori wars
of 1863-71. Three natives
who fought with the British
(one of them old Major
Ropata) had the distinc-
tion pinned on their blue
tunics; the other heroes
were pakehas. To-day
there are only seven sur-
vivors of the little squad.
One is the much-medalled
Rowley Hill, of Auckland,
who served his guns in the
old-time Navy in the Baltic
and before Sebastopol,
fought at Delhi, Cawnpore
and Lucknow in the Indian
Mutiny, and stopped a
stray bullet as a freelance
under Garibaldi; then
forest-ranged under Von
Tempsky and saved the
day at Jerusalem pa on
the Hawkes Bay coast in
’69. Another isi. William
Lingard, one of John
Bryce’s cavalry. His
special bit of gallantry
was hauling out a comrade
from under a shot-down
horse in front of the fire-
spitting Hauhau palisades
at Tauranga-ika. Captain
Gilbert Mair, another of
the old brigade, put the
fear of the Lord into Te
Kooti in 1870 by chasing
him over the Rotorua
plains, with only a couple
of dozen or so men, against
the rebel’s two hundred.
and himself killing the Ilauhap’s best, fighting
man, the half-caste Baker McLean. Mair
is a physical marvel in his old age—fit and
keen and badly disappointed at not being-
given a chance to deal Fritz one to go on
with.
A South Yarra (Melb.) maiden of 14
had a day out last week. There was nothing
doing at home. Things were dead slow. Also
mother found fault with the bored damsel
for not paying enough attention to her luxu-
riant crop of hair. So the little lady went
forth into the world and cut her hair short,
returning home at eventide with the news,
that some bad men had chloroformed her,
clipped her locks, dressed her up in boy’s
clothes and left her at large to recover her
skirts before she went back to mother. On
the strength of this news her parents sent
for the police. Of course that was the end
of it. Nevertheless, Lizzie’s effort to relieve
the monotony of existence was a brilliant
success. In the dead-slowness of her present
and future, whilst she is getting her lost
hair back, the young adventuress will have
a Past to live upon. She can just sit and
think of the exciting day when she invented
the brutal men, and the chloroform, and the
change in the sex of her garments.
Twenty-five or 30 years ago the maiden
aged 15 to 40 who went walking at dusk on
a quiet road and was rushed at and tied to
a tree by from one to 18 men was quite
common. Generally they tore her blouse
and in desperate cases were known
to steal her boots. They also took
her long distances in different vehicles
and left her at different places after
swearing her to secrecy. There were occa-
sions when they broke into a house during
the absence of the family; and despite the
desperate resistance of the general servant
or the cook or the flapper or whatever soli-
tary girl it might be, they made a wreck of
her collar, mussed up her hair something
dreadful, and left her fastened to the bed-
post by a bit of string. Occasionally articles
of small value were missing and occasionally
they weren’t. Heavy furniture was never
removed. As a rule the ruffians did no seri-
ous harm, but at times the victim swore that
they did. and in those primitive days the
Law lent an ear to the tale, and sometimes
served out hangings and other long sen-
tences. But either the little lady isn’t as
persuasive as she used to be, or the Law is
older, and probably has troubles with its
own wife, and its ear is not so easily bor-
rowed. *

“H.G.T.”: The Majali happened along
early the other morning and passed an
orderly carrying a couple of pails.
Realising the difficulty of giving a salute
with two handfuls of buckets, the orderly
“carried on.” The Majah, however, wouldn't
stand it. He invited the orderly to put down
the buckets and salute. I don’t know how
much time is wasted at other places in this
way; but this little farce stands to the
credit of Keswick (S.A.).

I never ’ad the poetry complaint,
And use me ’ands more awkward nor a crab 1
But cuttin’ aht the lovey-dovey fuss,
’Liza —say vus!
I ain’t a bloomin’ pictcher, by the books ;
But other things comes ’andy, so to speak;
You needn’t ’ave to worry over looks
With bizness clearin’ thirty-bob a week!
I ain’t the sort o’ man to drink an’ cuss.
’Liza—-say yus!
I’ll do it on the ’andsome, you kin bet;
I ain’t yer parsimonious sort o’ bloke.
There’s four-roomed ’ouses waitin’ for to let —•
What price me yeller barrer an’ me moke?
Yer’ll never ’ave to travel in a ’bus.
’Liza —say yus!
Nor yet again it ain't the bizness side —
I never loved a gal like you before!
You love me, too, so up an’ be me bride,
An’, if we’re ’appy, ’oo kin give us more?
Why, all the bloomin’ world belongs to us!
'Liza —say yus!

Neurasthenia has robbed the Federal
Opposition of Charlie McDonald’s fire and
eloquence. The poor man is now incapable
of a big mental effort and spends much of
his time in trying to dodge his malady in
the peace of his home. McDonald will be
badly missed by the Laborites, for, before
he rose to the Speakership, he was one of
the best of their light artillery. In style
he resembled the late Fraser and Roberts,
and like them wore his nerves to shreds.

Brig.-General Johnston, of Melbourne, is
to go to Itabaul to represent the Common-
wealth Government, vice Pethebridge,
deceased. Johnston has had quite his fair
share of active service: he joined up in Aug-
ust, 1914, and his last command was the fifth
division of artillery in a place where the
mud is deep and the going willing. He was
one of the few old militia officers who had
the permanent man’s passion for polished
metal, and the only growl Billjim had about
him was that, whether they were in the line
or resting (his crowd didn’t have much rest-
ing), he was always liable to have them
whistled out to make something shine. A
lirm administrator and an experienced busi-
ness man with a saving sense of humor and
no swagger, he ought to do good work up
north.
From the Federal Gallery:—
The disembodied spirit of John Murray has found a
refuge in Jens August Jensen, Minister for Customs.
Jensen is socially at his best when he has bent him-
self into a U with a cushion behind one extremity
and a table supporting the other. He rarely sits ; he
lounges like a grotesque mastodon trying to shift
the centre of gravity from his stomach. I have seen
him using the front bench as a hammock ; and there
is mute pathos in his constant but unsuccessful hunts
for the most recumbent position. Besides resembling
Yawn Murray in his inability to maintain a
thoroughly wakeful attitude with perfect bodily
repose, he recalls the Victorian in his love for a good
story. Fasten Jens to an expensive cigar after a
decorative dinner, and he rapidly becomes a Curate’s
Guide to Knowledge.
“LIGHTING MAG," Captain-phaplain. of
the Fourth Battalion.
A returned Auzac, asked who was the
most popular officer in the Fourth, replied
“Foightin’ Mae ivery toime.” “Mac’’ was
born in Scotland more than half a century
ago. His family emigrated to Australia in
the ’seventies, took up land in Queensland
and prospered ; but the youngster wanted to
see the world and he succeeded. But, as
the doggerel puts it, “at the Cross he came
a cropper,” joined the Salvation Army and
has been one of its leading lights ever since.
He went with the Fourth Battalion as chap-
lain-captain, and the story of Gallipoli is
sprinkled with references to Mac —the big-
souled padre who never ducked and never
preached, and just because of that was the
guide and friend of the men who most needed
“a hand from home.”
The universal chorus of Australian praise
over John Redmond’s grave recalls that
when he was in N. S. Wales 35 years ago
various J’s.P. who had dared to sign an
address of welcome to “the rebel Irishman”
were struck off the roll.
Let Melbourne not forget that the late
Brassey was the first person with a large
voice to suggest the improvement of the
Yarra bank on the south side of Princes
Bridge. In Brassey.’s time that portion of
our world was a collection of marine store
bric-a-brac affording a natural Forum for
Comrade Fleming and other Bolsheviks, and
Brassey used to pass it often with eyes and
nose closed to its peculiar charms. * At a
Mayoral spread he proposed the conversion
of the offence into a garden, whereon the
Age took it up and hammered away at the
idea, whenever a few inches could be made
available. “Bill” Admans, now of Federal
Hansard, was the staff-man ordered by
David Syme to shout for the reform of the
landscape, and as Admans was a gardening
enthusiast there was a punch in every para-
graph.
Passed at 68, Bob Norman, one of the
best-known Sydney labor men of the early
’nineties. He went West when Coolgardie
was booming, and was the first Labor candi-
date to run for Kalgoorlie, but failed to beat
the then sitting member, C. J. Moran. That
was in 1597. He made several other at-
tempts to get into the Westralian Parlia-
ment on the Labor ticket, but never suc-
ceeded. When the Labor split occurred he
sided with Hughes, and has been a sad man
for many months past.
John Symott D’Arcy, Secretary of N. S
Wales Navigation Department, was a fine,
brainy battler. His father was a policeman
who had climbed to sex-geant and in that
rank had settled down at Mudgee to bring
up his family. Young Jack kept on at the
local public school as a pupil-teacher, and
read till he got his B.A. and was admitted to
the Bai\ He became the Navigation Depart-
ment’s Secretary in 1010. Outside the shop
he was the Public Service Association’s
chairman, a Public Sei’vice Board’s official
examiner, a cricket and football enthusiast,
and chairman of the Board of the University
Club, which he helped to establish.
Old J. H. Howe will fade out of S. Aus.
politics before the coming elections. He has
been in the State Parliament since 1881, with
a single break of one year, representing his
solitary defeat at the poll. Now, at the age
of 79, he thinks he has earned a rest. A
well-to-do grazier who began life as a
police-trooper (like his old colleague the late
Speaker Coles), Howe has seen things from
various angles, and has always been useful.
He was Minister for Lands in various
Cabinets under Downer and Cockburn ; but
perhaps his best work was done in the
Federal Conventions of 20 years ago, where
he was a hard-headed Scotch business man
among the politicians.
“Paddy Star” : In 1881, when John Red-
mond was in Queensland, he was invited by
Dr. K. I. O’Doherty to dinner at the officers’
mess at Lytton Camp. O’Doherty was on
the medical staff. The visit caused as much
commotion as a cat would in a canary cage.
I l’ode up to Brisbane —about 12 miles —
starting at midnight, with Redmond, and
found him a charming companion. Next
morning we went up to Goodna —Willie
Redmond being of the party—and John laid
the foundation-stone of the R.C. church.
When John Dillon came to Queensland, later
on, a little dinner was given to him by the
late Paddy Perkins. Dillon, talking with
me, advised that Australians should not in-
dulge in locally-elected Governors, as they
would be likely to lean to their political pals.
Redmond was not a fast speaker as a rule,
but there were times when he climbed up to
180 words a minute.
Wounded four times, gassed once, D.S.O.’d,
mentioned in despatches more than once, and
still on duty—that’s the record of Major
Jerry Selmes, who in private life was an
accountant in Sydney. He is a son of Cap-
tain Jeremiah Selmes, of North Sydney, who
followed the sea for close on half a century.
Though the major isn’t 40 yet he has seen a
lot of fighting. He went through the S’Afri-
can trouble, and when the Big Scrap started
left with the First Division. Three of his
bi’others are also serving with the guns, and
the fourth brother has reserved a bunk on
one of the next transports.

81, Elizabeth Banks Smith, the widow of
Canon George Banks Smith, the last of the
skypilots in Tasmania to draw Imperial pay.
The old lady was a sister to the late Wil-
liam Walker, M.L.C., of Windsor (N.S.W.).
She was the last of the original Walker
family.

“Macliaon’s” Book of the Browns :—•
The old hands of Newcastle would chuckle if they
could read in the asbestos edition of The Bulletin
about the fuss that is being made over Jimmy and
Sandy Brown, the founders of the coal firm of J.
and A. Brown. Jimmy and Sandy were well-known
characters in Newcastle in the middle of last cen-
tury. They were a pair of Scottish “wabsters,” and
came to the colony to work at the Stockton Cloth
Factory, which was founded in the early forties of
last century by the Donaldsons, one of whom is
still flourishing on the shores of Lake Macquarie.
Newcastle had in those days a population of 2000
and Maitland 6000; Sydney had 35,000 and Parra-
matta 10,000. Yet with that small population to
work on Donaldson’s Cloth Factory was prosperous
and was proud of its products, and it is recorded
that a billiard-table cloth 10 yards long and six
yards wide was made there at Christmas, 1845, for
the Northumberland Hotel, West Maitland. Mr.
Donaldson was then the leading man in Newcastle.
He owned, besides the cloth factory, saltpans and
ships, and worked a coal-mine in competition with
the A. A. Co., while Jimmy and Sandy Brown were
yet working weavers at his Stockton factory.
The story the “old hands” would tell of the
historic lawsuit which ended the A. A. Co.’s coal
monopoly, and in which Jimmy Brown figured as
defendant, was that the rich Dumaresqs and others
put Brown forward as a man of straw. Probably
Jimmy Brown had not enough money to fight a police-
court case, let alone a Supreme Court action and a
Full Court appeal. A Scotch “wabster” is not a
man of means, and Jimmy Brown came here on con-
tract wages. Nor could he have got much money
with his wife, for she was a miner’s daughter and
earned her respectable living as a servant at
Donaldson’s house. Sandy Brown lived and died a
bachelor. It is most improbable, then, that the
Browns would have risked the few pounds they had
been able to earn at weaving in fighting the rich
Australian Agricultural Co. Later on in life they
certainly were a pair of money-grubbers, pure and
net very simple, and .they left no single record in
Newcastle of anything attempted or done for the
benefit of the city or the citizens. So the old
hands would probably be right in their statements
that Jimmy Brown was a mere catspaw used by
the rich Dumaresqs to grab the coal monopoly out
of the hands of the A. A. Co. in 1846.

On the day that his tenth child was born
Jack Wiltshire, a councillor of the Walpeup
(Vic.) Shire, got word that his eldest son
had been wounded at the Front; so he
went and enlisted to fill up the gap. His
brother Bob kept his end up through the
Gallipoli row, and after it was all over a
bolting horse ran into him and killed him
as he walked down a street in Cairo.
Forty months of active service without a
single day in hospital was part of the record
of Lieut. W. T. Watson when a piece of
high-explosive shell ripped a nine-inch slit
in his equator. A N. S. Wales and Aus-
tralian Rugby Union football forward, he
was playing with Newtown, Sydney, when
the war whistles called. The first transport
out of Fort Jackson carried him to Rabaul;
then Egypt and Gallipoli; and finally France
and a D.C.M. during the Somme offensive.
“Billy Blue” adds a footnote to history:—
John Black, the pioneer of the Black family
(there’s a grandson, Reggie, still in the N. S.
Wales Leg. Council), was the first merchant captain
to sail a vessel through Bass Straits, earlier skippers
sailing south of Tasmania to get to Sydney. Captain
Black, in command of the Harbinger brig, of 56 tons,
from the Cape of Good Hope, arrived at the mouth of
the Straits on January 1, 1801. He had no chart,
but believed there was a passage, and pushed in.
He got through without a mishap, made a chart of
his track, arrived in Port Jackson on January 12 and
reported his success to Governor King, only to find
that lI.M. sloop Lady Nelson, in command of
Lieutenant Grant, R.N., had gone through the Straits
during December. Governor King purchased the Har-
binger, changed her name to Norfolk, armed her, and
placed her under the command of William House.
This was the genesis of the Australian navy. The
little vessel was armed with six small guns and
carried a crew of 20.
GREETING: EASTER, 1918.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Now when others speed good wishes to the loved
and living only
In the far lands, on the sea-ways, or wherever they
may roam,
1 am sending you a greeting lest your spirit should
go lonely
In the Halls of Highest Heaven for a loving word
from home.
I irst, I send you love most loyal (though on earth I
shall not meet you)
From the land you loved, and died for, that is robed
in summer sheen;
And I think that you will listen when my love goes
out to greet you,
Though I may not hear you answer, for the star-
ways lie between.
Know the things you loved recall you; for the well-
known gully’s holding
Moss and fern and flickering shadow, as when we two
wandered there;
And the rills go singing ever, and the woods are
green and golden,
And the sweep of misty mountains at the dawn is
still as fair.
And at night the grey road stretching where the
moonbeams shift and glisten
Still is fringed with silken grasses, as when we two
walked thereon;
And the dark pines o’er it whisper as they whispered
—do you listen ?
AVhen we strove to catch their meaning on a night
forever gone.
Oh, I think 3'ou know, as memories of the dead days
gather o’er me,
That I speak your name this morning with a
passionate pride ; and yet
Bitter tears of hopeless longing blot the blue hills
out before me—•
Pride is mighty, Ileart’s-Beloved, but it cannot curb
regret.
N.S.W.

When a man has to clothe him-
self out of a limited allowance for
dress, he invariably thinks more
about the price he should pay for
a suit than anything else.
But the fact that his spending
must be limited is the very reason
why he should consider what he
is likely to get for what he intends
to pay.
The cheapest in made-to-measure clothes is nearly
always the lowest in quality —is cheapest, as a
rule, because something of quality is sacrificed
consequently is of doubtful value in relation to price.
An Austral Sac Suit to measure, however, may
be purchased to a price low enough to meet the
demands of the man of limited means who wants
to appear well-dressed. And by virtue of the
quality of fabrics, standard of tailoring, style and
fit, which are essentials to service and satisfaction
in made-to-measure clothes, offers the very best
his money affords.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Bulletin has hitherto tried to get the
Commonwealth Treasury to do three things
in connection with War Loans: —
(1) To refrain from prejudicing tlie future of
Australia by issuing' tax-free loans.
(2) To make some arrangement with the banks
by which people could lend their future savings.
(3) To sell annuities.
(1) In connection with the present loan
lenders have the option of taking either 44
p.c. tax-free loans- or 5 p.c. loans subject
to income-tax. It is an unsatisfactory
arrangement to this extent: that 44 p.c.
tax-free is still worth a good deal more
than 5 p.c. to the very rich taxpayer.
Watt, Federal Acting-Treasurer, who is
handling the present loan, announces,
however, that this Government will not
issue another tax-free loan. This should
produce some big subscriptions. (2) An
arrangement has been made with the
banks by which they will advance to approved
clients 00 p.c. of the face value of the new
loan, charging 4 p.c. for the accommodation,
the advances to be for periods up to IS
months. Two years would be better ; and
it may be possible to arrange for that in
special cases, though the matter will not
rest wholly with the banks, for they, of
course, have had to arrange to get this cheap
money, if they should need it. Whether
some of them will be able to finance sub-
scribers for periods in excess of 18 months at
the 4 p.c. rate depends therefore upon
whether the Commonwealth Bank will give
them backing. For, of course, it is the Com-
monwealth Bank that is standing behind the
new scheme. This plan for helping would-
be lenders to subscribe future savings will be
of special value to insurance offices, which
can tell in advance pretty well what their
accumulations for the next two or three years
will amount to. As a matter of fact the
Mutual Life and Citizens’, which has already
subscribed four and a half millions and is
now putting in for another two millions, has
adopted this plan throughout. It didn’t have
all the money. But it was as sure as any-
body can be of anything in the future that a
certain amount would flow in, in premiums,
etc., during the next two or three years. (3)
Nothing has yet been done in the way of
selling annuities; yet this plan would prob-
ably suit thousands of people who have a
certain amount of capital to live on and
would be quite satisfied to invest it in return
for an assured income during life. With a
live man at the Treasury something will be
done with this proposal in the future if it
becomes necessary to float another loan.
Meantime, however, it is some satisfaction to
know that two out of three of this paper’s
pet ideas in connection with War Loan flota-
tion have already been either wholly or in
part adopted.

But the increase of foOOO-odd over the
figures of a year ago does not necessarily
mean that the pint-pot is being filled more
often. Last year the metropolitan breweries
combined to do a little price-fixing. First
of all, in conjunction with the_ licensed
victuallers’ associations, they fixed the mini-
mum retail liquor-prices for hotels, the
arrangement incidentally providing that
supplies, would be denied the price-cutter.
The breweries, having created the machinery
whereby Bung could make a little more,
naturally didn’t forget themselves. And, no
doubt, the increased profit for the past half-
year is principally due to that mutual
arrangement between brewery and pub.
That something had to be done is shown by
the increase in expenses during recent years:
Half-year Gross Rents Wages, Charges

In IDI2 wages, charges and management
exes, absorbed 05.1 p.e. of gross profit on
brewings; in 11)17 the cut had leaped to
almost 84 p.e. Now. however, on the in-
creased profit the percentage has been got
down to 80.3.
In spite of the effects of six o’clock clos-
ing and increased costs. Toohey’s is not
going into mourning. Take two five-year
comparisons:—
1908. 1913. 1918.
Liabilities £89,709 £83,846 £72,178
Breweries, hotels and plant 315,200 358,481 392,657
Stocks 68,291 88,297 132,535
Book debts . . . . 112,954 147,965 143,067

In five years to 11)13 the co. improved its
liquid assets by £57,330, and, besides making
additions to plant, added £43,281 to proper-
ties, etc. During the past five years the
liquid position has been bettered by £57,793,
and £34,170 has been added to properties,
etc. For the first period the dividend aver-
aged almost 0 p.e. p.a., and for the past
five years almost 8 p.e. p.a: So if Cold Tea
thought six o’clock closing was going to
prove a smashing blow to the breweries it
was mistaken. As a matter of fact, some
inquiry ought to be made to find out exactly
what has been the result of early closing:
whether it has not turned every brothel into
a sly-grog shop, and thus indirectly in-
creased the ravages of venereal disease.
Still, the beer industry is under a cloud.
And the future cannot be foretold : anything
might happen when the present arrangement
lapses six months after peace is made. The
no-liquor party is still very active —only the
other day the Hotel Sydney, one of the
city’s most important residential pubs,
acquired a license by permission of the
Court, and Cold Tea has promptly lodged an
appeal against the transfer. But in the
liquor referendum there were many who
considered early-closing merely as a win-
the-war necessity; and there is no telling
how they will view the question later on.
Apart altogether from the matter of restric-
tions, there will be many a parched tongue
when we commence to pay our war bill;
so the future for breweries is not at all
bright. Wherefore this co.’s 16s. shares,
which were worth 275. in July, 1914, can be
had now for about 18s.

To be quite frank, this page doesn’t think
much of the table it has constructed with
difficulty. To begin with, the first little co.
—Pearson, Law, Ltd.- —paid £7OOO for the
goodwill of the Pearson-Law business and
something also went in preliminary exes.;
but this paper doesn’t know exactly how
much, because it doesn’t know* what profit
was made during the first few months of the
co.’s life. The business, with a lot of
energy behind it, had got a good start before
the war came to give it a further lift. The
original capital of about £35,000 was soon
insufficient; and the ordinaries, which the
old proprietors had accepted, left the profit
in the concern for three years. In 1916, to
make up for this, they took £5664 in shares.
Then, as extensive advertising had thor-
oughly introduced the co.’s “Pelaco” trade-
mark, it was decided to re-form the co. and
give it that name. The capital as at Dec.,
1916, and Dec., 1917, compares this way:—
1916. 1917.

Some of the shares were evidently issued
against goodwill; for the old goodwill item,
which had been reduced to £2OOO net, has
sprung up to £17,051; and underwriting
commission and preliminary exes, stand
among the assets for £3435. Also there
was a special dividend on Bept. 20 last
probably to pay for some of the shares.
For the period since Sept. 20 the div. on
ordinaries was at the rate of 10 p.c. p.a. At
that rate, the ordinaries and the prefs. (with
£12,000 paid on the 4 ‘B’s”) will call for
about £7150 a year. But as the co. still
owes about £25.000, no doubt more of the
pref. money will be got in. though at 9 p.c.
it is pretty expensive. There certainly is
some patriotism about a co. that will apply
for £5OOO of 44 p.c. War Loan, as this co.
has done, when it is getting in money at
9 p.c. to reduce its liabilities. However,
profits iire good; and if the tariff is made
really Protective, they will continue good;
for there will be a few more tens of thousands
of men back in Australia presently to wear
the co.’s products. And when Australia makes
up its mind that it wants all the work it
can provide for its own people, and conse-
quently settles down to a policy of effective
Protection, it won’t be necessary to offer a
9 p.c. preference in order to get capital.

But don’t attach much weight to the figures
for any single year. The co.’s properties,
Thurulgoona and Bundalear, are away out
in the south-west of Queensland, where
nature goes on dreadful sprees. One year
there is a drought; the next, with a fat
rainfall, there is an excess of everything
except stock. In the 24 years from the
middle of 1913 to the end of 1915 there was
only 164 in. of rain, and most of that was
in little lots that did hardly any good, so that
for 1915 only 4030 lambs were marked.
After that there was no rain worth men-
tioning for another six months, and in 1916,
not only was the lambing a failure, but in
the drought and the early weeks of the rain
23,500 sheep were lost. With the rains came
the blowfly pest; and that and the short
supply of stock have been the past year’s
troubles. At the end of 1913 there were
107,791 sheep on the run; by the end of
1916 the number was down to 49,708. At
Dec. 31 last the tally was 63,014, and with
ordinary luck the end of this year should see
the flocks pretty well restored to the end-
of-1913 figure. It is a wonderful business
this. There is no other in the world, per-
haps, where the man or the co. that can
hang on can make so quick a recovery.
As the profit is to a very considerable
extent governed by the increase or decrease
of stock, a better idea of how the business
has panned out, so far as actual money is
concerned, can be got from this comparison :
1907. 1915. 1916. 1917.
Liabilities.. ..£44,075 £1,031 £1,114 £652
Assets:—•
Livestock .. 79,602 43,740 34,833 38,430
Plant and stores 21,420 7,063 7,163 5,802
Wool . . . . 20,289 10,057 21,027 17,339
Cash, etc. . . 103 33,665 16,635 27,969
Thus in 10 years liabilities have been reduced
by £43,423, while cash, etc. (the etc. is now
War Loan), has been increased by £27,866;
total improvement £71,289. Meantime share-
holders have had £134.000 by way of divi-
dend and bonus. (This includes the 1907
dividend, but not that for 1917. which had
not been paid as at Dec. 31.) This is on a
capital of £292,005. The dividend has thus
averaged 4J p.c. and the improvement before
mentioned is equal to nearly 24 p.c. p.a. more.
That isn’t so bad, especially as throughout
the shares have been at a heavy discount.
There are, however, three huts in this case:
(1) Since 1907 the co. has lost about a
fourth of its holding; (2) the lease of the
other 1018 sq. miles will expire in 1935 or
thereabouts; and (3) though with good
seasons—and on the law of averages a couple
more are due —breeding-up will be rapid, the
holding has still less stock than it had 10
years ago. On the other hand the woollies
are probably worth more; and neither they
nor the clip are likely to be low-priced for
years to come. Because of the reduction of the
holding and the running out of the lease the
directors advise shareholders to regard any-
thing over 34 p.c. they receive as return of
capital; and when a couple more two-
shillings are paid they will probably have
the capital written down from 60s. to 50s.
per share;

Better than this paper figured on 12 months
ago. “If,” it said then, “the co. can aver-
age a net profit of 7d. per lb., which seems
a fair figure to assume, the total for the
current year should be about £1780, which
is equal to just about 8 p.c. on the very
moderate capital of £22,431.” Both the
factors—output and profit per lb.—were
rather better than the estimate: the co.
Siived 04,2S41b. instead of the expected
01,4501b., and the profit was very nearly
It ought to be more by this time; at
any rate, exes, ought to be less. But the
Federated Malay States, are not putting the
bill for everything on to the file; hence,
according to the directors’ statement, the
co. had last year to carry extra taxation
amounting to only a shade under 4d. per
lb. of rubber —equal to very nearly one-
third of the gross profits. Still, if we pay
now we shall not have to pay presently.
There is no dodging the bill altogether.
The estate consists of 808 acres; plant-
ing was commenced in 1010, and this shows
the progress:—
1910 .. 36 ac. 1914 .. 251 ac.
1911 .. 175 „ 1915 .. 21 „
1912 .. 40,, 1916 .. 172 „
1913 .. 69 „ 1917 .. —
So far only the 1010-11-12 trees have been
tapped. It would be possible to pick up a
few in the 69 acres (1913 planting) this
year; but there isn't enough skilled labor
t-> dodge about, and this lot will be left till
next year. Then 820ac. will be tapped, and
there will still remain 444ae. to tap. They
should be returning 2501b. to the acre in
the seventh year. By 1921 the co. ought
therefore to have a nice little harvest. Mean-
time, having planted all its area, it is looking
round for other worlds to conquer, but
hasn’t yet found any in the neighborhood.
The labor trouble is less unsatisfactory than
it at one time promised to be, and appar-
ently it hasn’t been found necessary to per-
severe with a former intention to introduce
Javanese. The other uncertain quantity in
estimating probabilities is the rubber mar-
ket. When the co. was formed the stuff
was worth very nearly 5s. per lb., find had
been more. Last year the average price
was 2s. 1.3d. Singapore; and, though there
has been such an enormous increase in the
use of rubber, the price has been still lower
lately. There is an interesting little story
hanging to that, by the way. The two great
Powers in the rubber-growing Malay Penin-
sula and Archipelago are Britain and Hol-
land ; and the Dutch-controlled production
continued to go to “Continental ports”—
Oh, no, not Germany! However, though
the Hun lied manfully about his famine in
rubber, etc., and though the Censorship
encouraged the English-speaking public to
swallow the Hun’s lies, finally the Allies
were driven to take action to control the
shipment of rubber to “Continental ports.”
As soon as this was done, the old market
being gone, rubber flowed in on Singapore;
and that and the scarcity of shipping to
get it away has made the market very limp
for the time being.

But the figures for any one half-year don’t
tell truly the results of mining operations in
that term. If, for instance, the mine were
closed throughout the current half-year, a
considerable profit would still be shown at
the end of the period. The Sulphide Cor-
poration is the only Broken Hill co. which
does its own smelting. The others now send
their silver-lead concentrates to the Associ-
ated Smelters at Port I’irie —the old Broken
Hill Prop, works which general-manager
Deiprat managed to get rid of by one of the
shrewdest deals ever made in Australia.
The Associated Smelters have contracts with
the mining cos., which receive at once 90 p.c.
of the metal value ; for the purpose of this
preliminary settlement lead is at present
taken at £25 and silver at 2s. The final
settlement is made on the basis of the aver-
age values of the metals in the half-year
succeeding delivery. During the past half-
year lead has been kept steady at about £29,
while silver has been as high as 45,, and is
still over 3s. 9d. Now, in the half-year to
June, 1917, the North mine produced 111,290
tons of ore, yielding in concentrates 14,770
tons lead and 500,4000 z. silver; so there was
a nice little surplus to be collected on that
lot—nearly £5 a ton for the lead and, say,
Is. 6d. for the silver. For the current half-
year the surplus will be much less, because,
mainly owing to the strike which laid the
mine idle for _ ll weeks, only 64,030 tons ore
were produced, containing in concentrates
7414 tons lead and 270,4(X>0z. silver. That,
of course, doesn’t mean that the total profit
for the current half-year will be less than
last term’s, because on present appearances
this term’s output will be much greater.
The profits, despite the strike, are daz-
zling. As mentioned above, the output for
the half-year was 64,030 tons ore. The
profit, after striking off £11,592 for depre-
ciation, and without including some small
reserve-fund accretions, was £209,838. This
was equal to £3 ss. 7d. per ton of ore. Now
go back to the corresponding half of 1913,
the last accounts issued prior to the war.
The profit, computed in the same way for
that half-year, was £134,583, and the output
was 154,829 tons; so this pre-war profit was
17s. 4d. per ton. There you have the war
profit—the difference between 17s. 4d. and
£3 ss. 7d. per ton. Yet, according to the
advices which the cos. have received, this co.
will not have to pay one penny on account
of war profits. The Hughes Government has
done hardly a thing besides talk and pass
this war-profits tax. And that is the sort
of tax it is.
Even 17s. 4d. per ton is above the normal.
Computed over a long course of years the
average Broken Hill profit has been under
15s. per ton; and certainly no more than
that can be relied upon for the future.
Spelter (zinc) is now well over £5O per ton;
a normal price is about £23. The ore re-
serves above the 1400 ft. level are estimated
at 2,750.000 tons. At 14s. that lot is worth
£1.975.000. Besides that, the co. has a sur-
plus of liquid assets as follows :
Dec., 93. June, ’l7. Dec., ’l7.
Assets apart from mine
an<l plant .. ..£234,718 £763,154 £862,504
Liabilities . . . . 25,677 261,669 270,960
Surplus ..£209,041 £501,485 £591,544
These assets include 200,000 shares in the
Associated Smelters, against which the co.
now owes £179,400 of the original £200,000
of debentures. Excluding the share holdings
on the one side and the debentures on the
other, the co. has a liquid surplus of
£558,143. Add that to the £1,975,000 above-
mentioned ; total, £2,533,143, divided among
600.000 shares = £4 4s. per share. But it
will take, say, 10 or 12 years to work out
these reserves and pay the dividends. On
the other hand, the mine doesn’t end at
1400 ft. During the past half-year the main
shaft was deepened to 1624 ft., and prepara-
tions made to drive a level at 1550 ft. The
lode has not been explored at that point;
but at 1400 ft. it is 85ft. wide in section 15,
and though it is an irregular body, a lode
that width isn’t likely to peter out in a few
feet. As to metal contents, take last half-
year’s figures:—
lead Silver Zinc
