So the ore going underfoot is well up to the
average. The zincy tailings from the mill
go to De Bavay’s; but “arrangements have
been entered into with Minerals Separation
and De Bavay Processes Co. for a license to
use its patented processes; but until the
expiration of the agreement with the Amal-
gamated Zinc Co. that license will be limited
to the treatment of slime.” The agreement
referred to runs to April 30 next year.
Occidental, Cobar (N.S.W.), during its
last half-year treated 8018 tons of lO.lGdwt.
ore for a profit of £1022 ; but the position is
not as satisfactory as the profit suggests.
From the beginning of this year the manage-
ment was handed over to J. Weir, and this
is what he says :
Whilst the body of ore is undoubtedly there and
contains payable value, it has been left in such a
way that advantage cannot be taken of the position.
Instead of having the reserve of broken ore that is
absolutely necessary to work large stopes, I found,
when I took charge on January 3, that the mine
was almost stripped of ore, and the struggle now is
to build up this deficiency and keep the battery
going. Mullocking of stopes was also a long way
behind. Development work has been entirely
neglected, and if this policy was persisted in much
longer it would either mean a drain on the share-
holders’ pockets or a suspension of operations. That,
shortly, is the position ; and though it appears gloomy
it has its brighter side... .There is no reason why this
mine, with its large body of ore averaging about
lOdwt., should not with judicious handling be made
to pay.
The same old story; but what were the
directors doing to allow the mine to get into
such a state? And what are the share-
holders doing?
Great Cobar is applying for suspension of
labor conditions on its Cobar (fold and
Chesney leases. Not that it matters very
much to labor, because the Chesney is filled
up with water to 20ft. above No. 2 level, and
the only men employed there Are two or three
who are demolishing the £BO,OOO flotation
plant. The co. is selling odds and ends and
using up parts, etc., to keep the stores
accounts down to bedrock at the Great
Cobar. The Cobar Gold Mine has about four
men underground when there is nothing for
them to do on top, and the water is being
kept out of the property. Shortage of basic
ore for the smelters appears to be the
trouble. But the bailiff and the black cat
are considering things, and may possibly
decide to create a sensation in the near
future. A fine staff is still maintained on
Great Cobar, which continues to look well.
By sluicing 364,000 cub. yds. last half-
year, Cock’s Pioneer, Eldorado (Vic.), did
almost as well as in the previous term, in
spite of floods. Yields showed a nice im-
provement, Id. per cub. yd. more gold being
got and fid. additional tin. During the last
two runs the plant strayed into ground on
the north side of the big paddock, thereby
proving that there is good wash outside the
limits assigned by Moline as the probable
boundary of the pay dirt. That fact makes
Cock’s look a .better market tip than ever
before. Altogether the co. won 17id. per cub.
yd. in tin and gold, against 15.171 d. per cub.
yd. in the previous half-year. But costs
shot up to 94d. from S.9d. in the previous,
term. The merits of Cock’s Pioneer to-day
are that there is more ground up-stream
than is shown on Moline’s plans, and with
shallower and less broken ground costs should
be lighter than heretofore.
* * * *
Stawell (Vic.) has annexed John Couch-
man from Maldon to run Sloane and Scotch-
mans. With costs up because of dear sup-
plies and labor, it didn’t take Couchman long
to quit Sloanes as too poor to pay just
now. Still he talks of getting a payable
go out of the old Magdala within six months.
To do this he will draw solidly on the decent-
grade dirt showing round the 1200 ft. level,
where he has 25,000 tons to play with. Ilis
further intentions are to dig after the stone
bored into ahead of the “M” crosscut and as
soon as possible to unwater the mine to
1500 ft. Then he will hunt after the exten-
sion of the Magdala shoot under 1400 ft. So
Stawell. has dreams of better days.
* * * *
W. L. Baillieu, talking to the fatmen of
the Victorian Leg. Council, says he was one
of the syndicate that brought out the Ger-
man experts before the war to sample Mor-
well brown coal. Thus goes to the wall
the yarn that W.L. hadn’t a finger in the
importing of that clever quartette. W ith
usual skill in picking out the right track,
Baillieu recommends the State to make sure
that big users of current like the Melbourne
City Council and the Melbourne Electric
will take power if it goes to work at
Morwell. Nothing easier, should the Fat
Men agree to make the State the wholesale
supplier, as in Ontario, Canada. That is
not going a step further than the recom-
mendation of the Coal Conservation Com-
mission of Great Britain, of Avhich engineer
Mertz is the chairman. It proposes that
England and Scotland shall be cut up into
16 supply districts, with huge central power-
stations. These are to displace all private
isolated stations, with a saving in coal of
SO million tons annually, besides furnishing
cheaper power than ever was dreamt about.
Such a scheme means industrial revolution.
It is this that drives men like City Electrical-
Engineer Harper and other brown-coal en-
thusiasts into preaching Morwell as Mel-
bourne’s salvation. The issue put by W. L.
Baillieu is either a private monopoly in
electricity or a State one. The Bulletin
knows into whose tender mercies it would
prefer to put the power for the manufac-
turing industry of a State.
* * * *
Chairman T. Iv. Fairthorne put Mount
Bischoff shareholders in a pleasant frame of
mind at the 89th half-yearly meeting by
hinting at a further ss. dividend this month.
Thereupon the retiring directors were re-
elected without opposition, and the directors’
fees were increased from £859 to £675 (the
directors got £750 in the old days when
shares sold for £6O and paid 10s. per month
in divs.). General-manager Millen told them
that everything at the mine was looking well,
the new batteries were doing all that
was expected of them, and incidentally the
timber which had been sold from the sawmill
had practically paid for what had been used
in the mine.
Mt. Bischoff Extended (Tas.) has
struggled for years to make 1 p.c. tin from
narrow lodes pay exes., but now that 1 p.c.
pure metal is worth 60s. the show has a
better chance. So the plant is to be re-
modelled and 10 head of heavy stamps put in.
* * * *
The Queenslander, the only working mine
belonging to Etheridge Gold Mines Ltd.,
Forsayth, has its days numbered. Available
ore will be exhausted in eight months
or so. The reef is not cut out, by any
means, but is strong underfoot; and what
is more, it is the best-defined lode on the
Etheridge field. It is 700 ft. long and aver-
ages 3ft. in width, with loz. gold and a
silver-lead concentrate to help. To sink the
shaft and open up No. 4 level the co. sold
its fine electrical plant, and since then
no development has been undertaken. When
this bit of ore is removed the To-let notice
will be posted up, as the co. has no more
plant to dispose of and cash is its weakness.
Costs per ton of ore milled amount to 625.
7d. Mining the stone costs 265. 2d. per ton,
milling eats another 20s. The balance is
made up of charges for marketing the con-
centrate (11s. 7d.) and transportation of
ore from mine to mill by horse teams (4s.
10d.). The ore is treated at the Havelock
mill, about three miles from the mine. Dur-
ing the wet season the problem is to get
sufficient ore to keep the mill going one
shift.
* * * *
Mt. Cutlibert (Q.) has struck a 6sft. ore
body in the Orphan mine at 180 ft. giving
10 p.c. copper. A few weeks ago a smaller
ore body was struck in the crosscut at the
140 ft. level. The Orphan is near the famous
Dobbin mine arid is connected with the rail-
way.
* * * *
Having paid the second instalment of
£7OO to the vendors, Golden Butterfly (W.A.)
announces that the shaft on the optioned
Blue Streak lease at Tuckabianna is to be
sunk to 150 ft. “in order to prove the mine.”
It is a pity the co. could not have withheld
payment of the instalment until the value of
the show at 150 ft. had been determined, as
some experts who ought to know are very
dubious about the permanency of Tucka-
bianna shoots.
* * * *
Several favorable comments have been
made recently concerning the Eastern-financed
Mount Rankin Co.’s latest acquisition, the
old Day Dawn. Eighteen years ago this
show was taken in hand by a local syndicate,
but at 200 ft. water became troublesome and
refractory ore was struck. This was more
than the syndicate could bear, so it gave in
after spending £12,000. It is alleged that
the sulphides exposed carry payable gold,
and that something might be made out of
the arsenic they contain. But the Mount
Rankin crowd had better go warily.
* * * *
Very little happens at Ivanowna (W.A.)
nowadays, but the few remaining inhabitants
worked themselves into a fever of excitement
a week or two ago over a gold discovery
within the town boundary. A number of
allotments, including one on which the Pres-
byterian Church is built, were pegged, and
according to reports the outcrop can be
traced a long way. But it will take a good
shaking-up to arouse Kanowna.

FOR WOMEN WHO HATE ROUGE.
A TOILET HINT.
Ladies who suffer from pale, sallow, bad
complexions, yet who quite naturally object
to the “loud,” coarse and “made-up” appear-
ance almost inevitably associated with the
ues of rouge—which also, by the way, is
often distinctly harmful to the complexion
—will be interested to learn that they can
easily regain the velvety, child-like fresh-
ness of their complexions by using a simple,
inexpensive lotion composed of 2oz. of rose
water, 1 dram tincture of benzoin and 2oz.
of flowers of oxzoin. Shake well before
applying with a soft cloth or sponge. When
dry brush lightly with a soft cloth or a
piece of chamois leather. If you will do
this whenever you go out you will always
have a soft, natural refined color, of which
your friends cannot be suspicious and _of
which you need not be ashamed. This lotion
is also exceptionally beneficial in relieving
and preventing chapped faces and hands, as
well as redness due to exposure to weather.
—Advt.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, March 11, 1918.
My Dear Arini, —
The Easter show brings the girl-astride
into the helter-skelter over the hurdles, and
there will be hardly a side-saddle in the
whole high-jump kit. There’s still a Women's
Industries section, though it is more of a
weakling than ever since the war came down
like a universe of curses on the crochet quilt
and knobby patchwork cushions. As for the
wax flowers, no one has yet been able to get
Secretary Somer’s honest opinion on the sub-
ject.
Miss Henderson, sister of the commodore
who was in command of Port Jackson for a
while, is now perambulating sec. for the Aus-
tralian Board of Missions. She’s the lady
who couldn’t get a passport to return to
England, and has been hon. sec. to the
sailors’ wives’ social affairs here, with after-
noon parties at the Naval House as fluffy
interludes. If the capable woman orates
with the same telling force for Black Brother
as she does for the Red Cross, that casual
person’s Bible fund will get a great move-up.
Leichhardt Park was a human antbed on
Saturday afternoon. The lads of the village
played cricket on the green—as in the peace-
ful days of old —and a big concourse of other
lads gave their applause. Later on, a khaki
band played a large motor on to the oval, and
the joyful cricketers moved out of the way
to a more secluded side of the park. But the
crowd found time to cheer the long grey car,
built so that a wounded soldier can stretch
out if necessary. This handsome chaise, the
gift of Leichhardt, Rozelle, Haberfield and
other interlocked suburbs, was then handed
over to the Broughton Hall Military Hospi-
tal. Mrs. Laidley represented the Red Cross.
Mayor Blackmore, the local municipal parent,
made a few remarks, and Hoskins, M.L.A.,
gave his benediction to the men who have
fought for us.
At the Town Hall on Saturday evening a
huge audience watched brass-band conductor
Kerry lift his finger for “God Save.’’ Soprano
Lillian Mitchell has brought back from
Britain a pure and well-trained voice, and the
a pure and well-trained voice, and the
frank and boyish manner of a cathedral-
choir angel. But she was very nervous
at this her first appearance in her native
Sydney for nearly four years. And a
husky little imp in her throat—the cold of
the moment—made her less effective in the
operatic snippets than in one or two delight-
ful ballads. Various ushers lent their aid to
pass the flowers—a waggon-load—over the
platform’s edge. Lilian Frost was at the
organ, and a good concert company, includ-
ing Staell and his fiddle, supported the singer
who dared to take the Town Hall in these
war-worn days.
Easter-week races won’t attract Mrs.
Harry Osborne, Lesley Madden and other
well-known members of the family of the
kindly Victorian Chief Justice, John
Madden. His sudden death brings a sheaf
of reminiscences and regrets.
Frank Coffee, of Chatswood, had a boy,
another Frank, who was among the very first
Australians to leap ashore at Gallipoli ; but
at the Evacuation he was lying in the shadow
of a wooden cross. Naturally father wanted
the grave to carry a more permanent mark,
and applied to the Pope, through the Austra-
lian Apostolic Delegate, for help. “To the
most zealous efforts made 1 v the Apostolic
Delegate at Constantinople,” Cardinal Gas-
parri reports, “the answer of the German
naval authorities was as follows: ‘The erec-
tion of a marble cross on the grave of Lieut.
Coffee can only be effected by express order
of the High Command of the Otto-
man Army and by Turk workers or
soldiers.’ ” Then a better idea hit the
Cardinal: as it would be very difficult to
maintain a cross on the grave, even if per-
mission could be got to put it there, why not
have the body x - emoved? The Turk and his
lord the German agreed, and there’s a good
Australian lying now in the R.C. Cemetery
at Constantinople, and a more contented
father waiting in Australia till he can go
and wipe away a tear alongside it.
Before leaving for Maoriland to use that
extra two months’ holiday, the Chief Justice
and his lady gave a chatx-iotic garden party
at Tregoyd—“to meet the Governor and
Lady Davidson.” Therefore all and sundry
who go down to their depots with knitting
or swarm round pyramids of Red Cross
oacking, were summoned to the party.
Mr. and Mrs. Tom Kemp, who bought
Lilianfels, the one-time home of the Darleys,
on the eyebrows of a Katoomba gorge, have
persuaded Governor Davidson to open their
Red Cross fete on Easter Saturday. Lady
Davidson, when she was Miss Feilding, once
spent a few days there with the first owners.
Since then the scenery has acquired a thatch
of tin hats, and mountain cots are plentiful
as blackberries.
Our new sub-regal lady generally wears a
diamond cluster ear-ring in each pink lobe—
nothing else, as a rule, in the way of gems
for daytime functions. In spite of the short-
age in dyer’s delicate colors, she manages to
wear shoes and hose in the exact shade of
her favorite grey gowns.
Lieut. Norman McKeown, actor, author
and soldier, lately married his ex-command-
ing officer’s widow. Another son of the
Waverley parson has been invalided home.
Norman —he wrote “The Muck Rake”—finds
time between shells to still turn out a little
light literature.
On Friday afternoon Excellency Helen
presides over a big meeting that’s summoned
to Her Majesty’s Theatre. The object is to
reach woman’s ear in the interests of Red
Cross Day.
Danger Owen, K.C., and daughter Gladys
are back from Maoriland after a few weeks’
rest from their Red Cross and recruiting
sacrifices.
Governor Davidson had a look at the
Miller’s Point Free Kindergarten and play-
ground the other day. The playground—the
land was given by the Harbor Trust —is an
oasis in a desert of bricks. To Mrs.
Francis Anderson, wife of the Uni. profes-
sor, goes most of the applause when there’s
a mention of these lungs for the babies of
the crowded places —her hobby for five-and-
twenty years.
For the Titbits show in the Repertory
on March 30 and April 1 one fascinating
young matron has already sold 80 guinea-
tickets. Society is ambushed behind the
affair, which is to raise funds for the Light
Horse and Camel Corps boys in the desert.
The Women’s Hospital (Crown-street)
having got a recent lift has acquired adjacent
premises called “Hillerest.” This will give
temporary shelter to some of the nurses.
Later, when the Nightingales get into decent
quarters of their own, the waiting patients
will be domiciled in this building. There’s
to be a general meeting to wash up the
report and balance-sheet with tea and music
on March 22.
The Journalists give their first 1918 lunch
at Farmer’s this week. Our New-found-
(land) Excellency will be the guest.
Save your fat! Kitchen fat, I mean. The
Red Cross pickle factory is now a dripping
depot. Here Mrs. Joscelyn and her V.A.D.
girls are rendering down the dripping that
goes to our half-starved prisoners-of-war in
lib. tins. The Commissioner’s abroad now
advise smaller tins so as to spread the sus-
tenance over a larger surface.
John Longstaff will
be well represented
at the coming Loan
Exhibition. Happily
Sydney possesses in
perpetuity the fam-
ous Victoria n’s
“Lady in Black.”
whose serene face
and haunting eyes
arrest the gaze of the
most casual visitor
to the Australian
court. There are
many great portraits
besides, notably that
of Henry Lawson;
for Longstaff, whose
precocious genius won
the first Victorian
travelling scholar-
ship with “Breaking, the News,” has been
twice in Australia since Europe claimed
his brush. During the earlier visit he
shared Sid Long’s studio in Sydney. Here
he slapped on the gorgeous color in Chief
Justice Frederick Darley’s portrait. His
last visit was less satisfactory to the artist,
though it filled the business man’s pocket-
book with cheques. He worked at the high
speed of one who had so many acres of
canvas to cover before the short winter days
closed in. Commissions poured upon him.
He painted social celebrities —the artificial
elegance of Lady Dudley, turned obediently
to catch the right light as John in his
butcher-blue overall (which he grew to hate
as part of the rush) visualised the lady’s
delicate aquiline nose and chocolate-brown
eyes. He painted Prof. Andy Stuart’s por-
trait, gay as any macaw in plumage of stiff
red and blue silk and that added another
picture to the National Gallery’s Australian
court.
Mrs. Aldous C. Arnold, a Sydney woman
whose husband commands the Australian
Camel Field Ambulance, is doing war work
in England. She’s a sister attached to
Queen Mary’s Hospital for Armless and Leg-
less Soldiers and Sailors —a billet that needs
all the Australian sunshine one of its daugh-
ters can infuse.
The cards are out for Nancy Chisholm’s
wedding. The happy soldier is Ivan Young,
lieut., not so long home from the Front. It’s
to be on March 20. Nancy is one of the
Goulburn sisters who have been amongst the
city’s most persistent Red Cross workers.
The Australian V.A.D.'s who left here 18
months or more ago—-Enid Armstrong and
Adele Brennan were with those white aprons
—recently got a Red Stripe of Honor apiece.
This badge of efficiency is the British Mili-
tary Hospital’s recognition of devotion to
duty. They’ve been attached to a haven for
wounded in Leicestershire.
Here is a little
photo, of a busy man
who makes a recrea-
tion of writing books
about Australian
timber. It. T. Baker
is Curator and Eco-
nomic Botanist at
the Sydney Tech.
Museum, and has a
crowded lair in a
dusty purlieu of
Ultimo, where he
does simply tremen-
dous things not only
for economic botany
but for Australia.
After 25 years he has
almost got us to be-
lieve that our forests
are fit for soniething
besides the firestick. “In hardwoods we can
beat America,” he says, tenderly handling a
specimen in the big timber court; “and for
tannin, thanks to the black pine, we are
independent of the world. The essential oils
and other qualities of the eucalypts of East-
ern Australia, embracing about three-fourths
of our big vegetation, make this genus of
plants the greatest forestry asset of the
Commonwealth. When the average Aus-
tralian learns to see a towering gum with-
out itching for an axe to chop it down, he
will have become a true lover of his country.”
An authority jOn timbers, the Gov.-General,
is making a collection of Australian woods,
and when in Sydney the Tech. Museum is
one of his haunts. When the war eame,
Germany, looking (as usual) ahead, declared
pit-props contraband. This was a blow aimed
at Britain’s coal-mines. Soon the shortage
showed signs of becoming acute, and many
British timber plantations gave up their splen-
dor. Amongst the biggest hauls was that
which carried a cheque for £50,000 to Sir
Ronald Ferguson. Yet the forest of Douglas
firs that brought this sum was a stony Scot-
tish hillside 40 years ago.
Lady Galway’s son, Lieut. d’Erlanger, is
due in Sydney. He was invalided home to
mother in S. Aus., got better and is shortly
off to have another whack at Fritz:., This
time he’s going to drop souvenirs on that
furriner’s thick head.
Little Madeline Clarke, the Brisbane press-
girl who went to China and Japan and
thereabouts with the Freddy Shipman vaude-
ville eo., is home again. She’s in town for
a day or two before going back to Queens-
land. Madeline, who is five-feet-notMng in
her .dippers, was the first woman to “go
ahead,” alone, in China and Japan'. “The
East” opened its almond eyes a trifle wider
when the small bundle of energy arrived in
out-of-the-way towns and started to get things
ready. “But the kindness of everyone (I
loved my job, mind, and never knew nervous-
ness) and the special courtesy of British
consuls —why, I couldn’t tell the tale in a
week,” enthuses Madeline. Mrs. Shipman, a
devoted daughter, got a cable from Chicago
to hurry her home owing to her father’s ill-
ness. So she had to leave us at Shanghai.”
The company went where it listed at the end
of the contract. Madeline booked for home
and mother, but stuck for about nine days
on a coral reef ISO miles north of Cooktown.
After being hauled off, the steamer met the
full blast of the Townsville tropichl disturb-
ance, and did about tw T o knots an hour? But
all’s well now.
There’s a new hon. matron at Rose Hall
convalescent home for sojers. Miss Fox, its
first and only chieftainess hitherto, gives up
after a long spell of hard work. The just-
appointed head is Miss Preston.
Home on short furlough, Flight-lieu t.
Kingsford Smith, a Mosman boy, ,only 20.
owning a Military Cross and a foot-wound.
Young Smith saw Gallipoli as a private, but
the air service caught his fancy, and he now
has wings in the R.F.C. A brother is secre-
tary to the O.C. of Garden Island. ,
About two years ago Dr. Charles Lloyd,
an English musician, came this way as
examiner for the Royal Academy, jgince
then a brilliant Sydney pianist, F. S, Kelly,
has been killed in action. Lloyd, who was.
as precentor at a big English school, in touch
with the Australian’s boyish aspirations in
music, has written an Elegy to his
memory. This was the “special” played by
Ernest Truman at a Town Hall organ reci-
tal the other afternoon.
Billjim’s younger brothers, Australia’s
first batch of midshipmen, are now well
established with the Grand Fleet. They are
split up amongst five ships, and most of
them have been chosen for special duty,
while one has already kept his last watch.
They don’t like the North Sea winter, but
revel in their work. One of them, follow-
ing a period of mine-sweeping, went to Lon-
don and voyaged across the Big Smoke
many times by balloon during his aerial
observation course. He was subsequently
sent to the Lucia, a submarine training-ship,
and describes the work as “priceless.”
The success of girls in the “hardier” sub-
jects at the recent Sydney leaving certifi-
cate exam, has caused a rustle in the high-
schools. At botany 16 girls took all the
honors; in geology four girls had the only
passes; at zoology three girls took the
honors; and one youth who shared honors
with four gii’ls in physiology was a novelty.
And there is a girleen who took honors in a
country exam, recently in agriculture and
farm work !
A Sayidali Revue—it catches its title from
the Arab greeting—is part of the April cam-
paign for the Red Cross. It is booked for
the King’s Cross Theatre, Darlinghurst, on
Tuesday in Easter Week. Minnie Everett
is hon. adviser to the amateurs who are to
do the Sayidahing; and Mrs. Ashton, Helen
Macarthur Onslow and Anthony Street are
the hon. secs.
Prawn parties are local sassiety’s latest
recreation. There was one at Rose Bay a
little while ago, when our loveliest got into
glorified surf-costume and waded into the
water that lapped the foot of the garden.
Hefty young “sports”—carrying lanterns—
also stemmed the current with their brawny
limbs. A fair haul was the result. The
prawners then retired to cook the little shell-
backs as part of the entertainment. Some-
one inadvertently blew up the bath-heater in
the course of warming-up after the chill sea-
weedy wade.
“Pompoms everywhere,” says an ecstatic
Australian overseas, after glueing her fea-
tures to London shop-windows. This means,
of course, the fluffy knobs on the winter hats,
and the same as a finish to the new coat-
mantles. But may the plump be preserved
from the folly of a pompom on their expan-
sive corsages!
“Newfoundland” still rolls off the Gover-
nor’s tongue if he isn’t watching himself,
and “this great State of New—found-land”
came tripping along in one of his Ex.’s
recent Hospital speeches. (Jiggles brought
a hurried New —South Wales to the rescue.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

GOOD DIGESTION WITHOUT
MEDICINE.
UNIVERSAL ADOPTION OF ANTACID
THEORY.
In the old days pain in the stomach after
eating or inability to retain food, variously
known as dyspepsia, indigestion or gastritis,
called for the taking of powerful pain-killing
drugs or useless and expensive artificial
digestants; the usual result being big medi-
cine bills and an irreparably damaged
stomach and intestinal tract. Modern en-
lightened research has changed all this. It
has shown conclusively that acid and fer-
mentation, which irritate and inflame the
delicate stomach lining, is the direct cause
of most of the trouble. Specialists therefore
nowadays, instead of giving medicines and
drugs, follow the more logical plan of neu-
tralising the dangerous acid by taking imme-
diately after eating, in a little water, half a
teaspoonful of a form of magnesia prepared
especially for stomach use, and known among
chemists as bisurated magnesia. This
quickly neutralises the acid, stops food fer-
mentation, and makes the food contents
bland and sweet, thus permitting the stomach
to proceed with its work without hindrance
and under normal conditions. Try it and
see for yourself, but be sure that you get the
genuine bisurated magnesia.—Advt.

Melbourne, March 31, 1918.
My Dear Yabrie, —
The old Chief Justice went as he would
have wished. There were no long farewells,
no moaning at the bar. In the midst of a
game with the children—he was in some
ways still a child at 1 4—the Messenger
called. John Madden died practically with
little baby Jowett on his knee.
Before leaving for Sydney, the stormy
Mannix, quieter than a lamb these balmy days
of early autumn, has been spending part of
his three months’ holiday at Queenscliff.
There he distinguished himself from other
visiting sky-pilots by invariably wearing his
canonicals, in season and out of season, even
when right amongst the neck-to-knee contin-
gent on the beaches. Somebody asked the
archiepiscopal bodyguard if his Gracious
wouldn’t like to tear ’em off now and again
and take a dip. “Ah, no,” was the solemn
reply, “the Archbishop never bathes.”
Mrs. Merson Cooper and Mrs. Barry
Thompson did a little preliminary bushrang-
ing at Menzies’ last Thursday for Our Boys’
Day. They sold dowers and kewpies at
Kelly Gang prices to. visiting racing men.
Please drop a line to the anonymous sender
of the dowers and thank him (or her) for
his (or her) beautiful donation. Sam Hor-
dern, Captain Waters and the Bruntons were
easy marks; and Sam Mackay, from West-
ralia, dung down a fiver for a tiny bottle of
scent.
The Stock Exchange is usually invaded by
pretty bushrangers on patriotic days. This
time, for Our Boys’ Day, ’Change means to
take a hand itself. ’Change members, with
Mrs. ’Change helping, will have a kiosk out-
side and a theatrical entertainment indoors.
The principal stars have promised to shine.
Mrs. Bail lieu and Mrs. Fred Knight will
supervise the kiosk.
The last night of the Whittington panto,
went off with a fizz and a bang for a packed
house. Bob Whiting, with his diminutive
spouse, and Rupert Clarke had a pen. The
Geoff. Symes, with a representative collec-
tion from their nursery, snuggled down in
the opposite box. The aftermath of the Nan
O’Hara-liill wedding wandered in about
half-time, and scattered through the
house were Mrs. Norman Ealkiner, Mrs.
John Osborne and Mavis Thiel, Gordon
Chirnside’s fiancee. Plump and debonair
Eeonore Eddington had a khaki escort.
Maggie Dickinson’s “Cheer Oh” stunt evoked
whirlwinds, and the cast came in for much
cheering. The usual floral finale eventu-
ated, and the cost of the vegetables would
have stocked a Y.M.C.A. hut for soldiers.
Vera Pearce stood in a lily-starred doorway,
with “Our Vera” plastered on it. Maggie
Dickinson and Sidney Yates scored two
kewpies dressed a la vogue, whilst other
bouquet recipients tried for that “didn’t-
know-it-was-coming” expression—and failed
badly.
A social tragedy of last week was the
death of Dr. Cairns Lloyd’s young and
pretty wife, who collapsed on the operating
table. She was a daughter of late wealthy
woolgrower Scott, who formerly owned the
William Leonards’ Toorak home. After
their parents’ death the two younger Scott
chicks went to London to live with a
married sister. The elder of the pair re-
turned a few' years ago as the bride of Dr.
Cairns Lloyd, who is a nephew of Mrs.
Robert Harper. She leaves a small son and
daughter.
Mrs. William Officer passed out very sud-
denly at her husband’s squattage the other
Saturday. She had been in bad health for
years and died in her sleep. The deceased
lady, who, in the ’Bo’s was famed for her
beautiful figure, was a sister of V.R.C.
starter Norman Wilson. She leaves two
sons. One married shortly before war broke
out, and the other wears a reject badge.
The youngest of the late Admiral Bridges’s
chicks has been tied up in Blighty with a
Canadian, Colonel Wilson. The Bridges
widow was a Wilson, but daughter Marjorie’s
soldier belongs to a different tribe. The
bride is dark, pretty and well-dollared. Her
wedding happened just a few weeks after
sister Alfreda changed her name to Share.
Nan O’Hara was altared to Mrs. Hill at
Christ Church last week. She walked through
her part in a long-trained white satin gown
trimmed with silver and lace, and sister
Betty wore a veil over her bridesmaid's
frock. Only relations were invited—but the
O’Hara relations are legion. Elder sister
Mrs. Rowan (she is a large likeness of Clara
Butt) was a prominent help in the cere-
monies. Big bluff surgeon dad wept tem-
peramental tears at parting with one of his
colleens. The bride and ’groom got away
quietly and the wedding party went out en
masse to the races, where they lunched and
afternoon-teaed merrily, and created a sensa-
tion at one of the theatres in the evening.
Pretty little Nan’s man is a Bananalander.
His station is adjacent to sister Mrs.
Rowan’s place, so the Melbourne girl will
not be lonely.
Gwen Warnock is now Mrs. Wharton, of
Bananaland. She was married in the chapel
of the English Grammar School on the wet
(sth to her returned soldier. Eieut. Wharton
had another returned soldier, Captain Fusel,
as groomsman, and 20 more returned men
formed a guard of honor for his bride. They
made an arch of their crossed swords (ail
the swords had been carried to battle, if not
actually used). The bride was the prettiest
thing—dark as a gipsy. She wore a soft
Georgette dress of a very faint pink tint,
pleated in flat straight pleats into small
wreaths of embroidered baby-roses; also a
big Gainsboro’ hat. (The wedding was
at eight in the evening, but that old
fashion-note of St. Paul’s about women’s
covered heads has never been repealed.)
Sister Leila Warnock, in a duck of a dress
all pinks and blues, and a big black hat, was
the only bridesmaid. The church was full of
khaki.
Mrs. M. M. Phil-
lips’s novel, “The
White Feather,” is
making a hit. The
scene is laid in Mel-
bourne and suburbs
—a territory which
the writer has good
reason to know, since
her grandfather got
in by the early door,
and her father,
Angel Ellis, was
bo r n here. Angel
Ellis died about a

Mrs. Phillips has been writing short-stories
for years and getting them published under
the name of “Philip Ray,” and The Bul-
letin, Lone Hand and Australasian have
all written their favorable criticisms of her
work on cheque forms. Her sister, Ada Ellis,
a talented woman who wrote much, was one
of the first Melbourne girls to get her Uni-
versity degree. “The White Feather” is
Mrs. Phillips’s first effort at a long story.
An attack of appendicitis was followed by
weeks of convalescence on a verandah, and
the energetic woman employed the enforced
rest in finishing her big work. Her husband,
to whom the book is dedicated, is chief clerk
and taxing-master of the Supreme Court, and
in his spare time a well-known figure in
Shakspearean circles and literary rings and
combines.
It is a good job there is some use for
swords. Until the other day (and I suppose
it is so still) every Australian officer was
supplied with a blade—and the thing with
its fixings must cost three or four pounds.
Of course nobody nowadays ever employs a
sword in action ; the use of the encumbrance
is purely ceremonial, and for that a man
can always borrow. So one of the first
things the non-swanking Australian officer
did when he got to Egypt in the old days
was to mislay his sword. You could find the
wretched things hidden under carpets and
buried in the sand. That’s where some of
the £200,000,000-odd which the war has cost
us has gone.
Madam Adelaida’s inset:—
The Auditor-General’s overhaul of the Repatria-
tion Fund accounts brings to light the fact that the
Returned Soldiers’ Toy Factory, which cost £9OO to
establish, dropped £538 between October, 1916, and
the following June. If somebody would now deter-
mine where the work of the South Aus. Soldiers’ Fund
ends and that of the Repatriation Fund begins, he
might help the cause of economy by eliminating
overlapping.
Frederick Halcomb, M.A., clerk of the Leg. Coun-
cil, is about to retire. He is 82, and he and good
health have lately not been on the best of terms.
His successor, J. C. Morphett, is 74. Alfred Searcy,
64, and J. P. Morice, 61, both take a step up. There
is no “too old at 40” about the S.A. Parliamentary
officials.
Greece has still her stalwarts. Behold Demetrius
Emmanuel! In Hindley-street on Saturday night he
attacked a fellow countryman’s fish-shop with a limb
torn off a tree, shattered both windows, battered the
fish to pulp, and, when arrested, valiantly fought
eight policemen more or less successfully from Hind-
ley-street to the watch-house. Horatius would have
made things even more willing at the bridge if the
good Demetrius had stood by his right hand.
Peter Dawson has announced a farewell concert
about Easter, and his friends and relations are pre-
paring a reception that will be a theme for the
oldest inhabitant in time to come. Peter was born
in Adelaide, and in his young days was more noted
for divilment than voice. Still, even as a very small
youngster he was in St. Andrew’s choir. You know
he is going into camp very soon. Though he has
been a star for a dozen years he is still on the right
side of 36.
To the Catholic Federation on Wednesday Arch-
bishop Spence, referring to Dr. Mannix, said that
“they might travel not only the whole of Australia
but the whole of the wide world and not meet a
more peaceable man.” And his Grace never batted
an eye, either.
After a four years’ hiatus the Royal Agricultural
and Horticultural Society is holding a two-days’ Show
in exceptionally fine weather. The town, is full of
Mallee cousins, and the shops are reaping harvest.
Frederick Young, our retiring Agent-General, has
joined the London Board of the Bank of Adelaide.
Perhaps London fogs suit his rheumatism.
The hotelkeepers of South Aus. have presented the
V.A.D.’s with a cinematograph plant for use in the
large shed at the Outer Harbor. The Film Supply
Co. will provide films, and Wondergraph and West’s
will detail operators. It is a little compliment
(costing £3OO, by the way) that the V.A.D.’s well
deserve, their work being too little appreciated.
H. S. Power, who did all his early work in
Adelaide, and has a horse-picture in the Gallery there,
has also a battle-painting on its walls just now, lent
by Surgeon-Major Bollen. It shows an Australian
advanced dressing-station on the Somme, all plastered
earth (rather roughly painted) in the foreground,
and battle-smoke at the back. . Heavyweight men,
with Lindsay faces, are giving or receiving attention,
and German prisoners file out of a trench bearing
more wounded. With unusual restraint, Power has
allowed himself only one horse, and naturally it is
the best thing in the crowded picture.
Major Jack Dyer is now in command of
the A.I.F. Engineers’ Depot, at Brittlingsea,
Essex. At the end of 1917 he received his
D.S.O. and gave a diamond ring to a pretty
English girl who hails from Northampton.
Mervyn Forrest, son of David and nephew
of Bigjohn, is on honeymoon in Melbourne
and has promenaded with his bride »t Flein-
ington. He married Madge Corthope, a
Westralian lassie. They have already
'mooned in the Apple Island and Sydney,
and are now returliing west.
There was great to-do over a christening
at St. Pat’s last week. Captain and Mrs.
Forrest’s first-born appeared in an all-lace
robe and cap made for him by the nuns of
the Abbostford; (Convent. Grandmother Mrs.
Forrest, from Charters Towers, was a proud
dapie. She was one of the recognised beau-
ties of her day when she came from Ireland
to marry the Bananaland doctor, and she has
not lost her good looks. She stood god-
mother to her grandson, and grandfather
Michael Monaine was godfather.
Mrs. Harold Russell came over from Syd-
ney for the races, and suffered an appalling
loss during the journey. Her luggage dis-
appeared, and she had to rush round Mel-
bourne and buy ready-made clothes. A real
tragedy when you see the way the Sydney
women dress to kill their Melbourne sisters
with envy. The late John Downer’s dame,
who was one of the Russell girls, is over for
all events from Adelaide.
The deferred Australian Cup, washed off
its sacred Tuesday by oceans of rain, was
run in perfect weather on Thursday. Pal-
freyman (he owns, or partly owns, Defence
and races under a nom-de- Flemington) was
in high fettle, and became the willing victim
of the women who were bushranging for Our
Boys’ Day. His donations were quite regal.
The Downer lady (Adelaide) was out, and
Mrs. Harold Russell, from Sydney, looked
charming in pale-green duds, in spite of lost
luggage. Young soldier Lewis was there
without mother, though Elliott Lewis is over
from the Apple Island for the races. Mrs.
Sam Mackay, from the West, looked about
18 in a soft dark dress and a big shady hat.
The Motts, from Bananaland, were with a
big batch of men from Cloncurry way. Squat-
ter Dick (six-foot-six in his socks, but, of
course, he had his boots on) excitedly dis-
cussed with them the results of a Meat Price
deputation he had led that morning to the
Minister. Cattle Kidman, solemn and grey,
genially distributed prize bullocks to busy
workers for Our Boys’ Day. Mrs. Pohlman
got one. Mrs. Burragh pocketed another, and
Mrs. Rogers Tompson collected a third.
Racing Shepherd’s daughter was with Mrs.
Sternberg. Muriel Forrest North chanced a
cold and a new cloth costume, and went
without Auntie and Uncle Bigjohn. Dr. and
Mrs. Osborne were over from Sydney. Sister
Mrs. Wilkinson wandered over the lawn in a
Glory Quayle sort of dress of blue stocking-
ette in the new “jumper” style.
Mrs. Waters, from Sydney 5 strolled in the
simple glorv of a white Parisian gown, and
wore her famous necklace of pearls. The
gaud has 68 peas of special splendor and
lustre. The beautiful thing is a temptation
to every jewel thief in Australia and the
envy of all women. Hubby is a pearl enthu-
siast and has gathered the gems for his lady
because she scorns other jewels. No other
woman caught so many eyes on the lawn.
Stewart Brunton was with his wife in white
cloth. The frocky Denison lady returned to
Sydney before the postponed race could be
run.
Flemington was crowded for the third
afternoon of the meeting. It was a Satui’day
holiday crowd, dowdy and domesticated.
Children were sprinkled about the fashion-
able grandstands, whei-e they munched fruit
and biscuits and made all tilings feel like a
glue-factory. Even the size barred in
theatre advertisements wei’e there, woollen
shawls, squalls and all.
The fashion abroad kept mostly to the
saddliug-paddock. Mrs. Danvers (W.A.)
carried an acreage of eye-veiling tulle round
her neck and wore an embroidei’ed tunic di’ess
and a hat topped with dyed emu feathers.
Mrs. Lowry stewed gently in a duck of an
ermine cape, while her husband’s Desert
Cold gave a flying exhibition in the Gover-
nor’s Plate. Mrs. Fi-ed Thiel wore brown to
match her eyes. Daughter Mavis, fair and
debonair in chintz and blue print, accepted
congratulations on her engagement to Gordon
Chirnside, who is away in France. Mrs.
Geoffrey Syxne was a cool picture in pale
pastel ninons. Mrs. Aubrey Campbell was
in the much-favored chocolate color. Mrs.
Mills wdi-e a bright-green hat on top of a
fawn dress; but Mrs. Jack Wallace had a
paddy-green hat that was even brighter.
She backed Wallace Isinglass in silk stock-
ings of the Wallace plaid. Mrs. Waters, fi-om
Sydney, was a vision in heavy blue serge
tailorings and a glorified Tam-o’-shanter with
a pink-purple thistle embroidered in wool
clamped on to her golden hair. The vice-
regal pen was handed over to disabled khaki.
Mrs. Leslie Stuart’s dance on Australian
Cup night was the sensation of race week.
It began as an informal “telephone” frivol
for young people just to say good-bye to
Char Crespin and Bill Baillieu, who are
going back to the trenches after a spell.
Every girl who didn't get an invitation was
pouting all through the races.
Ida Rentoul Outhwaite’s show of work
was due to begin on Tuesday. Black-and-
white and colored drawings are in the bill.
Ida was responsible for the designs of the
Butterfly Ballet in “Dick Whittington.”
Yours sincerely.

THE TOP NOTCH SCOTCH
A SAFE PROTECTOR

ALITIES
“6262”: This is the way to get a good
edge on an old blunt razor. Open it and attach one
end of a piece of string about 3ft.. long to the heel
of the blade in such a manner that it will balance
horizontally with the cutting edge downwards. Tie
the other end of the string to your gas bracket, or
to the ridge-pole of your tent, and let the razor
hang there undisturbed. If not influenced by wind
or by the twist of the string (which can easily be
teased out) it will come to rest finally with the
blade pointing to the magnetic north. A mass of
metal near it may, however, deflect it. Leave it for
two or three days, and it will then be ready for
comfortable use without stropping.

“Tarlton II.”: Nobody has yet explained
clearly to a recent inquiring novice how to distin-
guish the male magpie from the female during
infancy. So here’s the dinkum recipe. Observe the
shade of grey on the back and the shape of the
bill. The male is darker until he survives the moult,
when 12 months old ; the back then becomes white.
The female is lighter-colored, with a white edge to
the grey behind the head, but the back never alters.
Both have dark bills for the first five months, then
that of the male pales rapidly and at nine months
ia white with a blue-black tip. The female cannot
blanch her bill under 12 months. In section the
male’s picker is oblong, whereas the female’s bill
is almost square. Even when hungry Jack makes less
noise than Jill.
“87": Be “Billy Blue” (B. 18/10/T7) and
Ills Bullocky Bill with the box of tampered brandy.
I know a similar case. When my unit left the Nile
and pushed into the heart of the desert to catch the
Senussi army in the rear, I was left behind as
C.Q.M.S. A week later two men came back by
train (a narrow-gauge railway probed the desert for
70 miles) with a letter from my 0.0. instructing me
to give the bearers two cases of whisky. The men
returned with the medicine the next morning, but
the train was slow and the thirst great, and the
Billjims helped themselves to a bottle out of one of
the cases. Instead of pulling out the cork in the
usual way, they broke the bottle over a billycan and
replaced the broken glass in its straw envelope.
Another bottle was treated in a similar manner.
Later on water was poured over the straw. At the
journey’s end Bill explained that owing to the rock-
ing of the train a case had fallen overboard and
the contents were, he was afraid, badly damaged.
The officer was probably thankful for what rem lined.
* * * *
“F.Y.7”: That yarn about the swaggie
who was treed with his dog for four days by the
Rockhampton flood recalls the case of Mrs. Flynn, of
Donga mail change, Southern Queensland. When the
Balonne and the Warrcgo come down together a great
dampness is apt to prevail in that part of the
country. Recognising this, when the waters first
put in an appearance in ’93, Mis. Flynn, who was a
very large and capable Irishwoman, took to a tree
which had previously been well provisioned. The
domestic live-stock, including two rosella parrots, two
small black pigs, an odd lot of fowls and Mr. Flynn,
were also installed in the tree, which was a big,
straggling coolabah, and there they all remained for
nearly 10 days until the waters subsided. The missus
rather enjoyed it, and used to say that but for the
trouble given her by the pigs and Mr. Flynn, who
were continually complaining; she wouldn’t mind
another holiday just like it.
“G. Arden Hill” ;• Plagues of mice, grass-
hoppers, weevils, locusts, etc., are common enough
in Australia, but a plague of bees is unusual. South
Coast (N.S.W.) cordial manufacturers have lately
been complaining bitterly of the depletion of their
syrup stocks owing to the depredation of such insects.
Nor is this all. A Wollongong cordial manufacturer
declares that he has suffered a serious loss of trade
owing to 11 of his employees having been incapaci-
tated at times through stings. He advances the
theory that the exceptionally heavy rains on the
coast (25in. fell during January and February)
have washed the honey out of the blossoms, and the
bees have no alternative but to raid his premises in
order to replenish their tanks.

“Griff”: Add another to the list of cures
for tick-fever in eats and dogs. Give the sufferer
a drench of a teaspoonful of painkiller in milk
when he is seen to be getting weak in the hind-
quarters, not forgetting, of course, to remove the
ticks. Two or three doses of this will set him up.
I have cured not only cats and dogs with this
remedy, but on several occasions foals as old as 12
months. As with griped horses the battle is prac-
tically over when the pain is beaten.
“Steve Hart”: Now that some Billjims
are getting on to the land I’m doing a good turn
by introducing. them to the tree lucerne as the best
utility scrub to grow. It is ornamental anywhere;
flowers profusely in the winter, providing bees with
honey when they want it most; the flowers drop
millions of seedpods, which provide the fowls with
a good egg-producing food; and at any time you can
take the axe to it and give Cherry and Brindle or
the jumbucks a succulent fodder, which they eat
greedily and do well on. It grows high enough to
give a good shade, and will sprout anywhere, except-
ing in a wet situation. It grows very quickly.
Seeds are cheap and require boiling water poured
over them, then left soaking for 12 hours. Plant
in flower-pots, jam-tins or any old thing from which
they can be transplanted to permanent quarters.
* * * *
“Church Street”: Swans and ducks are
following the example of the human race in Vic.,
and leaving the country for the city. Swans fish
for their living among the motor-boats and smells
in the Yarra, and Richmond goes shooting swans
and ducks in the quarry-holes. It’s a dangerous
sport, too; one citizen who had shot a swan in a
quarry-hole at South Richmond was drowned while
he was trying to get it out the other day.
* ' * * *
“Douglas T.” : While trying to coax a fin
out of the river at Barham (N.S.W.) I noticed, in
a red-gum, a symmetrical-looking nest. I wanted
to shin up that old gum at once, but climbing is a
lost art at 80. Next morning I ascertained it was
a magpie’s nest, made almost entirely of the thin
wire used in tying up sheaves of hay. But to get
back to fish. It is strange that the Fisheries" De-
partment does not give greater encouragement to the
destruction of cormorants. These pests consume
five full meals every 24 hours, and in these times
when everything eatable is of importance it seems
criminal to let the gluttonous divers get so much.
A party of five men on the Wakool (an outflow «f
the Murray) seme time ago killed nearly 1000 in
trees where they had made nests. A small
allowance is, I believe, made by the Department, but
it is not enough to tempt anyone to waste time and
cartridges. I’ll tell you about the fish later on.
“Konini” : Flies have a sense of color, and
in my experience they damn blue. A friend of mine
in Taranaki (M.L.), who lives for his cows, dis-
covered that in one shed which had a decorative
scheme in blue the madames' were not fly-pestered.
So he painted all the sheds with this solution: 20gals.
water, 101 b. slacked lime and lib. of ultramarine.
That, lie declares, settled the fly curse.
“Ritasun” : How many young ones does a
black snake have at a birth ? While fishing in the
Big Trickle the other day an Elmore (Vic.) returned
soldier killed-, a lady of the species who had corked
up 45 snakelets, one of them with two heads. The
freak is being sent to the Bendigo Museum, in Billy
Hughes's constituency. Freaks are a great institution
with the Bendigo people.
“Bad Australian”: The ignorant South
American is doing his level best to encourage the
growth of the very tree that the scrub Australian
cockatoo regards as a curse and impediment to pro-
gress. It is only about 20 years since the euca-

“Hard Up, Esq.” : Add this to the collec-
tion. In front of the Foresters’ Hall, Broadmeadows
(Vic.), is a hollow iron lamp-post with a small hole
about 3ft. from the ground. Five months ago a
swarm of bees took possession of the cylinder, and
have since been perspiring to get together a shipment
of honey. Also, under the flooring of the local police-
station a tribe of bees have hived for some years.

JIM AND BILL ARE OUT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I wanter tell yeh, if yi 11 wait,
Jist wot I think iv Nipper Jim.
’E is a cow. Yiv got it straight.
Fair cow ’e is. I’ve done wif im.
Now if be any charqce we meet
Around the crib or down the street,
It’s brusher, see. No more up ’ere
I drinks his fags or smokes his beer.
’E done me crool, ’n’ there yeh are.
’N’ we was cobbers seven ye’r.
Jim hooks me girl frim Annie's bar,
’N’ does a quiet guy wif ’er.
Now, there’s a trick t’ play a lad!
’N’ at the Stajum we ’ave ’ad
A score iv battles, ’n’ when fit
They’d swarm to see us dongin’ it.
In twenty battles lost ’n’ won
’E’s often pasted me that ’ot
Mum didn’t know ’er fav’rit’ son.
I give ’im that thick ear ’e’s got.
The ’igh Tnnp on his nose ’e owes
T’ me. ’N’ then, strike, ’ere ’e goes
’N’ swipes me only bit iv skirt.
Say wot yer damwell like, that’s dirt!
Her eyes is black ez tar, her sniff
Is twisted pretty. Ah, well, bloke,
It sorter feels t’ me ez if
I’m down ’n’ out. Me ’eart is broke.
I’ll meet ’er yet, ’n’ when I do
You bet your sweet she'll stop a few ;
But ez fer ’im, I tells yeh plain,
I'll never fight the swine again!
VICTORIA.
E. DYSON.

DON’T DRUG YOUR NERVES.
IF THEY ARE WEAK TAKE PHOS-
PHATE WITH YOUR MEALS.
Some drugs lessen the sensitiveness of the
nervous system so that the brain acts slowly,
the nerves do not respond readily to the
directions of the brain, and every function of
the body is retarded. Others act as a lash
to the nerves, so that the user is fired tem-
porarily with feverish energy. Both are
equally dangerous, for there is the inevitable
reaction which leaves the sufferer far .worse
than before. Therefore, don’t drug your
nerves! Get to understand them and study
their needs. Nerves give way because they
are weak, and they become weak through
lack of food and nourishment. The futility
of thrashing a starved horse is obvious, and
it is just as futile and just as foolish to
lash weak, exhausted nerves by the adminis-
tration of violent drugs. Nerves feed on
phosphate, which they extract from digested
food, and the fact that your nerves are weak
proves they are not obtaining sufficient
phosphate for their needs. You can remedy
this serious fault by providing your nerves
with phosphate in a readily assimilable form,
a most suitable and convenient being bitro-
phosphate, which most chemists stock in the
form of 5-grain compressed tablets. Take
one of these small tablets immediately after
every meal and you will soon note a decided
improvement in your health. Your neiwes
will become steady and strong, your brain
will act quickly and clearly; your app°t : te
will improve ; you will sleep better, a u 1 wake
un br ; ght and refreshed, charged -v m an
abundance of vital energy snffi- io y
you through the most strenuous day.-Any T.
“Azack”: Can any apiarist explain why
it is that when bees leave the hive it is the custom
to ling a bell or belt a tin-can in the middle of the
moving mass? It is asserted by those who follow
this procedure that the noise causes the bees to
swarm again, so that they can be recaptured. * This
explanation appears to be wanting when it is known
that bees have no sense of hearing (entomologists
reckon so, anyhow). One story is that in ancient
days, when a swarm of bees left their hive, the
owner kicked up some kind of a row for the purpose
of letting his neighbors know that some of his
property was on the move, whereupon if it swarmed
on their property he was entitled to reclaim it, hav-
ing given notice. This yarn seems as good as any
other that offers.
“Leo Lear” : These are open to competi-
tion: (1) A ewe owned by Sandy Graham, Milltown
(West Vie.). In eight consecutive years she in-
creased the birth rate by 2,3, 4,2, 3,2, 3, 3—22 in
all —and would have had four this year if she hadn’t
died before the event. (2) A Beach Forrest sow
belonging to O. W. Jackson. In four years she has
mothered six litters numbering in all 101—12, 16,
20, 16, 16, 21. Finally (3) on Mr. W. Hawkin’s
farm, Trafalgar, three cows which have each had
twins; and they all blame the same gentleman !
* * * *
“Barcoo”: It’s correct that Brookong
(locally called Bluegong) shed in Riverina (N.S.W.)
had 104 stands in the early ’Bo’s, the good old blade
days. There were other sheds that had just about
a hundred men on the board —and one, at least, con-
tiguous to Brookong, had 101. Those cumbersome
gangs went out when the machines came in. Large
sheds were cut down 50 per cent, in stands. North-
ampton, on the Barcoo (Q.), put in 70 machines, and
the first year the barbers threw 10,800 jumbucks
down the chutes in one day—a record —and well on
to 10,000 every full working day. Wool was piled
in heaps as high as the shed in all directions, and
the operators were repeating their performance at the
next shed while the pressers cleared the accumula-
tion. Bowen Downs (Q.) was turning off between
8000 and 9000 woollies every day. It was quite
unnecessary, and very few sheds run 40 machines
nowadays.
* * * *
“E.S.S.”: I heartily endorse “Jimmie
Pannikin’s’’ observations re planting Australian trees
and flowers in school grounds (B. 14/2/’18). Most
of these dust-heaps are a disgrace to the Department.
If native trees, the glory and pride of our land,
appreciated and admired the world over except by
the Bumbles here, were planted round the boun-
daries, what a transformation there would be!
Besides being beautiful and useful in providing shade,
they would be instructive, and in time a valuable
commercial asset. I have a wild cherry-tree growing
in front of my house, and I don't think there is
another vegetable in Sydney that attracts so much
notice from passers-by as that good Australian. At
Undereliff (Sydney), where many workers have
made natty homes amid the rocks and scrub, the
fronts are planted with Christmas bush, banksia,
waratah, tea-tree, blueberry, wattle and others lifted
from the neighboring bush. Long may they live!
NO GENTLEMAN.
CAMBEWARRA BILLY: “Good day, Missus!”
MRS. EDWARDS: “You should raise your hat,
Billy, token you speak to me, like a
gentleman—like Mr. Edwards does when
he speaks to ladies.”
CAMBEWARRA BILLY: “Baal Misser Edxoards
gentleman. Baal that phella raise it hat
long a my Mary!”
I would plant native trees along all the main roads
wherever timber is scarce. We owe some such repara-
tion to posterity for the vast wealth that has been
fired, ringbarked and otherwise destroyed.
A CORNISH MAN’S HOLIDAY.
“Ded ’ee go to poor Billy’s funeral last week, Josiah?”
“I ded.”
“What soort turnout ded ’e ’ave?”
“Bootiful! There teas seventy-one carts, leavin’ out the ’earse, and twenty-nine singers,
’eludin’ meself. I tell ’ee, I doan’ knaw whenever I enjoyed a funeral more.”
“Malaria”;: Wanted, a recipe for keeping
malarial fever at a distance. I’ve been trading to
the Tropics for years, and had a spell of five months
(ashore and afloat) in the Papuan Government ser-
vice. During that time, which in-
cluded the rainy season, I visited
many places on the Coast and ad-
jacent islands, and went up every
river and creek of note. I’ve camped
in the bush and on seashores where
the mosquitoes were as thick as rain-
drops, and I’ve been in those early-
morning tropical mists that I’ve read
are deadly, yet not once did I show
any traces of malarial fever, and I
did not take any quinine to ward off
possible attacks. Then in ’l4 I went to
Rabaul, and was ashore for only an hour
one night when I fell down to malaria
and stayed down for a week. Last
November I again visited Rabaul,
Everything went O.K. till we struck
cool weather on the way back, when
I was off duty for four watches with
the old complaint. When it started
I imbibed a 20-grain dose of bi-sul-
phate of quinine and several hot
drinks, and covered me over with five
blankets. Sweat did the rest. On
the next trip, from the day we struck
tropical weather till the day we
quitted it, five and sometimes 10
grains were my daily dose of quinine,
and I had no trouble at all. Now
it’s on the subject of quinine-dosing that I’m
looking for tips. Some men dose themselves
every day, and malaria gives them the go-by ; others
refuse to dose, and they fare the same; others, again,
dose themselves moderately, and still they’re not free
from trouble.
* #• * *
“Leo Lear”: The native bear makes one
of the tamest and most affectionate pets of any native
animal we possess; but all bushmen know that its
life in captivity is short. I often wondered why.
Now I know. Norman Lindsay's pals die of appen-
dicitis. Darwin says they possess the longest appen-
dix known, sometimes three times the length of the
little fellow’s body.
“Melos”: When tired of pate de foie gras
and other minor delicacies try a serving of fried
gohanna or roast porcupine. The former tastes some-
what like first-chop chicken, the latter even better.
Black brother prepared the dishes in this way. The
go’ was slit from throat to butt of tail, small sticks
inserted in the opening to keep the flaps, well apart,
and the carcase placed on live coals. The two rows
of fat were sufficient for the frizzling, and the hard,
horny hide served the purpose of a pan. The pore,
was merely covered with glowing coals and roasted in
his juice.
* * * *
“D.F.”: The pearly nautilus is a very rare
shell in Tasmanian waters, and when it comes ashore
it is, as a rule, battered or broken. But there’s a
little half-mile beach at Lagoon Bay (Wilmot Harbor
on the maps), on the east coast of Forestier Penin-
sula, which makes a specialty of these shells. More
seem to > come up there than on all the other much
bigger beaches put together. And they are generally
in good condition.
* * * *
“Shepherd”: A while ago a’ previously
unknown tribe of Binghis visited the Violet Valley
abo. station (W.A.). The local abos. called them
“saltwater blacks.” They wore no clothes, not even
a loin-cloth, and could not understand English. It
is remarkable how these little congregations have
managed to exist without discovery. Only a ; few
years ago a tribe was discovered in the mallee-and-
spinifex country near the sou’-west corner of N. S.
Wales, though that territory was occupied long
before Burke and Wills passed through.
“Jackdaw”: Here’s the last supper for
sheep-worrying dogs. Shoot a wallaby. Cut him
through to the backbone. Drag the mess over your
tracks for one mile to the haunt of the thief. Steep
your boots in the mix-up. Strip wally. Stand on
his shirt. Hang the carcase to a tree by putting
one hind-leg through the hamstring of the other
around a limb just out of easy reach of Mephisto-
plieles. Cut out and poison the pluck. Drop it
under the decoy. Cover slightly with rubbish, using
a stick. Walk away, trailing the skin behind. 1
know of a £2O and have heard of a £5O bonus
earned this way.
* * * *
“C.C.P.”: “Machete” (B. 31/1/T8) is
quite right in believing that cannibalism was pretty
common in South Queensland. The greatest author-
ity on the local blacks, the late Tom Petrie, told me
that though the natives never killed their kind for
food, they most certainly never failed to eat anyone
killed in fight, and always ate a “turrwan” (great
man), no matter how old he was or what was the
cause of death. They had some strange ideas about
the body of a great man. They explained that they
ate it out of pity and consideration; that “they
knew where he was then” and “he won’t stink.” As
for the old tough gins, no one troubled to eat them;
their bodies weren't of any importance and had no
pity shown them. On the other hand, a young,
plump gin would always be eaten; likewise anyone
dying in good condition. As to the bunya feasts, it
was once stated by an early-day casual observer that
the blacks grew so tired of nut and vegetable food
during this period that to satisfy the craving that
grew upon them for animal food they terminated the
meeting by the sacrifice of one gin or more. This
was quite untrue. On their way to and from these
great gatherings the natives travelled along the
coast as much as was possible, and got fish and
oysters; also while there they hunted for ’possums,
carpet-snakes, eggs, etc. They, however, grew quite
fat on the nut diet. After the corroborees and cere-
monies were over a fight always ended proceedings,
and someone was sure to be killed. Then all parted
the best of friends. People speak of the great num-
ber killed in fight, but the truth is that the dead
were but few, though wounds were plentiful enough.
Singeing and skinning and dividing a corpse prepara-
tory to a feast was quite a ceremony.
“Te Whare”: In 1837 the New Zealand
Land Association published a report of its doings, in-
cluding, inter alia, an interesting account of the
trade in tattooed Maori heads. According to the
document this traffic was very profitable. The skulls
were sold in Sydney as curios. Finally, one ship’s
captain brought to market 10 heads of chiefs. That
settled it. While the grisly relics were being offered
for sale old parson Sam Marsden obtained an order
from Governor Darling declaring the practice un-
lawful ; and so a flourishing little industry was
summarily squelched.
WHERE IT SHOWED.
THE DEAR GIRL: "Yes, dear boy, I am
heavy; but you’d never think it to look
at me, would you?”
THE DEAR BOY: “P’raps not. But you
would to look at me.”
MATES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
His name was Clancy—white all through.
We shared whatever went,
An’ done our bit of dinkum graft,
Or legged it from the tent
An’ dodged the Mother’s Pets to have
A beer-up or a dance—
Cripes! but we had some shyin’ times
Together, me an’ Clance.
Now all that’s left’s: a little cross
Rigged from the splintered trees,
An’ on the top his ol’ tin hat
Is swingin’ in the breeze.
Christ, but it’s crook! Not that I grudge
The wing I dropped in France,
But home an’ all’s a hungry place
Without me cobber Clance.

“Cross-Cut”: “Myola” made one mistake
(B. 14/2/’18) in describing the La Perouse
(Sydney) porpoise industry. “Acres of
hides,” he said, “are thrown away to feed
the sharks. We prefer to import our bootlaces
and other porpoise-leather goods from the
North Sea via Europe.” It’s a very old
fallacy about the waterproof qualities of
porpoise skin. The so-called “porpoise-liide”
is simply a trade name for a good-quality
close-grained horse or similar leather, very
suitable for shooting-boots used in wet coun-
try. It has a reputation for being waterproof,
gained principally by the trade name “por-
poise.” The skin of the real thing is quite
worthless. If “Myola” can show the tan-
ners how to make any kind of leather from
porpoise, shark or even whale covering, he’ll
soon lind the fishermen bothering to take the
weighty objects to Sydney. By the way, did
"Myola” ever try to skin a porpoise?
“Squills”: M.L. is beginning to turn its
hydro-electric power to good uses besides
driving street cars, lighting the cities and
running factories. Some chemicals, which
since the war have swooped up to prohibi-
tive prices, are likely to be brought down to
normal or thereabouts by the development
of the Lake Coleridge (Canterbury) power.
Sulphate of iron, formerly imported for
purifying gas, has been replaced in Canter-
bury by hydrate of iron, obtained from old
tins. Caustic soda (it cost £l2 per ton
before the war and now costs £45) is about
to be manufactured in Christchurch by the
electrolysis of' common salt; same with
hydrochloric acid. Also, preliminary work
is being done on the direct electrolysis . of
water, to yield oxygen for acetylene welding
and hydrogen for the hardening of fats.
Birks, the expert in charge of the Coleridge
scheme, reckons that hydro-electric power
will enable the Shaky Islands to evaporate
enougti sea-water to produce all the common
salt the inhabitants want, at a very low cost.
For another thing, the water-power will
enable M.L. lo manufacture all the calcium
carbide it needs, all the ammonia, and all the
explosives required to strafe an invader. So
far only the Canterbury alpine lake is har-
nessed, but there is a vastly greater volume
of power waiting for the turbines in the
Waikato River and Lake Waikaremoana.
During the last Melbourne heat-wave two
workmen tapped an lSgal. keg of beer at
Flinders-street station. On' the Beak saying
it would cost them £lO apiece, one of the
beer-suckers complained bitterly that when
his turn had come at the bamboo it wouldn’t
reach to the beer, and he got none. But Jus-
tice wouldn’t even knock off the price of a
pint.
“Bluegum”: The old-time Maori had a
measuring-rod, called a rauru, on which the
owner’s arm-span and other measurements
were marked. But each brown man
was generally his own yard-measure. When
Timi wanted to find out the length of a ship
or a whale or a palisade he would lie down
at full length alongside the object, with his
arm stretched out, make a mark, then get up
and keep going till he had finished his reckon-
ing. Naturally there was room for argu-
ment with a tomahawk when a-bantam and
a six-foot-three cannibal compared notes with
regard to, say, Timi’s new war-canoe.
“Try Fluke’’: No need to import Irish or
other moss into Maoriland when the sea-
shore rocks for hundreds of miles are thickly
coated with exactly the substance that strafes
bronchitis, sore throat and kindred troubles.
The vegetable is called by the Maoris
karengo, and it is a palatable and nourish-
ing addition to pork and spuds. The natives
scrape it off the rocks at half-tide, squeeze
it into small lumps, and dry. it in
the sun as a preliminary to the
pot. I am told that there is practically
no difference in essentials between this
karengo and the carageen moss which is
gathered on the Irish coasts. A curious
point also is that Pat O’Flaherty’s and Hone
te Kooti’s names for the stuff should so
closely resemble each other. Here’s a bur-
row opened for the comparative philologist
to go nosing into.
* * * *
“Silas Snell’’: Not so long ago I struck
a far-east Gippsland family of five, all
grown up, not one of whom had seen a rail-
way train, so I can believe Tomlofty’s story.
Tomlofty is one of the many travelling re-
presentatives of a familiar sewing-machine
co. Late last year he conceived the
idea of taking a sample machine on the
razor-edge of a mule, and penetrating to
recesses into which no sewing-machine agent
had ever yet insinuated himself. It was in
the course of these explorations that he dis-
covered the McCances. He talked with
McCance about his miraculous machine, and
McCance was interested. “I’ve heard tell of
’em,” he said. Tomlofty said no family
could afford to be without the implement.
“What figger?” asked McCance. “Ten
quid.” said Tomlofty, chancing it. “Ten
quid?” drawled McCance. “Bring ’er along,
V let’s have a look at ’er.” Tomlofty un-
strapped his sample, and carried it into
McCanee’s kitchen. The sale he reckoned a
dead cert. McCance looked the apparatus
over. “Yes,” he said, “she looks pretty, ’n’
she seems all right, ’n’ I’d like to have her;
but blow me if I can see how you're goin'
to put in a crop with a machine like that!”

“Birregurra”: Melbourne firms have evi-
dently spotted a new method of dodging war-
time profits tax. An epidemic of premises-
renovation has set in round the city, which
reeks of paint and varnish. The other day
I counted 10 newly-decorated buildings in
one block.
“Suwarrow” : Outside the premises of the
Commonwealth Bank at Melbourne there is
a large sign urging Australians to be patri-
otic and pour their good Australian cash
into the War Loan. All the timber of the
framework to carry this appeal to be patriotic
is—imported Oregon !
* * * •*
“Wong” : The Primary Industries Develop-
ment League is a better infant than “Henry
Horsecollar” (B. 21/2/’18) thus far thinks
it is. It is joyously and frankly an organisa-
tion to boost, and has no time or room for
the stinking-fish whiners who have done more
to retard Australia’s progress than anything
else. Taking a leaf out of America’s book
of success, it aims at advertising and boost-
ing different country districts that have
sufficient self-confidence to put up some of
the expense of making Australia seem a
desirable place in the eyes of war-weary
white people overseas, our own and other
countries’ Billjims, and their like. And
it hopes to set the Government red-tape
bureaux an example by having a definite
proposition to put up to these newcomers,
and not allow them to wander around Sydney
spending all their oof before they know
where they are. Anyone who knows any-
thing of our immigration and land-settlement
methods of the last 10 years would not deem
a business-like organisation preparing to
tackle the job, openly and aboveboard, an
“unaccountable.” If “Henry Horsecollar,”
good Australian as we know him to
be, would wander round to the at present,
modest office of the League, 16 O’Connell-
street, Sydtiey, and put the acid on the scheme
I reckon he would withdraw the epithet.
* * * *
“Mirrool” : Ordinary coal-gas now pushes
almost all large cars and commercial lorries
in England, where severe restrictions are put
on the use of petrol. It certainly doesn’t
add to the beauty of the car. A platform is
built over the body, and on it is mounted a
cylindrical container. Gas cos. have estab-
lished refilling points along the main roads,
and the gas is sold at a rate which gives
cheaper haulage that petrol ever did. Inci-
dentally, the small boy finds great joy in
puncturing the gas-bag with barbed darts. It
is ever so much more sudden fun than push-
ing a pin into an inflated tyre.

* * * *
A waggon piled with furniture was trailing
through Sydney one day lately, when some-
body stopped the driver and asked him where
he was taking the bushfire. The astonished
charioteer looked behind, saw swirling clouds
of smoke and fell off. Then a load of firemen
clattered round the corner and doused the
blazing caravan ; after which insurance man-
agers had another interesting item for their
Book of Instructions to Canvassers.
The magistrate of Tower Bridge, London,
has been fhaking known his theory, formed
from long experience, that the honesty of a
crowd depends on the time of day. “It is
not a question of drink at all; a subtle
change se|ms to come over the character of
people with the later hours.” If he hap-
pened to drop 10 sovereigns in a crowded
street any time before midday, he would
expect to get back about eight of them, and
at the later hours a steadily decreasing
number, till at midnight he would be lucky
t-' recover two. It sounds ingenious, but
if you went to the right place you could
get your pocket picked just as easily at 10
a.in. as at midnight. The character of the
crowd in the street changes mostly because
the crowd changes.
* * * *
“Quidam”: The country resident, particu-
larly the non-travelled variety, possesses
weird ideas regarding prevailing high prices,
his settled conviction being that they are the
fault of the local storekeeper. As a matter
of fact, no one is more injured by the pre-
sent prices than small country tradesmen.
Whatever the profiteering, and whoever is
doing it, they are not benefiting by it, for
they are frequently unable, by reason of the
poverty and crass ignorance of their clients,
to pass on as much of the increased prices
as they are entitled to. A long-established
country blacksmith I know does his own
work and has enough to keep him pretty
busy, too. Most of his customers accuse him
of exorbitant prices, and allege that he is
coining money; yet for a month’s steady toil
of himself and his 18-year-old son his' tak-
ings were £2B, out of which £l4-odd went
for material, so that the wages of the two
workers barely amounted to £3 10s. a week,
with upkeep of shop, tools, etc., to come
out of it. Yet he recognises that it is im-
possible to raise his prices further, as the
local people couldn’t or wouldn’t stand it.
Undue profits would be snipped off sooner if
people stopped blaming the -wrong man and
started looking for the right one. To culti-
vate the idea that one of their own fellow-
villagers, whom they have known for years
and always found a decent chap, has sud-
denly developed furious rapacity, is a silly
sort of business that diverts attention from
much larger persons who are making much
more indecent profits.
SONNET—AVOIRDUPOIS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I have a quarter of a ton of lovers,
Who fatly sport around me all the. clay,
Kager my latest whimsy to obey
At furrier’s, draper’s, milliner’s or glover’s.
They are as downy-soft as new-fledged plovers ;
And, as the sun shines, I am making hay—
In fact, his earliest and latest ray
In them some fresh and puffy charm discovers.
There are but three—and I am six-stone-eight—-
But in their ponderous make is my delight,
As theirs in my faint, sylph-like build and measure ;
1 count my triumphs by their solid weight,
And watch them grow like mushrooms in the night,
Scarce knowing which is the most gorgeous treasure.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

“Davey-street”: After battling hard since
its birth 11 years back, Hobart’s Labor
Daily Post has at last found its feet,
manager Ben Watkins having just com-
pleted negotiations with the World Co. for
substantial backing. The Post’s staff had
recently dwindled to three, ex-Speaker
Walter Woods being editor, sub., leader-
writer and “cables.”
* * * *
“Baro”: “Bill Bowyang” (28/2/T8) is
on the right track when lie repeats a two-
year-old call of The Bulletin and urges
the Defence Department or the Mitchell
Library to collect the journals published on
hospital ships and transports. Of late they
have been mostly printed in Durban or Cape
Town while the ship coaled. The Cape
Times office has laid itself out to produce
“rush” editions. We took our copy for a
10-pager to the printer at 2 p.m. on Friday,
and got the thousand copies with illustra-
tions at 10 next morning. There are over
100 different productions on file in the office.
The Cape Public Library is collecting them
all. and I am sure a request from the autho-
rities here would bring a copy of everything
issued .to date. On ships having printing
plant on board Billjim journalism has also
flourished. On a White Star liner, for in-
stance, Transport Jottings contained the
original account of the exploits of Maud
Butler, a “recruit” who was two days at
sea on that vessel before her small feet gave
her away.
