Things seem to be a trifle mixed in Mr.
James’s house in Hunter-street, according to
The Australasian Journalist (15/2/’18).
Says that publication:—
During the past 20 months 18 men have either
resigned or been dismissed from the literary staff of
the S. M. Herald, at the rate of almost one a month.
The new office boy will be the Oldest In-
habitant directly.
* * * *
Judges often have to discuss the differ-
ence between “and” and “or” in an Act of
Parliament; but 8. Aus. Supreme Court has
been wrestling with an Act of 1850 which
says both, various printed copies differing
from each other. Which is the older or
more correct copy cannot be ascertained, and
the original signed copy of the Act has dis-
appeared ; possibly it may be in London at
the Colonial Office. So nobody can say
whether the Law reo.uires both “consent and
order,” in certain cases, or if either “consent
or order” is sufficient.
“S.T.S.”: It is pleasant to find, in
Hardy’s last book of verse, an Australian
turn of speech, “that cow Oblivion”- —and a
fair cow some ambitious people have found
it. P.S. —On a second look this cow turns
out to be a verb. The idea is that there are
in Shakspeare “harmonies that cow Obli-
vion,” whatever that may mean.
* * * *
The Christmas literary supplement of
London Times has a complaint to make
about the lady storytellers of Australia and
Maoriland. They “barely touch upon the
fringe of the many experiences a vivid
description of which would be both new and
helpful and vastly entertaining to girls of
this country.” Isabel Maud Peacocke’s
Maoriland yarn, “Patricia Pat,” and Mary
Grant Bruce’s Victorian bush-farm story,
“Possum,” are the text of these remarks.
Still, Possum is an example of “courage and
unselfishness in simple conditions which it
is quite worth while for English girls to
know.” The Times complaint has a lot of
justification. But why confine it to the
women? One of TriE Bulletin’s major
tribulations is that, while it is hungry for
the local color of places further afield than
the outer suburbs, the man or woman who
lives in Central Papua, or on a northern
canefield, or in a far-west boundary-rider’s
hut, will persist in writing stuff that'the city
literary tradesman can turn out just as well
in his factory. On the whole, in fact, this
paper’s experience is that men are more
prone to do this than women.
Jack _Maher, a well-known sporting writer,
is assisting Ballarat pressman Monty
McCallum in the compilation of “Victoria’s
Who’s Who.” dealing with deeds and mis-
deeds of public men, sportsmen and others.
McCallum recently published a special edi-
tion in Ballarat and appreciably improved
his banking account.

From Pearson’s :
Possibly you’ve heard of the Sydney Bulletin.
Those interesting young men in slouch hats orna-
mented with rising suns are fond of mentioning it.
It is, of course, the one and only paper worth pub-
lishing and readin-'-- that is their universal opinion.
But there is an exception and the other dav we met
him. “Believe me, dinkum,” he said earnestly, “there
are not three men on The Bulletin to-day who
could write ‘Hamlet’!”
Oh, well, neither could Shakspeare.
* * * *
“II.H.”: That man up Narromine way
who took fire with fatal results while sleep-
ing behind Dobbin in lus cart was remark-
able,. but a stranger occurrence once nearly
got into print in a provincial paper. It was
a pity that the editor found out in time to
kill it. A dissipated reporter was doing
“accidents and offences,” and the general but
hazy effect of his best effort seemed to imply
that the carburetter blew up, and in conse-
quence the horse bolted and Mrs. Smith,
being thrown out against a tree, died of
ptomaine poisoning. This attempt to con-
dense three happenings into one wasn’t
approved, and there was an aching void on
the staff that night.
* * * *
Ambrose Pratt has taken hold of the
Mining Standard. Critchley Parker’s
humorous fad of calling it also the States-
man has been dropped ; it is now the Indus-
trial and Mining Standard, and promises
to be a sane and vigorous fighter for the
development, as distinguished from the ex-
ploitation, of Australia.

DESTROYING THE DANDRUFF
GERM.
HOW TO GROW THICK, GLOSSY
HAIR.
The large increase in hair troubles within
the last year has become alarming, so much
so that almost every other person, whether
aware of the fact or not, suffers from hair
trouble of some kind or other from time to
time. In many cases the trouble gradually
increases and becomes chronic. The result
is probably falling hair and premature
baldness. One of the commonest and most
deadly of hair troubles results from the
presence of the dandruff germ. A reliable
hair tonic that will destroy this germ is
absolutely essential, and should be used by
everyone noticing the slightest sign of
dandruff or scurf. Many different prepara-
tions have been tried from time to time, but
the formula which is said to produce the
best results is the following: 3oz. bay rum.
loz. lavona de composee, and J-drachm men-
thol crystals. These ingredients can be
obtained from the chemist and easily mixed
at home, and if the tonic is well rubbed into
the scalp night and morning it will quickly
eradicate every trace of scurf and dandruff.
It will also allay irritation, stop hair falling
out, and make dull, thin, straggly hair grow
thick, long and lustrous once more.—Advt.

POLITICAL
POINTS
As soon as Billjim, returned invalided, gets
his ticket lie passes into the presence of the
Pensions Magistrate to do one of the most
important bits of business of his life. As a
rule, he doesn’t know much about business—
is hazy about the regulations and his rights;
and even if he wasn’t hazy, nine times out of
ten he wouldn’t know how to make out his
case. Further, his training in the army has
unfitted him for arguing with “superior
authority.” He takes what is given him —
and growls about it afterwards. And un-
fortunately he mostly does his growling
among possible recruits, which isn’t good for
recruiting. Three years ago the lawyers of
some parts of Australia did fine work for
Billjim—and for recruiting—by establishing
Red Cross Information Bureaus. They can
DINNER-TIME.
THE CHEF: "Well, no, I haven't exactly
cooked anything yet, but I am compiling
an excellent cookery-book.”
do better work for Billjim and recruiting
now by undertaking to see him through the
Pensions Court. And they ought to'lose no
time in setting up in every capital an agency
for the purpose.
“Senator Pearce- Stands Firm” was a
heading in a last week’s daily. It should
have been “Senator Pearce Wobbles All Over
the Place,” or “Senator Pearce Totters On
Both Feet.” The subject was the trouble at
Lithgow where the four barrel-straighteners
(a small group of highly-paid specialists
whose habit of sending an ultimatum to the
management is chronic) went on strike.
Australia’s very essential war industry was
paralysed, and 1100 men thrown out of work.
The grievance is alleged to be that the barrel-
straighteners objected to official military in-
spection of their output. In the interests of
Australia and the empire and of the civilisa-
tion which the Ilun is wrecking, it was
Pearce’s business to squash that strike with
the utmost rigor of the War Precautions
Act and to promptly redress the grievances,
if any, afterwards. Instead he only Stood
Firm with his Mouth. lie stood just like
Postmaster-General Webster’s lost letter-
bags, which ought to be travelling to their
destination, but aren’t.
A conference of unions is to meet shortly
and grieve ovsr the high cost of living. One
reason is the multiplicity of strikes, almost
all illegal and many of them trivial and
purposeless. In 1916 (latest complete
figures) the Common wealth lost 1,678,930
days’ work, worth £967,604 in wages. That
means, in the end, £967,604 added to the
scarcity of things and consequently to the
aggregate cost of things. Incidentally
£674,064 of the loss and scarcity and in-
creased cost was in N. S. Wales alone.
W. M. Hughes holds one record which is
likely to be good for a long time. He has
led four consecutive Ministries. The day
he retired he came back, so Hughes (going-
out three times) bumped against Hughes
(coming in) on the doorstep. Henry Ayers,
of S. Australia, who was his own successor
in two pairs of Governments, is next best.
“Canuck”: It is soothing to reflect that if
the Federal Government isn’t Winning the
War it is at least considering some people’s
interests. While the Victorian housewife is
so hampered by the alleged shortage of
sugar that she has had to abandon her
annual jam-making outburst, the breweries
have somehow contrived to obtain full sup-
plies. It is likewise pleasing to note that
owing to tons of good fruit being thus wasted
the pigs are having a birthday. We are at
least fattening our own hogs.
There are men called William who look
so important that one hardly dreams of call-
ing them Bill in a friendly way, but this
paper is going to call William Irvine Bill
for a change. Lately
Bill suggested that the
Government should ig-
nore the Referendum
just as Hughes ignored
his promise to retire.
It should, said Bill,
introduce a compul-
sory-service proposi-
tion and stand or fall
by the issue. There-
upon Sydney Tele-
graph spoke to Bill
and asked if, after such
treachery, the promise
of any politician
would be believed.
But politicians get on
pretty well without
being believed. For
instance, the Holman
crowd attained office
in N. S. Wales on a
promise to reduce loan
expenditure (whi c h
was a little over
£1,000,000 in the year
when the promise was
made) and to spend
loan money only on
works which would
pay full interest and
sinking fund. The
loans were rushed up
to £9,000,000 in the
worst year, and the
interest and sinking
fund idea went to the
bosom of Jonah. Yet
though the solemn
promise of Holman
and Co. on a big finan-
cial question was re-
pudiated, the public
not only keeps them
in office but accepts
the signature of the
repudiating crowd on millions and millions
worth (face value) of bonds, and even the
Telegraph hardly complains. So why
should Bill (any Bill) be more righteous
than his neighbors?
Hughes at a British Empire League’s
luncheon in Sydney :
How many votes would a man get who stood on the
hustings and expressed himself in favor of German
goods, or any other goods, coming into Australia free
for the next 10 years?
But why should a man be such a clumsy
fool? It is far more profitable for him to
talk magnificently about patriotism and
winning wars and so on while he’s letting
the sliprail down for the foreigner.
The change of Government in Victoria
has given one or two progressive minds a
chance to shake up the skeletons in
the State cupboard. One of the
loudest rattles has been caused by
Treasurer McPherson investigating
the Compassionate Allowance Fund
established in one of his mellow
moments by the late T. Bent.
McPherson has found it to be “prac-
tically a secret pension fund”; in
one instance, £1 a week was being
paid to an ex-Government servant
with two married sons, one with a
screw of £750 and allowing the old
man ss. out of his weekly £l4. The
Treasurer is going to abolish the
fund, if he can, and leave the State-
aided “benevolent” societies to do the
necessary compassioning. It seems a t
painful alternative, yet the Govern-’
ment can’t go on showering private
blessings around while it can only
meet its public obligations by tap-
ping the pawnshop.
In spite of the official decree that
wooden ships are of no use to Aus-
tralia unless they are built in the
U.S.A., Tasmanian shipbuilders per-
sist in turning the things out. Not
satisfied with the 400-ton schooner
already under way, Hobart ship-
builders are laying down a 000-ton wooden
sailing vessel, and they will build it, too,
unless the Pliewsion Government stops them
—as it stopped the Broken Hill Prop, when
it wanted to build steel ships. The biggest
vessel turned out in Tasmania in the old
days was 562 tons register.
PEACE ARRIVES: 19 ?
Just when it was definitely understood
that the Federal Government should build
ships at Walsh Island (N.S.W.) instead of
the State Government, as originally pro-
posed, it was suggested that the State Gov-
ernment should build ships at Walsh Island
(N.S.W.) instead of the Federal Govern-
ment as proposed after the previous proposal.
Whereupon Mr. Watt in Victoria discussed
the matter with Mr. Hughes in Victoria and
telegraphed to Mr. Holman in N. S. Wales
asking him to come to Melbourne in Victoria
and thresh the matter out. And now the
Lord only knows what will happen next.
This subject has all the dodgy capacity of a
Hea.
If Pearce has a Commission straying
about he might ask it to call in at Adelaide
and investigate the workings not only of the
Defence Department there but also of the
agencies set up for the assistance of returned
Billjim. There is, for instance, a Provi-
dent Soldiers’ Fund which is really an Im-
provident Soldiers’ Fund, and its operations
have the effect of confirming them in their
improvidence. One result is that it is
becoming more and more difficult for re-
turned men to get employment in S. Aus.;
and as that is most prejudicial to recruiting,
there is very good reason why the suggested
Commission should poke an inquisitive nose
into the matter.
The ancient judge slumbering on the
bench with one deaf ear in the grave is a
scandal, and N. S. Wales proposes to retire
his Honor (with a liberal pension) at the
age of 70. A protest was entered by two
legal members, who said that the measure
should only apply to “future” judges,
“present” judges not having bargained for
compulsory retirement on an age limit. They
talked largely about “keeping faith.” But
never a word did they say about keeping
faith with the litigant who didn’t bargain for
losing his case and a heap of money simply
because he was supplied (having no voice
in the matter) with a learned ruin who
hardly knew whether he was a plaintiff or
a hardened offender.
THE PIPE OF PEACE.
Chief Justice Griffith has been given a
Commission to find out how many reinforce-
ments are wanted. Well, and suppose he
does find out? In August, 1915, nearly
three years ago, Griffith’s colleague Rich
was given a Commission to find out how
things might be improved in the administra-
tion of the Defence Department. He
reported :— ■
In this war between nations the time is fully ripe
for organising the services of all classes of the com-
munity. Business men might well be employed in
matters where their training and experience would
be of the greatest advantage to the military authori-
ties, e.g., in connection with ordnance and on finance
committees.
After a few millions had been wasted, Justice
Rich’s recommendation was more or less
adopted in February , 1918. There really
seems to be room for only one more Commis-
sion. The German camps might be asked
to suggest the personnel, and its job would
be to tell the Ministers whether, in the
Commission’s opinion, they have left undone
anything that might help the Kaiser.
The loudest squeal over the Federal Gov-
ernment’s price-fixing of meat was that of
Edmund Jowett, M.P. According to this
excited but vague meat —er —consumer the
“proposal to reduce the prices of Australian
meat for local consumption to the prices
fixed for export would prejudicially affect
the people, not only of Australia, but of the
whole empire. It would mean a loss of
£45,000,000 to the stockholders of Aus-
tralia.” But why not turn it upside down
and say that it will mean a gain of
£45,000,000 to the people of Australia?
Meantime what are Jowett’s brethren going
to do to him for being so frank about the
size of the attempted steal?
LIES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“The cable is the most ingenious system of lying
ever invented.”—lf. M. lluglics.
It lies, it lies! The trembling word that quivers
About the world beneath the pulsing sea.
To think of how it lies gives me the shivers.
It lies, it lies —and, worse, it lies of me!
fn all the lands and over all creation
My venomed enemies forever lurk,
The vantage ground of every cable station
Affords their malice room for evil work.
Naught I can do, naught I can say protects me.
Some twisted shame their thoughts must still
devise.
The very city that with pride elects me
Ts fed forever with their cabled lies.
What’s that? The censorship is mine to handle!
Great is the glory of the pencil’s blue !
It is the light, the torch, the glowing candle
To win-the-war and lead the people through 1
Hut still those lies are always in the cable
That quivers underneath the pulsing sea ;
And not the boldest censor’s fist is able
To camouflage the words that speak of me.
Ties and more lies! But keep the old flag waving!
We yet may get a grip upon the news;
And by suppressing all may come near saving
The splendid name and high repute of Hughes!
N.B.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

COMSTOCK’S “DEAD-SHOT” WORM
PELLETS.
A SAFE, SURE AND RELIABLE SPECIFIC FOR
THE ERADICATION OF WORMS IN CHILDREN
AND ADULTS.
The general symptoms of worms are a
tendency to pick the nose and a sensation of
itching in the anus. Other symptoms are
nervous affections, such as fretfulness, irri-
tation of temper, wakefulness or disposition
to excessive sleep, disturbed sleep, and grind-
ing the teeth, sudden starting out of sleep as
if from fright, dizziness, spasmodic move-
ments of the, eyelids, dilated pupils, per-
verted vision, temporary blindness and deaf-
ness. General convulsions are not infre-
quent in children, and symptoms resembling
water in the head have been ascribed to
worms, and have ceased on their expulsion.
The long round or stomach worm is the
commonest kind of worm from which man-
kind suffers, and is of a light, transparent
color, resembling the ordinary ground worm,
except that the extremities are more sharply
pointed than the ground worm.
Young people are more subject to these
worms than middle-aged or elderly people.
They live in the bowels, and sometimes make
their way into the stomach and throat, caus-
ing sickness, vomiting and choking. Gener-
ally one or two would be the most a person
would be afflicted with, but sometimes a
much larger number is met with. The length
varies from four to eight inches, but young
ones an inch to two inches long are also
found. The first thing to do is to rid the
system of the worms, and Comstock’s “Dead
Shot” Worm Pellets, taken according to
directions, are an effective remedy for the
thorough eradication of the long round or
stomach worm —the common enemy of the
child, the youth, the middle-aged and aged.
A light, nourishing diet, out-of-door exercise,
and, if possible, a change of air will greatly
improve the general health.
*
Threadworms or pinworms are of very
common occurrence in children. They are
very small, from one-sixth of an inch to half-
an-inch in length, and resemble cotton
thread—they are sometimes called seat
worms, and reside in the lower bowel. The
chief symptom of this worm is severe local
itching, restlessness and irritability, and a
frequent desire to urinate, picking at the
nose; loss of appetite and anaemia are also
observed. To get rid of the worms and their
eggs their nests also must be removed, and
that requires a specific remedy. Comstock’s
“Dead Shot” Worm Pellets, taken according
to directions, on an empty stomach, followed
by a dose of opening medicine, will succeed
where others fail.
Comstock’s “Dead Shot” Worm Pellets
are packed with full directions, and
are for sale by chemists and stores
generally at 3s. 6d. per packet, or six
packets for 205., and if unable to obtain
locally will be mailed post free on receipt of
price. Prepared only by The W. H. Comstock
Co., Ltd., 23 Lang Street, Sydney, N.S.W.;
Farish Street, Wellington, N.Z.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The Australian Cup race was a one-horse
affair for about 1} miles, Prince Bardolph being the
lone quadruped. Then it seemed almost a sure thing
that Defence would catch and pass the leader;
finally, when Defence had fulfilled expectations after
passing the distance post, Star Comedian came with
a bit of a rattle and ran Defence to a neck. Bar-
cjolph was only a length behind the second horse, so
lie may be said to have battled it out as bravely as
the two lighter-weighted ones. Fifteen other horses
never gave their backers a suspicion of a thrill.
Wallace Isinglass figured as chief offender. He was
.last when the field passed the judge’s box first time
/round, and not much better at the finish. In such
moderate company the vaunted son of Wallace ought
to have made a show at some point of the race, but
somehow he ran like a duffer all the time. His drop
back in the betting before the start was only a sug-
gestion of what was going to happen in the race.
The lather heavy going made the long - , long Cup
procession a very genuine test of stamina, and the
victory of Defence pleased most people who didn’t
back the second or third. The crowd remarked that
the old imported animal had been run without any
idea of bringing off a surprise. He earned a 71b.
penalty a month or more ago, and won another event
on the Saturday previous to this best race of his
life.
Star Comedian’s second in the Australian
Cup was a rather odd coincidence. The runner-up
in the preceding race was Tragic Star.
* * * *
Defence was not the only imported nag 1 to
make the most of a light weight in heavy going
at the late V.R.C. meeting. Blackwood won the
Autumn Handicap on Saturday like an honest old
stayer. It wasn’t much of a triumph for English
horseflesh, considering that Bronzetti was trying to
give the aged gelding 121 b. ; but taken in conjunc-
tion with Defence’s running over long distances it
went to prove that the average imported horse is
comparatively better as a stayer than as a sprinter.
Of late years the fastest of the imported ones have
been absurdly uncertain. They never sprint twice
alike.
* * * *
An interesting item of the postponed Aus-
tralian Cup Day racing at Flemington was the re-
vival of Tinto, an old chestnut timber-topper. Tinto
hadn’t won a race for four long years, and was
down to 9st. 31b., or nearly the lowest mark in the
weights. The has-been was whispered to be in some-
thing like his best form, but punters remained
sceptical, all the same. Perhaps the heavy going
favored the ancient. Anyhow, he won in quite
commanding style. Nor was he favored by luck.
People say of steeplechasers that they are “bound
to win if they stand up.” In this case all 11
starters stood up.
* * * *
Cetigne emphasised his quality by winning
the mile w.f.a. at Flemington last Saturday, beat-
ing Whiteficld, Wedge and the apparently unripe
Panacre. This time he was meeting Wedge on 81b.
worse terms than in the Newmarket, but the luck-
less Wedge had probably gone off a trifle since he
won the Futurity, whereas Cetigne was still at his
top. Later in the afternoon Desert Gold ran away
with the Governor's Plate in champion style, doing
the 12 furlongs in 2min. 35Jsec. On paper it was
picking up money to lay seven to four on the Maori-
land mare in a field of four, but the aroma of his
Spring reputation clung to imported Lanius and
brought him many backers. Lanius failed badly at
the finish, so it really looks as though the great
November disappointment might as well be retired
from public life and put to matrimony. He is a
noble-looking animal, and his record indicates that
he should get stayers if he is promptly sent to the
stud.
# * * *
The Maorilander was beaten again on
Monday at Flemington, this time by Wallace Ising-
glass at w.f.a ; and if this does not send the fickle
chestnut out a loaring favorite for the Sydney Cup,
then there is no such thing as certainty in the wide
world. There were only three starters in the King’s
Plate —Desert Gold, Wallace and the tender-footed
Lingle—and the race turned into a match from the
turn into the straight between the mare and the other.
As usual, Desert Gold ran away with the lead, and at
one stage had a break of half a dozen lengths. Four
furlongs from home Wallace closed on her, and they
larded into the fairway together. Both were ridden
to the distance, and there the mare began to falter.
She was clean out, and Wallace ran in a winner by
a length and a half, Lingle a long way astern.
Whereupon an opulent colliery proprietor was so
full of joy that he was detected trying to grin.
# * * *
The once-boomed Sir Amyas gave unmis-
takable evidence of what’s the matter with him
by his in-and-out running at the V.R.C. meeting.
On the first Saturday he appeared to be home and
dry for the Brunswick Stakes when he led the field
into the straight. But it wasn’t his day for strug-
gling, or perhaps the mud worried him. A couple
of hundred yards from home he began to stick
his toes into the mud, and he -finished fourth
instead of first. A week later, on the same course,
the imported cocktail carried a stone more, and
won generously (from a weaker field) in ssec. faster
time. Sir Amyas may be nothing to write home
about, but he is at least a horse.
“0.K.”: Rather late in the day it has
become obvious to all Flemington racegoers that,
save in the dryest weather, the outside of the straight,
nearest to the judge s box, must necessarily be better
going than the inside, where a mob of horses is
being jostled in the ordinary way of racing for posi-
tions near to the rails. As regards sprints down the
6-furlong straight, the public always had a suspicion
that the outside horses had a pull over the crowd
that followed the beaten track. 'The Maribyrnong
Plate, for instance, was frequently won of old by a
youngster that ran on the outside all the way. The
Newmarket Handicap of recent years has rarely fallen
to one of the inside-railers. But the exact reason
why the outside division had an advantage in sprint
races was not generally considered. The public,
being used to noting the fact that horses on the
inside of the circus track had less ground to cover
than the outsiders in long-distance events, or in
sprints with only one turn in them, overlooked the
fact that the shortest way round was also the softest
way home, except in very dry weather. The course
may be levelled and rolled and rolled and levelled
after a day’s racing, but the side that suffers the
more kicking about must always be the side that is
comparatively softened by repairs. It is doubtful
whether, in wet weather, a horse gains an advantage
by saving ground at the turns.
Of late years very little had been heard of
little Tommy Sanders, who won a Melbourne Cup on
Dunlop in the distant past. He came into the lime-
light again at Flemington last Thursday. Dunwil,
an unknown 2-year-old colt trained by him, won
the first and last races on the programme. A clever
coup. The youngster won both events easily.
* « » *
Next to having a nag that drops into the
soup when it has the cash aboard, the most exasperat-
ing thing that can happen to an owner is to have one
which arrives in a hurry and snatches the stake when
he thought something else was going to do it. This
happened two or three times at Rosehill on Saturday.
The imported Snow King was one of the culprits.
His performances generally have been as cheerful as
a recruiting scheme; and when he went out for the
Flying on Saturday among a fairly slick lot, the fact
that he ran nowhere in slower company a fortnight
before made him an unmarketable commodity. It
was something the same with Dame Acre in the
Rydalmere Mile, the supposition in this case being
that the journey was too far. But they both turned
up at the right end amidst frantic applause from Ike.
Dame Acre won without effort, and as she ran the
distance in “4,” with Bst. 121 b. on top, it looks as if
the stable idea about six furlongs being the end of
her tether wanted overhauling, and that with Bst. in
the Doncaster the lady may be entitled to respectful
consideration. Snow King has 7st. 101 b. in the Don-
caster, and the record of having run last in the
Epsom, for which he was a solidly backed second
favorite with Wedge.
* * * *
No sports-meet, automobile accident or
ice-cream sociable is complete in Hobart without
Tommy Tabart. lie is waylaid by tailors, who
endeavor to get wrinkles from his unwrinkled
clothes, and by the
scions of the most
antique families, who
allow him three letters
of his Christian name.
Possessing the assurance
of a High Court Judge,
he is manager of a dozen
industrial and mining
companies, as well as
secretary and chief talker
for the* Speck’s leading
racing body. His boxing
ability prompts him to
butt in, and at the
recent Cup meet he
personally bounced off
the course a couple of
stubborn undesirables.
He was a solid back
when the Speck’s foot-
ball was in its heyday,
and captained more
than one inter-State
cricket team. His own
gee-gees have a habit of
running second ; and last
year Royal Pride filled
that place in the Hobart,
Launceston and Delo-
raine Cups.
TOM TAB ART, Jun.
Secretary of the
Tasmanian Racing
Club.
They stick to the job of trying to get a
race out of the Cooper stable’s expensive importation
Redfern with a persistence worthy of the barrowload
of cash the animal cost. Latterly he has been gallop-
ing soundly enough on the tracks, and on Saturday he
was given a fly in the six furlongs at Rosehill. There
were preliminary yarns about the race being a cer-
tainty, and misguided punters courageously swallowed
them and took 6 to 4. At no stage of the subsequent
proceedings had they a hope.
» * * *
If John Wren started Lansell in the
Maiden at Rosehill last week to get a line on his
Doncaster prospects, there was nothing in what hap-
pened to encourage anybody to back him. A big
commission was unshipped on the colt—and it stayed
where it went. He just scrambled into a place.
* * * *
The hurdlers produced on Sydney sub-
urban courses are often a queer lot, and the things
they do are queerer still. This paper thinks fondly
of being there someday when a field is started
and the rest of the hours are spent waiting
for them to get lifted over the timber.
On Saturday at Rosehill there were 14 starters.
One ran off. Another jerked his rider out of
the saddle. A third inflicted fatal injuries on himself.
And the winner, who wandered forth absolutely
unbacked in the ring, cut the journey out in record
time for the course. The second horse was a dozen
lengths behind, the third about 50 yards off, and the
remainder sprinkled over the landscape like a string
of tired cows. Between an unappreciated record-
breaker, well-backed animals that fell down and
others that looked as if they wanted to, the event
had a variegated aspect. #
Weights for the Doncaster and Sydney
Cup kick off with Cetigne in eacli ease. He get 3 9st.
121 b. in the mile and 9st. 51b. in the distance. Solid
helping though this be, there is not much to grumble
about. But the handicapping of Wedge to within
lib. of Cetigne in the Doncaster seems a rather
uncalled-for proceeding. And there might be reason
for lodging a caveat against placing Panacre above
King’s Bounty and counting horses like Quarale,
Kennaquhair, Hitachi, Rebus and Norbury to be sub-
stantially better over a mile than Petalster or the
Epsom winner Satin Bird. Delivered at the start fit
to do his best, Wolaroi (9st. 51b.) would in all pro-
bability show anything that wanted to win good
reason for being in a hurry. But there are others
likely to be in better form on the day. On paper
nothing looks much better than Wedding Day, Petal-
ster, Frank E and Satin Bird among thri first half.
Taking those on the other side who have done some-
thing, Franzae 7st. 31b., Hem 7st. and Yellowmun 7st.
are possibilities at any rate. Hem with 7st. 91b. won
the Christmas Handicap (eight furlongs) at Randwick
in “38J” without being pushed. The Cup field will
be more understandable when the likely starters can be
noticed and they begin to explain how they like the
faster work of the next fortnight. Lingle 9st. lib.,
Thana Bst. 101 b. and Itandos Bst. 101 b. are not badly
treated, though the time ought to be nearly ripe for
Red Pennant, 7st. 91b., to do something if he is ever
going to.
* * * *
There were 146 horses in the Doncaster
Handicap and 103 in Sydney Cup, and there are
nearly 15,000 ways of grouping them in doubles.
Obviously more than half can be ruled out right
away as certain non-starters. There are probably at
least 60 in the Doncaster that at this stage are pos-
sible triers and certainly a couple of dozen in the
Cup that can be described in the same way. Now,
even if the double-pickers limited themselves to these
—which they don’t —just consider the double mer-
chant on a £lOOO book. He was last week laying
them at 200 to 1. Of course it looks long odds, but
the cold fact is that the figures are miles away from
the chances taken by the punter. In a week or two
numerous takers of these odds will reckon they have
a good bet going, and they will be right. But com-
pared with the number whose bet will have evaporated
or be quotable at a heavy discount, they will be as
about 1 is to 100. The worst-off dealer in doubles is
the individual who selects a couple of rank outsiders
to start with. If 20 to 1 chances win the double, £1
would win £4OO on the day. For the Rosehill Flying
and Handicap on Saturday 100 to 1 could be got
about some of the rough Tins; and if a punter had
backed the first winner arid played up the lot on the
next, he could easily have taken out £2OO or more.
* * * *
Racing prophecies, except the llth-hour
ones, must be always based on the assumption of
firm going. What is called the best form of a racer
is the best performance it has accomplished in a
fast-run race. : The fact that it may have done com-
paratively better on a muddy track merely suggests
that its opponents may have done worse than usual,
owing to the weather. Weather in Australia is nor-
mally favorable to good, sound going, so form is
understood to be calculated on normal conditions.
Therefore the calculation is rather differently based
to that of the English form of imported horses, or
even M.L. animals that have never raced a mile on
ground as hard as iron. On any soft track the size
of a racer’s feet may be as much a matter of import-
ance as its style of action. The moral of which is
that form, the least uncertain guide to punters, is in
itself uncertain, and the odds laid by the bookmakers
are sinfully unequal to the uncertainty. Luck,
roguery among jockeys and the mistakes of trainers
in underworking or overworking their horses for the
races in view are thrown into the game to make it
the more ruinous to outside punters, whereas inside
experts are at least good judges of a horse’s condi-
tion on the day of a race, provided they have no
personal interest in it and are not blinded by
prejudice. Some trainers are notoriously shrewd
tipsters regarding everybody’s horses except their
own. Here and there a jockey has a fair capacity
for comparative criticism, but jockeys mostly remain
“boys” in judgment all their days. Owners, as a
rule, get all their ideas from the trainer and jockey,
who may be equally lacking in judgment. Indus-
trious touts are, perhaps, the best class of tipsters.
They go with frayed edges to the legs of their
pants because they are always betting, and luck
plays them many dirty tricks. They back horses for
crowded races round circular or semi-circular courses
where Luck gets many opportunities to operate. Bad
start, bump, “shut in,” stumble, interference, etc.,
are always probabilities against the punter who backs
the good thing, and in favor of the bookie who lays
it. The laws of chance forbid habitual punting.
Ikey Mo is the law-abiding person who lays ’em.
* * * *
“W.A.C.”: The most exciting item at a
Bananaland bush meeting is the draught-horse race.
Conditions —distance one mile; winner to pull a ton
100 yards. At the Dryrot meeting recently I was invited
into a tip-dray to make up the necessary weight. The
rest of the ton was local Cocky. Dobbin stepped out
to encouraging howls. Then what we didn't expect
happened. When I emerged from the heap of whis-
kers, boots and curses someone whispered in my ear
that he knew the person who loosened the tip-bolt,
and was going to find him. Judging by the crowd in
pursuit of a fleeting form around the track he didn’t
have the secret on his own.
* * * *
“Pick” : The most diminutive jockey ? At
the ’9l Pambula (N.S.W.) race meeting Blackbird,
owned by J.' P. Seceombe, and generally regarded as
an outsider, was so persistently backed to win the
Town Plate that Ikey smelt mice, and started to
lay poison. Seceombe got a whiff of the proceedings
and communed with himself. Then he loaded the
mare with a big stock-saddle and lead, and hoisted
his 31 stone, six-year-old son Edgar on top with
instructions to rush to the front and stay there. The
police began to talk when they noticed the infant
aboard, but as Seceombe claimed he could do as he
liked with his own property, the foorce retired to
read up the law. The race was a pursuit of Black-
bird, and at the finish Israel was smitten with woe.
* * * *
Davon was a big man, six feet four and
about 25st. weight. On the night before the races
at Moonee Valley he and some friends had made a
good dinner at Menzies’, when one of the party
remarked that he had a • pass to the meeting
which he was unable to avail himself of. Was it
any use to Davon ? “Just the identical,” said
Davon. Next day he duly handed the disc to the
gatekeeper, who promptly staggered. “Man alive,”
he gasped, “there are no elephant races here to-
day ! ” Davon’s friend had palmed off on him a
jockey’s nass.
* * * *
“Spreader”: Carbine blood is in the
heavily-backed topweight, as well as the lighter-
weighted actual winner, of the Australian Cup.
Whenever any of Carbine’s descendants get into the
limelight members of the Clerks’ Union and other
horsey men refer in terms of respectful admiration
to the magic Musket strain. They are all wrong.
Camel is the horse that deserves all the credit,
through his daughter Brown Bess. Carbine’s sire
was out of a daughter of Bess. His dam Mersey
was by a son of the same mare, and as Mersey
threw a first-flighter to Musket and another (Car-
nage) to a son of Musket, it looks as if there was
something, after all, in the scientific Brown Bess
nick. The other quadrupeds produced by matings
of Musket and Mersey were such a poor lot that it
would be a shame to include them in any decent
theory on breeding.
*•*#■*
From Malmsbury (Vic.) comes breathless
information that they are about to acquire a race-
course on which to settle soldiers. Nothing novel in
that. Australia is studded with racecourses on
which soldiers have been settled.
* * # *
When Fred Berry (N.S.W.) tried to put
up fresh motor-cycle figures between Sydney and
Melbourne he smashed the forks of his machine at
Wodonga (Vic.). As he was running just on schedule
time he looked about for something to kick for his
bad luck. Nothing being handy, he went on to Mel-
bourne, and after a rest decided to have a tilt at the
record from that end. Last week, with C. Sainty in
a side-car, Berry started on the 565-mile trip, and
despite a 40min. block at Gundagai, pulled the figures
down to 22hr. 20min. Jim Bolger put up the pre-
vious record (23hr. 40inin.) on a solo machine. Berry
was aboard a 7h.p. Harley-Davidson, with Barnet
Glass tyres.
♦ W * *
With two rounds to go in Sydney premier-
ship cricket, Petersham, Balmain and Waverley each
have a chance of hitting the top. The season has
been a see-saw between Petersham and Balmain, each
having won seven matches and lost three; but Waver-
ley, with six wins and four losses, is in the rosier
position with only Gordon to play and a bye. Mean-
time Sydney, which took the prize last year, is giving
Middle Harbor a hand to hold up the tail.
* * * *
C. V. Grimmett (South Melbourne), who
has stirred Victorian cricket this season by taking 40
wickets averaging 13.4 each, was in Sydney in other
years. In premiership matches he took 28 averaging
10.64 during ’l4-’l5 (the best was 12 for 65 against
Redfern), 59 averaging 20.72 in ’l5-’l6, and 20 at
a cost of 17.3 last year.

CONSTANT HEADACHES
People with thin blood are much more sub-
ject to headaches than full-blooded persons,
and the form of anaemia that affects grow-
ing girls is almost always accompanied by
headache, together with digestive disturb-
ances and constipation.
Whenever you have constant or recurring
headaches and pallor of the face they show
that the blood is thin, and your efforts should
be directed towards building up your blood.
A treatment with Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
will do this effectively, and the rich, red
blood will remove the headache.
More disturbances to health are caused
by thin blood than you have any idea of.
When the blood is impoverished the nerves
suffer from lack of nourishment, and you
may have insomnia, neuritis, neuralgia or
sciatica. Muscles subject to strain are
under-nourished, and you may have muscu-
lar rheumatism or lumbago. If your blood
is thin and you begin to show symptoms of
these disorders, try building up the blood
with Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills and see if
the symptoms do not disappear as the blood
is restored to normal.

the Nervous System,” to the Dr. Williams’
Medicine Co., Box 594 G.P.0., Sydney.

pills, or they will be sent, post paid, at 2/9

IMPORTANT.—LOOK TO YOUR TEETH.
There are no teeth like those furnished by Nature. For service, comfort and good
health, artificial teeth cannot be compared with a natural set.
Sensible people will accordingly try to preserve their teeth as long as possible by
keeping them clean. Cold water and soap and water are useful, but somewhat
ineffectual. Rough powders, such as cuttlefish, crabseyes, crude chalks and charcoal
are too severe on the enamel.
EUDENTA TOOTH PASTE is scientifically prepared to clean the teeth, sweeten
the breath, harden the gums and disinfect the mouth (thus preventing decay).
EUDENTA is therefore regarded as “the perfection of dental luxuries.” 1/- tube every-

Things seem to be going nice and smoothly
now with this co., except, of course, that
the war has disturbed the foreign market
into which it was steadily pushing, and in
which, in course of time, Australia ought to
be a very large factor. There was an old
story that Australia couldn’t make leather
because the climate doesn’t suit. But that
excuse for loafing is nearly played out. It
nearly choked off the sheep-breeding busi-
ness in Australia in the first place, and it
did choke off the butter business for many
years—and then Queensland and the N. S.
Wales North Coast, the places which of all
others ought to be the most unsuitable
according to the old lie, proved themselves
easily the most suitable. Now and again,
of course, we get nasty knocks, because the
Australian workman is an independent per-
son who has some opinions of his own, and
we scold him for striking. But we don’t
want to get it into our heads that that is the
end of all our hopes. It may do us a bit of
good sometimes to reflect that in many cases
the qualities that produce strikes are the
very qualities that make superior workmen.
The placid, pattering, pottering Indian
doesn’t strike. The Australian wouldn’t,
either, if he was no better than the Indian.
We have been magnifying our strikes lately
to make political capital out of them ; and
that is no good. Indeed, we have come .to
this point: that the anti-Labor political par-
ties are promoting and intensifying indus-
trial troubles and difficulties for political
purposes. Australia will have to watch this
development very carefully and stamp upon
it hard whenever it shows itself.
The old-established Ludowici’s capital is
£56,644, and its liabilities on all accounts on
December 31 were £11,262. It is a business
in which not much capital is required for
plant (premises and plant run to only
£20,430) ; but stock in various stages takes
£46.353 to support it. The balance of about
£11.500 is in book debts and War Loan and
other investments.
* * * *
W. 11. Twelvetrees, State Geologist of
Tasmania, has published an excellent circu-
lar on “The Search for Petroleum in Tas-
mania.” The history of the hunt for oil is
stated from the first finding of bitumen on
the West Coast to the failure to strike oil
at North Bruny Island with a drilling rig.
The monograph sums up the Tasmanian oil
situation thus: —
The two fields which offer the best off-chance of
discovery of native oil in Tasmania are, as far as at
present known: —•
(1) The Launceston Tertiary basin, in which
rapidly alternating beds of sand, clay and lignite
recur to a proved depth of 900 feet, and probably
persist for a few hundred feet deeper. The organic
muds of these beds are potentially favorable for oil
genesis.
(2) The Gondwana sand beds of the midlands,
where salt-pans are known to exist in various locali-
ties.
Up to the present the Department has no definite
knowledge of undoubted indications, but in view of
the benefits derivable from the opening of a petroleum
field in Tasmania, and in view of the reasonable
assumption that there is nothing exceptional in the
physical characteristics and conditions of sedimenta-
tion which would militate against the formation of
oil, it recommends renewed search for surface evi-
dences, and invites prompt communication of any
discoveries made, in order that no time may be lost
in visiting and examining the sites of alleged finds.
Altogether it is the most encouraging docu-
ment so far issued by any of the Australian
State Geologists about petroleum.
* * * *
“Oof Bird”: An association has been
formed in Perth to revive Westralian mining.
Prospectors, leaseholders, investors, specu-
lators and others who want to see the in-
dustry livened up are invited to join. One
of the main objects is to agitate for the
removal of the disabilities under which those
opening up mines now labor. Affiliation
with the Primary Producers, otherwise the
Country Party, is suggested, but nothing
definite has yet been done. In my opinion
the Association should free itself entirely
of the Melbourne and Adelaide company pro-
moter. Let small syndicates of speculators
be formed to go into mining for themselves.
Why should not the public in Perth or Ival-
goorlie or Cue deal direct with a lease or
group of leases at Tilgarn, East Coolgardie,
Mt. Margaret or the Murchison? These
syndicates must have no vendor’s interest
and should be possessed of substantial work-
ing capital, and their formation on such lines
would probably do more to revive mining
than anything else.
* * * *
The high cost of mining in Westralia is
gradually shutting down the low-grade
mines, and these are a few of the already-
deserted or almost-deserted towns: Peak
Hill,' of Darlington-Simpson fame ; Nannine ;
Jasper Hill, where Hans Irvine’s bubble
burst after six months’ crushing • Day Dawn,
where the Great Fingall is languishing; Mt.
Magnet, where the Morning Star. St. George,
etc., are now closed down; Sandstone (Black
Range and Oroya) ; Lawlers (Great East-
ern) ; Warriedar. Mt. Morgans. Yalgoo
(Emerald). Yum (Yuin Reef; under exemp-
tion). Leonora (now existing on the Sons
of Gwalia), Menzies. Kanowna. Soutl ern
Cross. Cue. Coolgardie. And the list could
bo extended by many others.

Collins-street is being offered interests in
molybdenite shows at Everton. near Wanga-
ratta (Vic.). The mineral occurs in quartz
and granite gangues, and rich specimen stuff
is being passed round by those who cot in
early. This is not the first time of offering;
but hitherto investors have been coy, as word
got about that the field was patchy. Most
molybdenite shows are.

“The soft

LR 2
A little talk on the
care of your lamps
FILL daily—with “Laurel”- so that the reser-
voir may be nearly full when the lamp is
lighted. Not too full —oil, when warm,
expands; allow for expansion. Don’t fill the
lamp when burning.
The contents of the reservoir
should be emptied at least once
a month to remove dust and
settlings.
When the lamp is not burning,
see that the wick is turned down
below the top of the wick tube.
Keep the burners clean— the com-
mon causeof odour is the clogging
of the tiny air holes at the base of
the burner under the cap, and the
crust which forms at the top of
the wick tube. These openings
must be kept free, because they
supply the air, without which,
there could not be proper com-
bustion.
I f the air passages become clogged
the flame will fail and emit an
unpleasant odour.
Boil burners occasionally in a
solution of sal soda and water,
which will soften and aid in re-
moving the crust.
Be sure the wick fits.
For lamps with flat wicks, trim
the wick daily, cutting off the
burned portion evenly across the
top with sharp scissors, and also
slightly at the corners.
The wicks on lamps with a centre
draught, or taking a circular wick,
should not be cut. Turn the wick
up till only the charred portion is
exposed, and rub off evenly from
left to right, always in same
direction.
Wicks should be replaced once a
month, or as soon as they become
dirty. A new wick should be
thoroughly dried before putting
it in the lamp.
If your wick is too short to reach the oil, never put water into
your lamp to raise the oil. If the water reaches wick, it Will
moisten it and prevent proper burning.
Use good kerosene. The best brand is “Laurel
the soft, white light.
Don’t ask for “Kerosene” —say “Laurel ” It is our
new trade mark name for the highest. quality
American kerosene ever sold in Australasia.
Vacuum Oil Company Pty. Ltd*

More about medical fees,«from “Dr. Nil”
this time: —
“A.11.A.” (B. 28/2/’18) wants payment of med-
ical men “by results,” and stoppage of all payment
during illness of patients. I don’t know whether
the lady is aware of it, but this method is laid at
the door of the Chinese monarchs, who have always
been krtown as the most progressive aristocracy in
the most scientific country in the world. Their
example alone should be sufficient to show that
“A.H.A.” is right. But she says at the end of her
letter: “Doubtless there are details that I have over-
looked.” There are ! There are, indeed !
Under the present system the more trifling ail-
ments that people call in the doctor for, the more
will be the bill. So they prevent malingering. Who
in “A.II.A.’s” scheme is to prevent malingering?
It would be to the family’s interest to promote it,
because this (unless bowled out by some independent
tribunal) would secure a stoppage of payment of
all fees to the doctor.
Now, if father is well but drinking hard, and if
mother is well but about to be attended in a con-
finement by a Mrs. Gamp (all risks thrown on the
doctor), if Ethelbert is teething and Ethelberta is
suffering from the attentions of an abortionist —ye
gods, tell me what is the poor man who only lives to
cut up his fellow-idiots to do? Ethelbert’s teeth
would stop poor Pillgarliek’s screw from that family !
As for operations and the cost being borne by
the “family physician,” no medical slave would
think of doing one outside of a Government hos-
pital, where the visitation of God could not always
be revisited on the poor man who must get up at
1 a.m. and dash off to give little Tommy his castor-
oil, even though the barber-surgeon (to this he will
have been dragged back by “A.11.A.”) is ill him-
self and has never been paid by Tommy’s (reputed)
father.
These are only a few of the “details that I have
overlooked.” One other, for instance: What would
the Coroner say if it were implied by con-
tract that it was the doctor’s fault every time a
patient died? Let “A.H.A.” imagine herself that
unknown bird, a woman working a screw-cutting
lathe in Australia. And what would she say if
her wages ceased because there were bubbles in
the castings?
And a lot more.
“L.E.A.” (London) : I take off my hat to
the Australian mother who called to-day to
see about the transfer of her soldier son to
a neutral country. He chased the Hun so
hard that he was prisoner soon after the age
?f 16; a second boy was 16 when he arrived
in London with a later contingent; a third
son, at the age of 15, is in the Navy, and
a smter has gone into a military hospital
Their father was a British officer.

Consider the requisites for an
ideal motor tyre —the quality
of materials needed, the ex-
pert construction, the type of
labor, and the manufacturing
equipment necessary to weld
them effectively together.
Besides these, consider the
factors that have part in the
making of every Goodyear
Tyre—the care spent in
choosing materials, the ex-
perience behind their construc-
tion, the skill of Goodyear
workmen, and the great fac-
tories in which they labor.
We believe you will find the
two —the ideal and the real
—astonishingly in alignment.
We believe you will find
Goodyear Tyres to come
nearer your wish in all things,
than you believed possible.
We build them to embody all
the quality material that we
are able to command through
our vast organization and
improved practice.
We build them to serve in a
manner befitting a product on
which is staked the present suc-
cess and future development of a
great institution.
Through intensified factory me-
thod, and nationally effective
distribution, we are able to mar-
ket them at a price companioning
economy with satisfaction.
Now and henceforward Goodyear
Tyres should be on your car —
by every reason of efficiency and
value.

BOOKS v. TOTE.
WHICH IS BETTER? Sometimes Books, Sometimes Tote.
THE TOTEX, a new invention, tells at a glance without calculation which
is better in each case before the race for any horse, any race, any time, no
matter how many runners, or whether one, two or three dividends paid, or whether
tickets 5/-, 10/-, £l, or £5.
THE TOTEX also tells whether favorite to win or any other horse finishing
second or third is the better investment.
THE TOTEX also tells after the race the approximate dividends payable
for first when one dividend paid, first and second when two dividends paid, and
first, second and third when three dividends paid.
THE TOTEX thows light on investing, and each investor knows which
institution will pay best in each case before the race.
THE TOTEX is indispensable now it is an accomplished fact. No more
careless waste of money in taking 6-1 from the books when the Tote paid 9-1 as
in Bombita’s Squatters, and Tote 6-1 as in Pah King's Clibborn, when Books gave
12-1.

The film rights of Dennis’s “Sentimental
Bloke” have been disposed of for a sum
running into four figures.
“Cockaigne”: Forbes Robertson, engaged
by Hugh Ward for Australia, was notably
associated with the Bancrofts and Irving,
and developed slowly into a star. His Ham-
let was the best on the English stage after
the retirement of Booth and Irving. Sym-
pathy distinguishes him; wherefore his
trump card has been “The Passing of the
Third Floor Back,” introduced to Australia
by Matheson Lang. Robertson made a for-
tune from this in the United States. Another
trump is “The Light that Failed,” not seen
in Australia for a long time. He will draw,
although he has retired from the English
theatres. His wife, Gertrude Elliott, is
Maxine’s sister.
The Maoriland Government has appointed
veteran Tom Follard adviser to the Minister
of Inteimal Affairs in regard to the censor-
ship of cino films. The first job he had was
to fix up a scheme for exhibiting the official
war pictures, which have hitherto been in
the hands of the Y.M.C.A. without conspicu-
ous success. The old general’s appointment
seems to have pleased everybody. It is the
second time he has been in Government show
employ. He was Director of Entertain-
ments in the M.L. International Exhibition
of 3906-7.
“W.P.T.”: Peter Dawson’s enlistment re-
minded me of poor old Tom Dawson, whose
grave is in France. I’ll never forget when
he was up for promotion. “Come out and
drill this squad,” said the 0.C.; “I’ll see
how you shape.” Tom was told to open the
squad out. The word of command is,
“Odd Numbers Front Rank, Two Paces For-
ward ; Even Numbers Rear Rank, Two Paces
Step Back; March.” Tom got slightly
mixed, but threw his chest out and shouted in
his best voice: “Odd Numbers Front Rank,
Two Paces Forward ; Even Money the Rear
Rank.” He got his stripe.
The names of IS members wbo figure in
the cast of the war drama are inscribed on
an honor board which was unveiled at Mel-
bourne Greenroom Club by Bert Bailey.
Two of the number —Max Clifton, who took
part in a hundred stage scraps, and comedian
Tom Dawson—will never return to the
footlights.
“Fax”: Emile Vanderveldt tells the story.
Five thousand Petrograd factory hands, who
were already getting three times their former
wages, demanded of the firm that the in-
crease should date back to the beginning of
the war. The total back pay was 36 million
roubles, and the hopeful workers brought
13 sacks to the conference. These were to
be filled with cash within 24 hours, other-
wise the honest unionists would fill them
with the 13 directors and throw them into
the Neva. But whilst they were locked in
the directors sent a message to the Minister
of Labor, and he came round and convinced
the 5000 workmen that the retrospective rise
was an impossibility. Whereupon they
politely withdrew their claim and begged the
bosses’ pardon for their little mistake. It
reminds me of two showmen, long dead and
gone, who had to deal with a little crowd
from Switzerland. Pete Hughes and Jenkins
brought the Jungfrau Kapelle jodellers to
Australia on a contract which guaranteed
them return passage to Europe at the end
of the tour. There was no specified length
of time ( for the tour, but it turned out an
unexpected boon and lasted a year or so. The
Swiss rustics in velveteen and cross-garters
had never before earned so much salary. The
only cloud on their happiness was the loss
of one of the brethren, who had died before
he was able to earn much salary, and was
duly buried by the management. When,
at last, they came to receive their return-
passage money a spirit of unrest was ap-
parent among the musical aliens, and Hughes
and Jenkins wondered what was in the wind.
They quickly found out. As soon as the last
songster was paid off, the jodellers all re-
turned to the office in a deputation to
demand the passage-money of their defunct
comrade!
“0.K.”: “Turn to the Right,” according
to managerial ad., “comes like a breath of
fresh air from the mountains.” Quite so.
The prologue consists of conversation con-
cerning gaol and burglary between three
young men in a pawnshop, two greasy old
Jews being in the background. The Ist Act
ends with a droll case of safe-breaking and
pocket-picking, followed by the departure of
the “crooks” to a prayer-meeting. Act 2
terminates in the arrest of an educated felon
for persistently robbing his old man. The
drama is full of mountain air.

of 1917) from France, where he has had a
long spell of soldiering:—
Loved ones ’neath the wattle-trees
Cull this message from the breeze:
Next year from across the seas
Christmas brings us home.

“F.H.”: Back to Melbourne, after spend-
ing one-seventh of his life at the Front and
sharing the glory of Messines Ridge and
Broodseinde, young Bill West, a Wesley
boy who earned his pre-war crust with the
A.M.P. Bill belongs to a family which has
made a vast amount of mixed music in its
time. Dad Hautrie West holds the record
as long-time double-bass player at the Tiv.
- —-22 years of it. Nowadays he is at the
Princess’s. Uncle Haydn West has a useful
reputation as a piano-practitioner and writer
of tone-poems; while grand-dad Hautrie
West, of Ballarat, teaches the young musical
idea how to shoot in the big-organ gallery.
Dad and uncle both did orchestra work with
Marshall Hall in the kultured days of the
old Conservatorium.

Snowy Baker qualifies as an Australian

“Ancient Prompter”: Australia’s old, not
to say venerable, footlights friend Nat
Goodwin has come to light again on the
American stage. He is starring, in a comedy
entitled “Why Marry?” Nathaniel, who
has been married and divorced more times
than any man on the boards, knows how to
make the best of his misfortunes.
“Bluebeard”: Whilst away from Aus-
tralia, Muriel Starr acted “on Broadway”
—every mummer’s ambition. She was
Lady Langworthy in May Martindale’s play,
“Gamblers All,” at the Maxine Elliott
Theatre. The authoress is the daughter of
the writer of “Jim tbe Penman,” made popu-
lar in this country by Harry St. Maur.
Ronald Squire, an English actor, and Muriel
got the best notices of May’s poor play,
which hobbles round a woman gambler in
difficulties. Daisy Belmore, who was in
Australia with Wilson Barrett, and married
one of the local Waxman family, was also in
the cast. One New York critic’s idea of the
show: “A prehistoric ark afloat on an ante-
diluvian flood of prattle.” It hqfl a short
but stormy voyage.
“Lab”: A recent par. about a much-
travelled veteran mummer led me to search
for a flimsy green handbill, and a night of
drizzle came back to me. Mr. and Mrs.
were billed to give a performance on
the only famous goldfield in India, and two
Cornishmen and I tramped three miles to see
it. The place required special advertising
to secure anything of a “house,” a fact
evidently unknown to the visitors. We
arrived to find that we three would probably
constitute the audience; but after a time
two or three Eurasian lads came along.
Under the circumstances w T e had given up
all idea of a performance, and had not both-
ered to go inside. We sat chatting
on the doorkeeper’s table (there was no
doorkeeper) waiting for the drizzle to stop.
About the time when the show should have
started, the veteran joined us, and that
man was a sport. He hadn’t a grouse about
him —merely said in a pleasant way some-
thing about bad luck. We proceeded to
explain local conditions and how the game
ought to be played. There was no hotel,
and one of the dressing-rooms was obviously
being used as such. After allowing any pos-
sible late-arrivals a feyv minutes’ grace, the
mummer suggested that it was time to be
“getting on with the show.” We said we
would not hear of it, and were quite content
to go and let him get to bed (in the dressing-
room) ; but he was not prepared to agree to
that, and after some argument, very pleas-
antly conducted, suggested that we should
toss for it—the winner to do as he liked.
He won the toss, and said, with cheerfulness
apparently : “Well, that means we give the
show.” So the audience of six trooped in,
and the mummer and his wife (the only
attractions billed) conscientiously went
through the programme. The audience was
very appreciative. Since then I’ve often
wondered whether he was so fond of getting
blank-yerse off his chest that any old oppor-
tunity would do, or did he (poor soul, I hope
not!) need the money?
“J.F.11.” (London) : “The Maid of the
Sunny South” is announced as the principal
Christmas production at Manchester; and as
Oscar Asclie is the producer there may be
something Australian in it. But more likely
there isn’t, Spain and Italy being the Sunny
South from the viewpoint of the Fog. Asche
produced the enormously successful “Maid of
the Mountains” at Daly’s, London, so its
boss, Robert Evett, naturally secured his
services for the similar Manchester show.
THE ACTOR’S AVIARY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Said Hyphen-Howler, once a star, but now
A dim, obscure and faded satellite:
“They will not have me act, but, anyhow,
I am an artist still; I can recite!
“Since melodrama uses me so ill,
A lower plane my art shall glorify;
Pll quit the stage and take to vaud-e-ville I
Yes, Bernhardt did it, damme! So shall I!”
A scone from Shakspeare, passionate, profound,
A fitting medium for his turn supplied—
llis turn! He shuddered at the very sound!
And so did all the managers he tried.
He kept on pestering them from day to day,
Till one, more sympathetic than the rest,
Promised a trial at a matinCe,
To put his great recital to the test.
At making ready for the fateful hour
Old Hyphen-Howler happily perspired,
And sought the menial —misanthropic, sour- 1 —
Who furnished any “properties” required.
“My man,” said he, with condescending grace,
“I’Ve here the list of props, you must supply !”
The caitiff made an impolite grimace,
And scanned the paper with a bilious eye.
“ ‘Best furniture. Some statues. Rugs,’ ”he read—
“ ‘A handsome carpet, covering all the stage.
Rich curtains. Pictures.’ Very well!” he said,
“But, bli’-me, Mister, you’ve forgot the cage!”
The actor frowned. “What need I with a cage?”
Then Props, relit a damaged cigarette.
“You’ll need a big ’un, matey, I’ll engage,
You’ll want it f’r the bird you’re goin’ to get!”
N.S.W.
NORMAN CAMPBELL.
THE HYPOCHONDRIACTRESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Though on the stage, unknown to fame
Was Mary Jones, who seemed to lack
All talent; but she made a name
By turning hypochondriac.
We had not heard aught of her art
Until we read about her ills;
The lady really got a part
By taking Screecham’s Liver Pills.
Next simple citizens were told,
Beneath her photo, in the press,
That she was ne’er without a cold
Till Scottish Moss cured her distress.
Her name was blazoned far and wide
As one who suffered from catarrh ;
Then Podger’s Nasal Douche she tried,
And very soon became a star.
Craig’s Bunion Plasters helped her, too;
Tubb’s Magic Tonic did its bit;
Each week her public larger grew,
Till now she’s an undoubted “hit.”
Though it may cause all thinkers grief,
Playgoers have, in point of fact,
Been “drugged” into the quaint belief
That Mary Jones can really act!
VICTORIA.

Size does not of itself constitute greatness.
An industry is as great as its service to
the public.
But size does usually constitute oppor-

London's Beauty Writers.
Weekly Selections from Noted Beauty
Experts Writing for the English Press.
Simple and Effective Methods.
By Olga Amsdell, Special Correspondent,
London, Eng.
The Killing of Superfluous Hair.
It is easy to remove superfluous hair tem-
porarily, but to remove it permanently is
quite another matter. Not many women
know that for this purpose such a simple
substance as powdered pheininol may be
used, applied directly to the hair. The recom-
mended treatment is designed not merely to
instantly remove the hair, but also to event-
ually kill the roots entirely. Almost any
chemist could supply an ounce of pheminol,
which quantity should be sufficient.
Renewing Complexions by Absorption.
If your complexion is marred with eallow-
ness, moth patches, pimples or freckles, it is
useless to potter with powders and paints,
lotions, creams and things in an effort to get
rid of the trouble. Unless you have some
ability as an artist, you will mar your ap-
pearance still more. The new and rational
way is to take off the complexion itself, with
all its offensive marks. Just get some pure
mercolized wax at the chemist’s and use at
night the same as cold cream. Remove next
morning with water and soap, following with
dash of cold water. The mercolized wax
absorbs the half-dead scarf skin in flaky
particles, so gradually that no one guesses
you are treating your face—unless it be by
(lie result, which is truly wonderful. There
is nothing like it for bestowing a natural,
healthy and beautiful complexion.
A “Blackhead” Secret.
Blackheads, oily skins and enlarged pores
usually go together, but can be instantly
corrected by a unique new process. A tablet
of stymol, obtained from the chemist, is
dropped in a tumbler of hot water, which will
then of course “fizz” briskly. When the
effervescence has subsided the face is bathed
with the stymol-cliarged water and then dried
with a towel. The offending blackheads, of
their own accord, come right off on the
towel, the large oily pores immediately con-
tract and efface themselves naturally. There
is no squeezing, forcing, or any drastic,
action. The skin is left uninjured, smooth,
soft and cool. A few such treatments should
be taken at intervals of three or four days
thereafter in order to insure the permanence
of the pleasing result so quickly obtained.
Don’t Have Grey Hair.
Grey hair is often a serious handicap to
both men and women while still in the prime
of life. Hair dyes are not advisable because
they are always obvious, inconvenient and
often down-right injurious. Pew people know
that a very simple formula, which is easily
made up at home, will turn the hair back to
a natural color in a perfectly harmless man-
ner. You have only to get two ounces of
taramalite concentrate from your chemist and
mix it with three ounces of bay rum to prove
this. Apply this simple and harmless lotion
for a few nights to the hair with a small
sponge, and the greyness will gradually dis-
appear. The lotion is neither sticky nor
greasy and has been proved over and over
again for generations past by those in pos-
session of the formula.
About Shampoos.
To quote an eminent London beauty
specialist: “The slimy egg cannot dissolve
the scalp impurities, but only adds to the
trouble by completely choking the pores with
animal matter.” The substance sticks, de-
composes, thereby causing the hair to assume
a dead, “dull color.” , Avoid eggs (and soap
too) on your hair. Try the delightful pre-
paration made by stirring a teaspoonful of
stallax in a cup of hot water. It will bring
out new beauty possibilities in your hair,
will clear and prevent dandruff, and leave
that fine fluffy effect so much sought after.
It also removes excess oil or greasiness. [1

14th March, 1918. FORREST, Treasurer.
Norman Marshall, son of Melbourne’s
reverend doc., who went into Broadmeadows
as a recruit in 1914, is now a lieut.-colonel
and wears the M.C. and D.S.O. He was
married last year in London to a daughter
of Dr. Black, of St. Kilda-road. For a son-
in-law Dr. Black has one of the most popular
men in the A.I.F.
“R.W.” from a sloppy trench: —
We’ve just heard the result of the Referendum.
Lord knows what’s going to happen now ! I suppose
we’ll just have to keep on splitting up until there's not
enough left to split, and then turn it in. Some wag
said that they’d only need two ships to take the
Australians home —one for the staff and the other for
the identification discs.
Donald Mackinnon, M.L.A., Director-Gen-
eral of Recruiting in Victoria, has stated in
the Botanical Garden of Ballarat that any-
body who obstructs Australia’s war work
must go. Well, the prize obstructionist is
something of a goer. He goes out in the
Government motor-car. He goes to the
country now and then. He goes the pace
in the matter of loans. He goes the whole
hog in the matter of speeches. He goes
almost everywhere to see if a royal com-
mission is needed. He goes into details to
show that the country can’t do without him.
And once he did go—but it proved next day
that he wasn’t gone.
The crackling successes of General
Cbauvel, of the Australian Light Horse,
recall the part his father, C. H. Chauvel,
owner of Tabulam, played in a smaller
shooting drama in ’6B, at Drake (N.S.W.).
“Yarry” and “Neddy,” two aboriginals,
charged with the murder of a white man
on Bonalbo, were in the lockup on remand.
The constable, on drawing the bolt to give
the prisoners their breakfast, was over-
powered, and the blacks made off for the
scrub. A young bushman gave chase and
promptly shot both fugitives dead. Three
J’s.P., Chauvel, Robertson and Ellis,
solemnly returned the verdict, “Shot in self-
defence by T. Marsh.”
“Hamer”: There are periodical shakings
of the military tree to jolt the hundreds of
shy soldier-birds from their snug English
nests, and get them over to France. But
it’s a heartbreaking job. The official shaker
goes into orderly-rooms, stores, offices, cook-
houses, post offices, mess-huts and canteens,
and the slackers tremble —until they get
used to it. Just when it seems that a gun-
shy flock are caught at last, a crop of harm-
less new jobs springs up, and, with sighs of
relief, the slackers fall back into them.
Amongst the other things that have just
been found necessary in one Australian
training group in Britain is a concert party
—members to be given exemption from ser-
vice for three months. Oh, why didn’t I
persevere with my singing lessons years ago!
How long does it take to learn the bones?
“Maphsu”: Here is a tale of two Russian
Jews, a chemist and a dentist respectively.
They were in America at the time the Czar
lost his job, and, with high hopes of finding
a free Russia, started off to reach their
country via Japan. When they arrived in
Nippon, however, Russia had run wild, so
they decided instead to come to Australia.
The passports issued to them in America by
the consul of a Russia that was then officially
republican, were vised by the British em-
bassies in Hong Kong and Japan. When
the travellers reached Sydney the other day
they were practically broke. Now what
stupid official is responsible for -what hap-
pened next? Their passports, vised, were
dishonored, because they did not bear the
Russian Imperial arms! The two men were
not allowed to land and are to be sent back
to' Japan as steerage passengers.
From “The Oldest Inhabitant” :—•
Apropos the burial the other day of Melbourne’s
patriarchal undertaker Apps. The pioneer of the
gloomy business in the city was Robert Frost, car-
penter and joiner, who commenced to look sad in
Collins-street at the end of 1838, This Bob Frost
married the widow of John Shanks, who kept a pub in
Flinders-street. Frost was alive, to my knowledge,
in the late ’sixties. The next was Samuel Crook,
who put on the weepers in January, 1840, in Wil-
liam-street, near the wharf. He built a hearse,
which he announced would be let out on hire. Then
came Thomas Croft, who, as his “card” said, “com-
bines the office of sexton and undertaker and per-
forms funerals on the shortest notice and most
reasonable terms.” He added a “mourning hearse”
to the equipment. After him came Tom Jennings,
who kept at it till 1884. In the first ’fifties Crook
took a partner, John Sleight, and that name con-
tinues to this day. About the same time came John
Daley, who had all the R.C. business. The late
Apps’s big competitor was William George Raven,
of Fitzroy and Collingwood. He “worked” the
friendly societies.
Made his last flight, in Mesopotamia, on
February 19, Captain C. A. .Parker, M.C.
and bar. He was a Launceston (Tas.) boy,
but being in Queensland when Germany
decided that the time was ripe he enlisted in
the Light Horse. He was with the First
Division on Gallipoli, and then after a few
months with the Artillery was picked by
Birdwood for the Flying Corps. In this work
he gained his captaincy, the M.C. and bar,
and was then transferred to the R.F.C.
Special Reserve for service in Mesopotamia.
He was the only son of Erskine Parker, the
T.T.C. handicapper, and was just 26.
How an Australian padre died
On November 7 the infantry failed in an attack
on Sheria, some six or eight miles from Beersheba,
and the Light Horse were ordered to attack. During
the charge a number fell, and one, Trooper Foster,
was seen by the chaplain, W. J. Dunbar, to rise and
attempt to I'egain the lines. In doing so he
drew the Turkish fire and almost immediately fell.
Once more he struggled to his knees. Then Dunbar,
calling for a stretcher-bearer to follow him, charged
out to bring the struggling man in. Before going
many yards the padre himself was dropped, shot
through the thigh. Managing to rise, however, he
again pushed on towards Foster. He reached him,
too, but found him already dead. Just as he and
the bearer were on the point of returning, a machine-
gun was turned on them. Dunbar fell, riddled.
Dodd, the stretcher-bearer, was also wounded, but
managed to get back to the lines. This padre
Dunbar was a native of Orange, and was shepherding
at Tilba Tilba before he got into khaki—as a pri-
vate to begin with. To panegyrise a man like that
is superfluous. His record is the only praise he needs.

Hupmobile Breaks Fremantle to Sydney Record by
45 hours, 18 minutes.
On Tuesday, January 29, at 8.15 a.m., Messrs. Burton and Smith, of West Australia, left Fremantle with a
STOCK MODEL HUPMOBILE in an attempt to lower the existing record between Fremantle and Sydney.
Below is appended the distances, times and previous times between the various towns.

AVERAGE MILES PER HOUR EN ROUTE, 15.7 M.P.H.
Left Fremantle 8.15 a.m. on January 29, arrived Sydney February 5 at 12.32 p.m.
FREMANTLE TO PORT AUGUSTA, 1538 Miles, in 93 hours 55 minutes; Average, 16.3 miles per hour.
PORT AUGUSTA TO BROKEN HILL, 404 miles in 38 hours; Average, 10.6 miles per hour.
BROKEN HILL TO DUBBO, 488 miles, in 28 hours 5 minutes; Average, 17.3 miles per hour.
DUBBO TO SYDNEY, 248 miles, in 10 hours 17 minutes; Average, 24 miles per hour.
FREMANTLE TO SYDNEY, TOTAL MILEAGE 2677 MILES, in 7 days 2 hours 17 minutes.
AVERAGE MILEAGE PER GALLON OF BENZINE, 15.7.
FROM PORT AUGUSTA MR. BURTON LEFT THE BEATEN TRACK, via Adelaide and Melbourne, and
struck out across the desert and WASHOUTS FOR BROKEN HILL. The nature of this country can be
imagined when it is shown above that it took 38 hours to do 404 miles on an average of only 10.6 miles per hour.
It is worthy of note that the only spares carried by the motorists were a Crown heel, a Stub Axle and two Main Spring Leaves, none of
which were needed. They depended entirely on their Electric Installation for Lights.
The whole performance speaks volumes for Hupmobiße Reliability and Service.
