THE thing that the people should have
been allowed to realise from the outset
is that WE MAY GET A LICKING. We
still may get a licking. Are you, going to
let the Ravisher of Belgium win without
lifting a hand?
J. L. Williams, Under-Secretary of N. S.
Wales Justice Department, is now a captain
and a weird thing called a Deputy-Assistant-
Judge-Advocate-General.
One Queensland candidate, Lieutenant
Little (Nationalist), is blind and crippled
through wounds received at the war. Inci-
dentally, a number of others are blind and
crippled, though they didn’t go to the war.
G. H. C. Meyer, an old identity of
Adelaide, came from Hanover in his early
days, and on his recent death an ancient will
was found to leave, after a lot of bequests
to Adelaide charities and to Australian
friends and relatives, the residue to people
in Ilunland. Of course they won’t get it
while the war is on; what happens when
international law is restored remains to be
seen.
A dreadful thing has happened. There
are two episcopal aprons in Gippsland—one
the Anglican Bishop of Gippsland’s; the other
the R.G. Bishop of Sale's. Now, a local
Anglican flock, having decided to replace its
old wooden temple by one of brick, called
in “Gippsland” to lay the foundation stone;
and it wasn’t till it was all over that some-
body noticed that, according ,tp the inscrip-
tion, the job was done by' the Carthlic
potentate —the Bishop of Sale.,
Major W. A. D. Smith, of Molong (N.S.W.),
who has got the D.S.O. after good service in
Gallipoli, Egypt and the Holy Land, is a
son of Wallace Smith, who was the fifth
son of John Smith, M.L.C., who married
(September 12, 1842) Mary, eldest daughter
of “Parson” Tom, one of the earliest Metho-
dist local preachers out West. John Smith,
Cornishman, gave all his sons fancy names —
Fergus Ja'go (M.L.C.), Lancelot Noel,
Edwin Ambrose, Claudius, and Wallace
Arabin. The eldest of his daughters married
the late Justice Innes.
Captain Burkett, the Victorian recruiting
spruiker, has a bright young brother, Lieut.
T. Burkett, in John Bull’s Flying Corps. The
lad lately won the Military Cross for a
pretty bit of work over the German lines.
Getting cut off from seven companions, he
seemed a safe subject for Frightfulness by
a covey of German machines that rose from
the ground. But young Burkett became
rude, shot down two Huns after skilful
manoeuvring, and though he and his observer
were wounded, managed to sneak his little
’plane back to safety. Burkett was a gunner
in Gallipoli and France before be developed
a fancy for a higher existence.
“Centurion” : While the Horse regi-
ments were on Gallipoli, their nags in Egypt
were looked after by native syfes, bossed by
“Old Ali,” head jemadar, ex-Soudamesh
N.C.0., who had served with Hicks Pasha
and Gordon, and later with Kitchener. Ali
was an expert at the diagnosis of malinger-
ing, of which there was more than plenty.
The Walers were fat as butter and fresh,
and there were some casualties; and Ali
seemed to separate them from the frauds
mainly with a vast rhinoceros-hide koorbash.
Three were paraded before me one morning.
“Well, Ali,” I said, “what do you think’s up
with this lot?” Ali spat. “All damn right,
sah. Only Mahomet he musquash [no good],
Achmet he magnoon [idiot], and Abdul, this
one, he fool.”
“0.K.”: Speculation as to the origin of
the popular cockneyism, “I don’t think,”
brings one up against the fact that “rotten”
is a very ancient term of abuse. Every
pedantic person within the past 40 years
has railed at the inanity of such as spoke of
“a rotten day” or “rotten luck”; and no
university professor has failed to gird at the
slangy creatures who describe most things
and conditions of life as “rotten.” Yet the
modern vulgarian rarely stretches the mean-
ing of the word as far as Shakspeare did.
And the Immortal Bard didn’t use “rotten”
as a mere colloquialism. He put into a
sonnet: —
Why didst thou promise such a beauteous day,
.And matte me travel forth without my cloak
;r Bo let base clouds o’ertake me in my way,
Hiding thy bravery in. their rotten smoke?
That’s Immortal William singing to the sun,
if you please! I’m not yet On the track of
“blooming,” but I have a notion that Noah
often referred to the Flood as that sort of
nuisance.
“Narroc” : A while before the war I was
travelling to the Fog on a famous liner,
whose skippah was the snobbiest of his
breed. Two of the passengers, one an old
squatter and .the other a quiet sort named
Smith] got more than their fair share of
insults from the captain. When we arrived
at Gib. the naval bosses signalled the ship
to stop, as they were sending a launch out.
The skippah was dashed annoyed, at the
impertinence and was going to find out who
ordered the outrage. Smith quietly re-
marked : “I did, Captain.” “You, Mr.
Smith! You!” snorted the captain. “And
who are you to give such orders?” By this
time the launch had arrived and the officer
in charge showed profound respect to Smith,
who with the old squatter was preparing
to go ashore. They kept the blessed liner
waiting nearly three hours before they re-
turned. Then someone got a tip from one
of the launch’s crew that “Smith” was
Prince Louis of Battenberg.
A varied citizen passed out in Tassy a
-few , days ago. Lewis P. Collins was a
native of England, and was apprenticed to
a surveyor in London. Next he appeared
in Tasmania and did some farming on the
North-West Coast. Then he managed the
Launceston Examiner’s branch office at
Burnie. From there he moved to Launces-
ton, and had a spell as sub-editor of the
Examiner. Turning from the Inky Way,
Collins entered the debt-collecting business,
joined Tinning Bros, and took over the
Launceston branch of the Tasmanian Trade
Protective Institute. Later on he founded
Collins’s Mercantile Association, and pre-
pared such an intelligent system that ; the
defaulter had very little chance. In spare
time, Collins was a bowler, many years secre-
tary of the Launceston Bowling Club, prac-
tically the founder of the Tasmanian Bowl-
ing Association and its first secretary. He
was 61 when the clock stopped. A few
months ago his second son. Vivian, after
passing through Duntroon, was killed within
a brief day or two of his arrival in France.

Pen of Mine.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PEX of mine, pen of mine,
I will give you ink for wine
And white paper for your play
All a merry, windy day,
When pond waters come lip-lapping
On the grass
And the little leaves are tapping
On the window as they pass.
I will give you ink for wine
Till you dream a fancy fine,
Ben of mine, pen of mine!
Pen of mine, the world js grey,
Making dead men all the day,
Stamping sorrow in the clay ;
And a bitter chorus runs
From the muzzles of the guns—
Death is early, death is late!
In his garments of black hate
Running youth and beauty after,
llow he slays the mirth and laughter
Of the day!
Let us play,
Pen of mine.
1 will give you ink for wine
Till your starry fancies shine
With the lilt in every ray.
Pen of mine, peri of mine,
We’ll go gipsyipg again
Over lands and blowing brine
To our castles in old Spain,
To the places that we know
Where the yellow lilies grow,
And the little lutorStrings quiver
To the dimples of the river,
While the old delicious note
Pulses from the whitest throat
All the night till morning-shine.
Pen of mine, pen of mine,
There is laughter in the wine !
Pen of mine, the days return ;
Happier suns are still to burn.
Of our years the (latest comer
Shall bring back the first white summer.
Shrill your pipes forever, Pan,
As when pinkest dryads ran
Blowing kisses red and rude
To the satyrs in-the wood
And the frolic thought went free
Laughing over A ready.
Pen of mine, pen of mine,
Drown the Gadarean swine!
Pen of mine, the Spanish Main
Sees the silver ships in train.
Fare we forth from Bristol town,
Bold to take and bold to drown.
Swings old time upon its hinge—•
Philip’s beard is good to singe;
Drake will answer to his drum
Where they serve the reddest rum.
Westward ho ! and eastward ho !
Kiss me thrice before we go !
Homeward bound ! The Asian air
Tingles to the spices rare.
We shall find an Arab tent
Wider than a continent
Till, with all our canvas free.
We round the bend of memory,
Seeking with our jolly crew
That first music laughter blew.
Blow, Pan, blow! And here’s the Devil
Come hot-foot to grace the revel
With the clown and pantaloon
And elfin dances ’neath the moon,
Orpheus and the muses nine
And Venus smiling from the brine,
With Prester John and Paul and Peter
And Rosamund, with Hal to greet her,
And jolly friar and modern ranter
And Quilp and Punch and Tam O’Shanter—
Blow high, blow low!
The world shall go
To the mad dance it used to know!
For pen of mine, pen of mine,
There’s wine in ink and ink in wine.
x.s.w.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
Trouble.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
METCALFE knew there would be
trouble: there always was when
Harrison was absent. The premo-
nition was born at San Jos6 railhead
when Harrison turned in his tracks and
went back to Buenos Aires, obediently to the
Chief’s laconic telegram. It grew with the
inexplicable death of two pack mules and
the desertion of three peones two nights ago.
Bad luck, bad luck!
He stood at the flap of his tent,' the dark
foreboding beside him and all about him the
Argentine night. Velvet-thick, black; a
swooning dust of tangible stars six inches
from a man’s head; the sullen glow of
Santa Maria’s lights a mile away, and to the
right Challis’s tent and the bundle of rags
and sticks that was the habitat of the
peones. And sand —and more sand.
Over the windless waste of hellish heat
and sand the new line to Neuquen was
to run, tying up that prosperous centre with
San Jose, taking the Uriburi estancias and
the Schofield group on its way. It would
carry much profitable freight, this new line,
over this waste of sand. Sand in the central
pin socket of the only theodolite, sand in his
ears and eyes and hair; sand surely by now,
he thought, in his immortal soul —red sand
that would find for itself a way within the
shell of an unlaid egg. Damn the place and
the job and the railway! Damn the lot!
Young Challis was the best chainman in
the service—quick and eager; good head-
piece ; strong as a horse; work from dawn
to dark in 120 deg., and do it for months
laughing. A qualified assistant-engineer, he
followed Metcalfe into the interior- —all be-
cause of a row one night down by the Boca, at
a dirty cinema show of which Challis had ex-
pressed his opinion rather freely in bad
Spanish, having dined also rather freely. The
sudden Spanish knife had shone for a second
in the moonlit side street and buried its
gleam in Challis’s shoulder. Metcalfe, who
had not dined freely "and who spoke better
Spanish, got out of a passing cab, and in a
few words and in a few moments had emp-
tied the street., lie took Challis to his rooms
and dressed the superficial wound —and
talked. And at the end of his talk Challis,
a lonely boy, under a five years’ contract
with the Railway hopefully signed in Lon-
don and bitterly repented in Buenos Aires,
would have walked barefoot into Hell at
Metcalfe’s nod.
The qualities that make the successful
engineer on “interior preliminaries” are an
unusual possession; the combination of all
of them is miraculous. A man must be of a
gay courage, ready and quick on his feet in
incredible emergencies. He should work
urbanely and courteously, with violent death
o:a both sides; he must know his business,
that no hint of uncertainty reach the rank
and file ; and he must work hard that no pos-
sibility of their working less hard enter
their minds. Above all, he should have the
trick of popularity, of the regard of his fel-
lows, above and below and on his own level,
white and black and mixed.
Most of these qualities Challis had in good
nieasure, added to buoyant youth ; and Met-
calfe was glad to have him with the camp—-
very glad. He filled a place in Metcalfe's
mind (in his heart, if you like) that should
have been otherwise filled ten years agone.
If a man wander the waste places and there-
in build, no fireside is his, no quiet beloved
voices, no e-rowing minds to watch.
When the expedition reached Santa Maria,
a dirty little half-baked desert town, with
the usual Spanish distractions to offer, Chal-
lis in buoyant youth had “subbed” twenty-
five dollars and leave of absence for the
evening. The Head Office rule forbidding
“subs” was religiously and consistently
broken on every expedition whenever a town
or a village or a collection of huts was
sighted; it was a rule quite impossible to
keep, Metcalfe knew —quite impossible. If
leave and “sub” were refused, the men bor-
rowed from the peones and took French
leave, the morale of the camp disappearing
automatically.
The leave was given very reluctantly, but
without comment. Metcalfe was not a fool
-—he knew how far he could go even in the
boots of the “engineer-in-command,” and he
knew very well that any “advich” would
defeat its object.
Challis became drunk, of course; that was
to be expected. Men in buoyant youth do
not endure the hardships of an Argentine
“preliminary” without looking gluttonously
on the rare fleshpot. He came stumbling
and singing over the sand at break of dawn,
with a long fumbling, rambling story of a
woman and a dinner and sundry auventures,
more or less creditable, and all apparently
humorous. The cream of the joke, he con-
fided to Metcalfe, was that the lady was the
Mayor’s wife, and that the Mayor had per-
sonally interrupted the (more or less credit-
able) adventures.
Metcalfe grabbed Challis and steadied him
past the peones' camp into his tent; ripped
off his collar and coat and threw him on to
tlie stretcher; went back to his own tent re-
solved that no “back-sight” or “fore-sight,”
no “rise” or “fall” should keep the camp
long by Santa Maria, lie had rushed through
the absolutely necessary work, and now all
was in readiness to strike camp at crack of
day—they would push on with all possible
speed, he tnought; and the dark foreboding
turned and looked at him again.
The heavy night shimmered and burned
about him; the horrible stars leaned on his
skull with intolerable weight. The place
was pregnant with menace, throbbing with
some evil buried here with an Aztec sinner
long agm Santa Maria winked and leered
dully across the mile of sand; the peones
chattered and quarrelled over some idiotic
card game. Challis still slept the deep sleep
of the tired riotous, and the night boiled and
stifled about the camp, like a hot wet hand
about a man’s windpipe.
Metcalfe cursed the place and the job
again roundly, as he turned back to his
tripod table and maps. Better chuck it up
altogether after this job was done, he
thought. He v r as getting old or had the
wrong temperament —had lost his nerve, or
something. Sick of it, sick
A cry shuddered up into the night, tear-
ing its way in agony through the stars,
leaping, leaping upward, and fell silent in a
sudden strangled gasp. The unmistakable
last call!
On his feet instantly, Metcalfe grabbed
the first weapon to hand, a short hide-whip,
and in a moment was at the other tent.
“Challis! Challis!”
No word from Challis. No words any
more from Challis.
He stumbled across the tent, bumping the
pole, and found the stretcher and the prone
figure—felt the warm blood on the boy’s
mouth and on his shirt; the bright lad; the
bright, strong lad.
The camp was silent now. The chattering
voices at the back had ceased ; but no man
came to help—the peon does not interfere in
an affair of the knife. A swishing, whisper-
ing sound came to Metcalfe’s ears, as of
someone running desperately in the loose,
clogging sand. He caught the man half-way
to Santa Maria, wrenched the lifted knife
out of his hand and thrashed him. whipped
him, scourged him into Santa Maria ; threw
him into gaol there, a tangled mass of dust
and blood and sand, half-naked and weeping
bitterly. Metcalfe registered a charge of
murder, and went back to the camp, disposed
his “evidence” as reverently as he could, and
watched beside it until the dawn broke.
“I will make it my personal duty, Seiior, to
see that the matter is examined very closely.
I regret it —regret it very much—indeed.
Such events—crimes of this nature —are of
very rare occurrence in Santa Maria. We
are peaceable, Sefior Ingles, peaceable and
law-abiding.”
Thus the magistrate in his room, very cold
and calm, with the strange, still indifference
of the Spaniard in authority. ..Metcalfe
could see that there was not much hope.

“However, I will see that all the evidence
is collected and that every effort is made to
find the offender. As for that poor cabaUero
the senor so maltreated last night, inno-
cently and in good faith, I know” —he bowed
—“it is incredible, impossible, that he could
be the guilty person. The man is my own
cousin.”
Metcalfe looked up suddenly, but the
magistrate still stared coldly at his fingers
drumming on the table.
“It appears that he was returning from a
visit to some friends”
“Some friends in the Argentine desert,
Senor?” interrupted Metcalfe bitterly.
“I believe so, Senor,” said the magistrate
with a cold disregard of Metcalfe’s obvious
meaning. He rose.
“A full report shall be furnished to the
Senor on his return, if unhappily the crimi-
nal is not discovered by then,, or to the
Ferro-Carril in Buenos Aires, or to any other
authority whom the Senor shall name.”
He walked to the door and held it open
f-.i Metcalfe. Very suave and bland, but
with sinister, implacable eyes, he said :
“I am the Mayor of this town, Senor, and
I know all my people well. I regret this sad
event very deeply: but I fear that our search
will be unsuccessful.”
Metcalfe gave in. He had talked until he
was sick and silly in a vain attempt to break
this wall of indifference, only to find that
this bleak stone of a man, this sole authority
in Santa Maria, was the sinned-against of
the night before last!
He went back hopelessly to bis waiting
camp, buried his dead with a wooden cross
at the head (which would disappear when
the north winds came), struck camp, and
went on into the interior, following that sad
vanguard Destiny.
Metcalfe told me the story at the
Strangers’ Club one boiling day in Decem-
ber.
“How did you manage without a chain-
man V” I asked.
“I found a drunk Irishman in Santa
Maria, a sodden vestige of a chap,” he said.
“And I hauled him into camp with me. How
he got stranded in that hole, God knows! He
was quick and clever enough when the booze
was sweated out of him.”
He threw his cigar-butt into his cup.
“Incidentally, he was blind sober for five
months ; and that hadn't befallen him in 20
years.”
“I suppose there was a hell of a row when
you got back to Buenos Aires,” I said.
“Oh, yes, all that,” he said grimly. “Old
Wilson went raving mad. of course —cursed
me, body, soul and breeches. Not that that
mattered much !”
His voice trailed down drearily to a note
of pain.
“I Avent to see the boy’s mother in Chelsea
on my next leave,” he said. “That mattered
a good deal.”
HILARY LOFTING.
Confessions of an Untoothed
Shark.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
JACK WATERS moved away from the
crowd under my office verandah and
came across the road towards me. He
had red cheeks, white whiskers and blue eyes,
and was kn®wn in Tauwhata as Union Jack.
Jack was bursting with news. He always
did know the latest.
Ellison, manager of the Southern Stock
and Produce Company, was in trouble and
things were serious. After the big dealing
sale at Maori Flat a large collection of notes,
cash and cheques had been placed in the com-
pany’s safe, of which Ellison alone had the
key. When the safe was opened in the morn-
ing it was empty. The company’s auditor
was up to investigate, and Ellison was sus-
pended.
Stories came thick and fast during the
morning: Ellison had bolted; Mrs. Ellison
had left him after a terrible scene; Mrs.
Ellison’s father had been seen motoring
away with the children; Ellison had shot
himself, and Doctor Badger was up there
now.
I felt annoyed with Ellison. He was a
cheery, likable chap. His wife was well off,
and I could not understand it. Anything of
this sort was not good for me, either. I did
not like suspicions being aroused about any-
body who was apparently in a sound posi-
tion. It was the end of the month, too,
and the mail was depressing. There was a
multitude of accounts and odds and ends,
and a peremptory demand from a finance
company for some (jocpmtmts. I looked at
the heap gloomily f when a knock at my door
startled me. It was Ellison.
His story was very concise. He said flatly
that he had nothing to do with the missing
money, and had no idea where it was. No
one else had a key to the safe, and his only
explanation was that someone had picked
the lock. What puzzled him was that the
safe was secure when he went to open it in
the morning.
We walked over to the Imperial Boarding-
house (known as the Mafeking Arms before
Tauwhata carried prohibition), and had a
drink. Under my cross-examination he
simply got irritated. I came to believe that
he really nothing. The mystery was
genuine.
In the morning I had to appear for Joe
Burrows, #who was being prosecuted for lay-
ing the odds. He had been the recognised
bookmaker in Tauwhata for years, and every-
one was in the habit of his billiard-
room to see the latest results from every-
where, which always showed on the pool-
slate. However, a pertinacious wowser cor-
respondent to the local paper had made
things too warm, and an information had to
be laid.
As both J's.P. on the bench were, in Joe’s
words, "on the sheet for a middling dollop,”
he hoped to get off with a £2O fine. lie had
also spent a tenner on what he called “bread
on the water,” and felt fairly sure, of a light
report from the sergeant. His optimism
proved to be well grounded. Aftef I had
addressed the Bench to the effect that this
betting transaction was obviously an isolated
one, and that it was well known thajt the
citizens of Tauwhata would not be parties in
general to such a surreptitious breaking of
the law, the Bench fined Joe £lO and Warned
him not to repeat the offence. He cattle
back to the office to settle with me. He was
full of surprises, but he outdid his previous-
best effort when he remarked casually across
the table while I was writing the receipt:
“I wuz talkin’ this mornin’ to the kewpie
that ’ooked the Produce Company’s dough.”
“Good God!” I said,. “Then it wasn’t
Ellison?”
“That poor mug!” snorted Joe. “He
couldn’t barber a deener. He’s only fit ter
mind goldfish.”
I thought I saw a chance to earn hand-
some costs and do a turn for a good fellow
at the same time. I explained Ellison’s fine
points to Joe at length, and entreated him
to see if anything could be done.
Joe listened solemnly to my eloquence,
commented thoughtfully on my being such
“a strong gee” for Ellison, and promised to
see his cobber.
While mixing my evening spot that night
the doorbell rang, and Joe came in with' a
redheaded youth whom he introduced as Bill
Wheeler.
Joe had wrestled to some purpose with his
friend, for Bill, after carefully looking me
over with a pair of small, sharp, grey eyes,
explained his modus operandi without more
ado. He had simply gone to Ellison’s house
and picked the pockets of the trousers that
were hanging on the bed. Then he had
slipped down to the office, gone through the
safe, and, returning to Ellison’s house, had
put the keys back in the trousers pockets.
Ellison slept soundly throughout, and Mrs.
Ellison, said Bill, was “a cow to snore.”
Bill had one yellow tooth at the top, and
it showed momentarily when his smile of
pride slipped on.
In face of a nerve as wonderful as this I
had some difficulty in putting my proposition
for restitution. However, Bill was a busi-
ness man only. “What’s it worth?” he
asked.
“That’s all very well,” I said, “but can I
he sure that you will get Ellison out of it?”
Bill nodded, and Joe Burrows asserted that
I could bank on it. “Stone blanky ginger
Bill is,” he said.
Bill named his price, and I said I would
pay Joe, wnom I asked to stay behind.
When we were alone, Joe, with that cheer-
ful recognition of my methods which
annoyed me sometimes, said :
“I suppose there’ll he room for you and
me in this somewhere between Bill and
Ellison ?”
I agreed that there might, and kept at my
point about Bill’s reliability.
Joe was sure that Bill was all right. “He’s
a bloke of class, Bill is.” lie said, "lie isn’t
one of these scrap-uv-paper blokes.”
I rang up Ellison, and he came down.
He decided to “give it a go,” and arranged to
pay the amount I mentioned. I allowed
ample room for myself and Joe; but once
Ellison’s mind was made up he was not
difficult over the matter of price. Besides,
his position was perilous. The auditor was
an officious sort, and had made the tour ot
the town to find out how much Ellison owed.
He was notoriously careless in the matter of
his trade accounts, owing to his wife being
so well off, and the auditor was in high glee
at the long list he had gathered. So Ellison
was very tractable.
Next morning I had another visit from
Joe Burrows. Bill, it appeared, wanted it
made quite clear that his - copyright method
of burglary was not to be disclosed or men-
tioned in any way. He had seen a city 1).
in Tauwhata, and would have to “make a
getaway” at once.
I gave the necessary assurance, and Joe
said: “Well. Bill’s done it. 1 told you he
wuz right, didn’t I? W’y, Bill’s as honest
as you or me.”
I looked hard at Joe for a moment, but
his face was quite expressionless.
Ellison was round quite soon, in high glee
and choking with laughter.'
The contents of the safe had gone again!
They had been found, carefully put in a box
along with the proceeds of the original rob-
bery, wrapped up in the auditor’s brown
hat! The sergeant had discovered them this
morning, resting on' the steps of the police
lock-up.
The auditor was in a fearful state. He
had fitted up a new lock on the safe at
great expense, and was the only person who
had a key.
It was not long before Ellison was re-
instated. and we heard that the official
explanation was that Someone had made
copies of the keys.
Joe Burrows, when we were dividing the
profits some days later, said:
“That, there brown ’at was a good touch
of Bill’s. Fer a ’armless, good-’earted little
cove like Bill is ’e’s a real Keystone when
he tries.”

Why NOT CYCLE
for Health?
After the day’s work a cycle
ride is an excellent tonic, the
easy gentle exercise in the pure
country air provides an ideal
method of keeping fit. Cycling
is admirably adapted for both
sexes and even the elderly will
benefit by it.

“Indispensable”
—a word largely used in these days—may be said to accurately express,
in so far as any single word can, what is the careful and considered
conclusion which has been arrived at by many people who use
Beeeham’s Pills. They consider that Beeeham’s Pills are “indispen-
sable” to their health, and, as a matter of course, to their happiness;
and they never allow themselves to be without a supply of this valuable
medicine in their homes. This latter is an excellent idea, and one
worthy of imitation—for there is no doubt whatever, that, as a preven-
tive medicine—one that may be taken with confidence when perhaps
only an “out of sorts ” feeling is experienced and before any definite
ailment has developed—Beecham’s Pills are a positive boon. They do
good all round;—they tone up the stomach, correct the action of the
liver, regulate the bowels and are a powerful agent in purifying the
blood. It is certainly a good plan never to be without
